INTRODUCTION
|1.  CONTEXT OF THE STUDY PROJECTl
1.1.   Background of the Trade Gateway Project (TGP)
The Government of The Gambia in collaboration with the World Bank is proposing to
implement the Trade Gateway Project.
The main objective of the TGP is to stimulate the foreign and local private sector to expand
investment, export-oriented production and employment in the Gambia. The project would
contribute to: (i) establishing a world class business climate; (ii) estabilishing local and foreign
new enterprises in The Gambia; and (iii) increase foreign direct investment. It will help to: (a)
improve the competitiveness of trade-related services (port, airport, customs, and service
providers); (b) attract new private activities through the implementation of a Free Zone
development strategy in the area of Banjul; and (c) foster existing private sector activities.
1.2.   Feasibility studies
In 1994 the consultant group TSG (The Services Group) undertook a study, financed by the
World Bank, of the feasibility of establishing a Free Economic Zone within the context of The
Gambia Trade Gateway Initiative. Although the study concluded that the initiative was feasible,
action was not taken due to economic crises in the sub-region which adversely affected the
Gambian economy.
Currently, a new study for the feasibility of the Gateway project is being carried out by
International Development Ireland (IDI). This study determines the location, the in-site and off-
site infrastructure necessary for the Gateway operations. The draft final report has been
presented on a one-day Stakeholders Seminar (lst June, 2000). Representatives of the EIA-
consultant attended the seminar.
The Consultant of the Environmental Assessment of the TGP will use the report prepared by IDI
as a reference document for EIA preparation.
Consequently, this recent feasibility study serves as a basic document for description and design of
the project .
INTRODUCTION                                                                     1



1.3.   Environmental Assessment: EIA and Environmental Management
Plan
Environment is crucial to allow a sustainable development of the Free Zones taking into
consideration the importance of tourism in the Gambia.
The National Environment Agency (NEA) wants to support the Trade Gateway Project by ensuring
compliance with environmental laws and regulations. The Environmental Impact Assessment
should cover the environmental impact and mitigation measures of on-site and off-site infrastructure
and utility improvements which may be necessary for the establishment of the Free Trade Zone
(FTZ).
1.4.   Definition of the study area
According to the terms of reference for the study project, environmental assessment will not be
restricted to the immediate surroundings of 5 nominated TGP sites.
Direct and indirect impacts of the project on natural habitats and protected areas have to be
identified, in particular for the following zones:
• Tanbi Wetland Complex (proposed Ramsar site), comprising
-        mangrove zones between Banjul-Serrekunda and Serrekunda-Airport, including
Mandina Wetland near Mandinari Point
-        the Stink-Comer/ the Saint-Mary mangrove
- Niumi National Park (proposed Ramsar site)
*  Baobolon Wetland Reserve (Ramsar site - designation date: 16.09.1996)
•  Tanji Bird Reserve and the Bijol Islands
*  Abuko Nature Reserve
*  Salaji forest park
The project sites and important natural habitats that could be affected by the development of free
zones are situated on a map of the study area (Figure 1./1).

2.  STUDY OBJECTIVES AND SCOPE - TERMS OF
REFERENCE FOR THE STUDY PROJECT
The aim of the study project , outlined by the terms of reference (TOR), is to carry out the
Environmental Impact Assessment of the Gambia Trade Gateway Project in accordance with the
World Bank Operational Policies (OP 4.01 - Environmental Assessment - and other specific
operational directives) and with the Gambia Environmental Impact Assessment Guidelines (Banjul,
July 1999), a copy of which has been provided to the Consultant by the NEA.
The EIA should address in detail the environmental issues associated with all phases of the
project. Special attention must be given to the following issues:
1! Due to the creation and operations of the free trade zone. the consultant should identifv:
*  All ecological effects that are likely to appear due to the creation of, and operations on the
free zone
*  The social effects on local people of the free zones
*  The effects on cultural heritage and historical sites
2) Due to the off-site constructions and operations
- All the potential ecological effects related tot the connection of the free zones with external
networks such as power, water, telephone, sewage
*  The effects on cultural heritage and historical sites of the off-site infrastructures
*  The social effects on local people
3! Due to the construction of basis wharves upriver
*  Identify and evaluate the erosion, and accretion effects, that are likely to occur where such
structures are built
*  Identify the effects off the wharves on the water quality and mangrove habitats
*  Assess the effects of the traffic and economic activities development around the wharves
areas
INTRODUCTION                                                                       5



4) Port operations development.
* The present and future impact of ship discharge (oily ballast, bilge water, sewage/from
waste collection to treatment and final disposal) on the natural environment
* The impact of maritime traffic development on the marine and Gambia River ecosystem
* The impact of traffic congestion and air polluting activities development around the port area
5) Direct and indirect impacts of the project on the natural habitats sites and protected areas
6) The potential negative impacts of changes in environment on tourism development

11. GENERAL DESCRIPTION OF THE PROJECT ELEMENTS|
The Trade Gateway Project comprises the following four project elements which, according to
the TOR, must be included in the environmental impact assessment study:
(i)      On-site infrastructure and operations of the Free Trade Zones;
(ii)      Off-site infrastructure;
(iii)     Upriver wharves;
(iv)      Port operations development.
Each of these components will be described hereafter in a general way. In order to allow state-of-
the-art environmental assessment of the proposed project it is necessary to add more details dealing
with the type of infrastructure, land use requirements, dimensions of factory cells and offices,
production capacity, etc...
As the detailed engineering of the Free Zone development plans is not yet available, it will not be
possible to describe all of these elements, however.
The feasibility study prepared by IDI Consultants (Feb. 2000) provides - for 2 out of 5 possible
FEZ sites - descriptions and site plans useful as reference for the project description. This
information, given in chapter 3 of this Part, principally deals with on-site and off-site infrastructure
of the two sites selected by ID] and the TGP Task Force. It adds no further elements to the general
description of the expected types of industrial activities that is given below (1. 1.). The development
plans presented in chap. 3. do not describe any information on project elements (iii) upriver wharves
and (iv) port operations development. Consequently, for these two elements the Consultant has to
rely on the very general description given in the present chapter (1.3. and 1.4.)
SECTIONA - PARTI: DESCRiPTION OF THE TRADE GATEWA YPROJECT                           9



1.1.   On-site infrastructure and operations of the Free Trade Zones
The proposed basic infrastructure of the FTZ development zones comprises:
- roads
- buildings: factory cells and offices
- land available for companies that want to construct their own facilities.
As a matter of fact a number of basic utilities have to be present in the zones in order to serve the
FTZ operations:
- power
- telecommunications
- water supply
- sewerage and sewage treatment
- solid waste collection zones
The tWpes of activities to be expected are:
* LIGHT ASSEMBLY
' ELECTRONICS
* TV/COMPUTER ASSEMBLY
* TRADING AND PACKAGING
* NEAR THE AIRPORT: EXPANDED AGRICULTURAL PRODUCE EXPORT
- At a recent Stakeholders Seminar on the TGP (June 2000) IDI Consultants elaborated on the
type of recommended activities for the free zones. Much attention has been given to "niche
manufacturing" (AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTS, FOOD AND FISH PROCESSING, PACKAGING OF
PHARMACEUTICAL PRODUCTS,...) as well as to the services component of the FTZ.
Although these recommendations are based on the results of market surveys and though they
were the object of careful consideration by IDI and the TGP Task Force, no further details of the
expected activities are available so far. Therefore, the EIA preparers were expected to work with
several assumptions regarding the activities and their expected emissions, residues and nuisance
for then environmnent.
SECTION A - PAR TI: DESCRIPTION OF THE TRADE GATEWAYPROJECT                       10



- Concerning the required on-site infrastructure and utilities, on the other hand, the general
information presented here can be complemented with the rather detailed development plans that
IDI Consultants worked out for two possible TGP sites (see below: chapters 3.1. and 3.2.).
1.2.   Off-site infrastructure which may be necessary for the establishment of
the FTZ
Two types of external networks and connection of the trade zones to them have to be considered:
- access roads to the industrial and free trade zones;
- links to trunk utilities:
* power
* water supply
• telecommunication
* sewage.
Site-specific requirements are available for two possible sites: details are given in chapters 3.1. and
3.2.
1.3.   Basic wharves upriver
According to our initial understanding of the TOR, Free Trade Zone development would comprise
the construction of a number of wharves (or revalorization of existing ones) along the banks of the
River Gambia. These wharves would support the TGP in 2 main ways:
- for the supply of sand, gravel and other construction materials (construction phase of the TGP);
and
- to guarantee a regular supply of basic resources (especially agricultural products) for Free Trade
Zone operation.
After studying the feasibility study - prepared by IDI - the EIA preparers did not collect any
description of this particular project element. This lack of information subsequently has been
discussed with the EIA Working Group. ECOLAS agreed to contact IDI in order to find out if
additional investigations in the framework of the feasibility study would provide more information
on location, size of the wharves, kind of traffic,...
SECTioNA - PARTI: DESCRiPTION OF THE TRADE GA TEWA Y PROJECT                      11



As a result, IDI provided background information dealing with river transport, current situation,
opportunities,... as well as with the relationship between the FTZ and river transport. Infortnation
that could possibly be of interest for environmental assessment of this project element can be
summarized as follows:
- The river traffic once played a prominent role in Gambia's economy, as the primary mode
for transport of both people and goods. However, the National Transport Plan 1998-2006
concluded that the fastest growing settlements are currently located on the roads, whereas
those on the river have stagnated or even declined.
- According to an analysis of the current situation the only freight transportation left on the
river consists of groundnut transport from 10 riverside jetties to a discharge facility at
Denton Bridge, near Banjul. Passenger services are limited to a number of ferries which
provide bridges for crossing the river. In addition, there are two tourist boats operating on
the river.
- The National Transport Policy concludes that there are expected to be opportunities for
enhanced river-based tourism and for a revalorization of the transport function (mainly for
construction materials and assorted foodstuffs from the Greater Banjul Area up country).
- Regarding the latter aspect, IDI developed some general principles for creating a
relationship between Free Trade Zone development and river transport (IDI, January
2000):
* with respect to the FTZ, the relevance of river transport is perhaps greatest concerning
the processing of agricultural goods , particularly the production of vegetable oils from
groundnuts and sesam;
* the river might play an important role on condition that groundnut processing could be
reinstated and that the scale of sesame seed processing increased, perhaps by establishing a
plant in the Free Trade Zone;
* furthermore IDI Consultants recommended that the potential for a direct connection
between the FTZ and uiver craft be considered as one of the factors affecting choice of the
FTZ site.
For the purpose of environmental assessment of upriver wharves these notes and recommendations
by IDI do not provide specific information, nor do they indicate suitable locations where wharves /
freight jetties have to be constructed to enhance river transport.
Consequently, this issue will perforce be considered in a very general way in this EIA-study, e.g. by
proposing the minimal requirements which should be observed when constructing wharves at
environmentally sensitive parts of the river.
SECTION A - PART I: DESCRIPTION OF THE TRADE GATEWAYPROJECT                         12



In this context an additional project element ( added to the initial TOR after the TGP Stakeholders'
Seminar) will be studied: the concept of a trade interface site along the road at Mandinari,
reinforcing the link between the Gambia River and the proposed FEZ at the airport.
See chapter 3.1.1. for further comments.
1.4.   Port operations development
It is unclear how port operations are linked to FTZ construction and operations.
In order to understand a possible connection, we consulted the existing EIA for the Third Banjul
Port Project (Dessau International, 1994) and had a meeting with both the Managing Director
and the Director of Technical Services of Gambia Ports Authority.
The following information has been obtained.
- The Gambia Ports Authority (GPA) currently reviews the Master Plan for the port of Banjul.
-  This review is in its early stages, so that no firm conclusions can be communicated at the
moment.
- Concerning the "Governnent Wharf' it is clear that a new function urges itself, seen the
important silting-up and insufficient draught for vessels. The Ports Authority mentioned a
conversion plan for the existing warehouses and a proposal to change their purpose to
storehouses as part of a trade centre with free zone (without any direct connection with the
proposed TGP Free Trade Zones, however).
- In the near future additional maintenance dredging of the river mouth will result in an
estimated volume of 3.5 million m3 of dredgings. The GPA suggests that this material be
reused as filling to prepare the FEZ sites for future development.
SECTIONA - PARTI: DESCRIPTrONvOF THE TRADE GATEWAYPROJECT                         13



2. GENERAL DESCRIPTION AND COMPARISON OF FIVE NOMINATED
SITES - ALTERNATIVES TO BE CONSIDERED
2.1.   Siting of the Trade Gateway Project
The Trade Gateway Initiative Task Force has nominated five locations for the possible
establishment of the Free Zones (see Introduction, Figure 1./i: map of the EIA study area)
They can be identified as follows:
1. Bund Road, Banjul:       along the highway between Banjul and
Serrekunda - edge of a tidal zone, including
some embranchments of creeks and
mangrove forest + mudflats
2. Denton Bridge, near Banjul:  stretch of coastal zone north of the highway
3. Airport area, near Yundum:  - main design within the boundaries of the
international airport of Banjul: low-density bushes,
scrub and grass;
- the area that extends east from the airport towards
Mandinari (indicated as Airport site B by IDI): mainly
abandoned farmland - no FEZ development but
intensification of agricultural produce is expected
4. Kuloro site, south of Koloro: extensive, flat area - in mixed use
(uncultivated scrub, some farmed zones,
few settlements)
5. Sukuta site, east of Salagi Forest Park.
along both sides of a brand new highway between
the airport and the Atlantic coast resorts; mixed use,
mainly orchards and scrub - important number of
settlements
SECTIoN A - PART I: DESCRIPTrON OF rHE TRADE GATEWAYPROJECT                      14



2.2.   Comparison of the five nominated sites
IDI Consultants substantially reviewed the earlier TSG-study (1994) in 1999. Each location has
been subjected to an audit to include the following items : climate; site and landscape features;
geo-technical conditions; environmental conditions; ownership; existing building analysis;
infrastructural audit [water supply - electricity and gas - drainage - solid waste disposal -
roads/traffic access ] .
Site 5 (Sukuta) was not included in the IDI-audit as it was felt to be lowest in order of suitability
due to its location and complexity in relation to ownership.
Table 2.2./1 (see next pages) was taken up from the IDI-study, slightly changed and completed
with our field observations at the Sukuta site.
The main conclusions of the comparison made by IDI served as a starting point for further
design work on only 2 out of 5 initially nominated TGP sites:
From the comparative analysis, it is considered that the two sites worthy of primary
consideration are the Bund Road and Airport sites. The remaining sites are considered
to be unsuitable in the short or medium term, due to environrnental sensitivity, location,
lack of infrastructure or issues of resettlement, or a combination of these factors.
(a) The Bund Road site
Given the lack of room for expansion at the port, and the limited area of Banjul island
itself, this site presents the only significant opportunity for the port to expand its
activities and for port-related FEZ activities to be located within a reasonable distance
of the port.
Taking into account all relevant conditions (including the environmental values) and
based on the type and scale of FEZ activities proposed, IDI Consultants propose to
locate a FEZ on a small part of the Bund Road site, at the northern end adjacent to
the Serrekunda-Banjul highway.
According to the IDI-study this location will allow port-related FEZ activities to be
located at reasonable distance from the port, while minimising disturbance to the more
environmentally sensitive parts of the site, and at the same time leaving open the
possibility of further city-related developments in the future.
(b) The Airport site
The airport site appears to present no major impediments to development, and would
appear to permit an early start on site, relative to the Bund Road.
SECTIONA - PART I: DESCRIPTION OF THE TRADE GA TEWAYPROJECT                          15



The proximity of the airport would be considerable attraction for this site in
facilitating the efficient movement of goods and people. The extensive and ambitious
development plans of the GCAA for the airport generally would further add to the
attraction of the site.
It is proposed to locate a "first-phase" FEZ on the airport entrance road, designed to allow
for considerable expansion over time.

2.3.   Alternatives to be evaluated in the EIA
For environmental impact assessment, the possible alternatives should always be evaluated. Indeed
these alternatives may have an environrmental impact that is less significant. The environmnental
impacts of the following types of alternatives will be considered in Part III of this study:
The zero-alternative
The predicted environmental effects of the project have to be compared to the situation without
establishrnent of the free trade zones.
Alternative locations for FEZ development
The alternative locations considered in this EIA are the 5 sites nominated by the TGP Task Force
and analysed in a recent feasibility study: see 2.1. and 2.2. for a general description and comparison.
Although the TGP Task Force clearly selected the Airport and the Bund Road sites as the most
favourable with regard to Free Zone development in the short or medium term, the other 3
nominated sites still have to be considered in the environrmental assessment study.
However, the degree of detail is different:
- The two priority sites have been studied in depth, on the basis of the available, rather detailed
development plans, design lay-out and project element descriptions given in the next chapter 3..
- For the three other reservation sites all environmental items have been described albeit in a
more general way. Consequently, the sampling and analvsis progranmme has been concentrated
on the water and soil quality of the development zones at the Airport and Bund Road sites only.
Construction and process alternatives
Despite the fact that development plans are available, very limited information exists concerning
the expected industrial activities in the project areas. Therefore, assumptions with regard to these
activities were made by the pollution experts in view of a rather general prediction of emissions
and residues.
Where relevant, construction alternatives will be considered mainly with regard to the on-site
infrastructure proposed by the feasibility study and described in the following chapter.
SECTIONA - PART I: DESCRIPTION OF THE TRADE GATEWAY PROJECT                          19



13- DEVELOPMENT PLAN FOR TWO PRIORITY SITES [AIRPORT SITE|
|& BUND ROAD SITE] 
From the conclusions of the IDI-study, it appears that free zone development should start at the
Airport site. Reclamation of Bund Road site would be a later phase, if the former proves
successful.
In accordance with the feasibility study, the project outline is given in this order.
The description is nearly entirely quoted from the feasibility study (IDI, February 2000 - draft
final report). We have omitted descriptive details when the environmental relevance seemed low.
3.1.   Development plan for the AIRPORT SITE
3.1.1. Site Location
a.  Free Economic Zone (FEZ or FTZ)
- The proposed site is located within the "EPZ Zone" designated by the GCAA (The Gambian
Civil Aviation Authority) on its lands at Banjul International Airport (see Figure 3./1).
- In the initial phase, a zone of approximately 8.8 hectares will be located on the existing
airport approach road and will be entered via a new roundabout on the approach road. The
zone is positioned so as to permit its future expansion to the west: the completed phase will
cover an area of approximately 162 hectares.
- The zone is planned on a regular grid of distributor roads, access roads and service roads
enclosing rectangular sites, capable of accomodating a variety of building types, from small
standard advance factories and warehouses to large detached custom-designed
manufacturing plants and office buildings.
- Service zones for power plants, waste treatment plants, gas and other utilities are located at
the edges of the zone.
b.   " Airport Site B "
We consider FEZ development at the airport - in accordance with IDI - in an area which is
currently bordered by the airport fence, thus ± 160 hectares or 200 hectares as the very maximum
expansion at the final stage.
However, according to the TGP feasibility study it may be expected that FEZ-development at the
airport will induce a "revival" of agriculture in the land adjacent to the airport, where
SECTION A - PARTI: DEScRIpTiON OF THE TRADE GA TEWA Y PROJECT                 20



agricultural use is of low intensity at present (abandoned farmland,grassland and scrub tending to
re-colonise former fields,...). This is the large area indicated as Airport site B by IDI (figure
10.1. in their study). This area extends east towards Mandinari. IDI agrees that the Mandinari
area is ecologically unsuited to development as this would lie close to a very sensitive part of
Tanbi Wetland Complex with both inner salt flats and outer mangrove areas.
For environmental assessment of the project, it is important to state the following in a clear
manner:
i. FEZ development at the airport is limited to an area of max. 200 ha, within the existing fence
demarcating the GCAA's lands.
ii. An eastern extension, up to -Mandinari area will not be developed as Free Economic Zone but
in this area an expansion / intensification of currently marginal agricultural areas could occur
due to "expanded agricultural produce export" at the airport-FEZ.
It is particularly important to note that in this area the project could have both site-specific
environmental and social impacts, as well as indirect impacts that extend beyond the proposed
FEZ site.
c.    Trade Interface (Mandinari area)
An additional project element will be studied : the concept of a trade interface site along the road
at Mandinari, reinforcing the link between the Gambia River and the proposed FEZ at the
airport. This interface would provide a basic infrastructure for transshipment of goods (mainly
agricultural products and fish) that could be trucked to the airport FEZ for processing.
This study component has been added to the initial TOR, after the TGP Stakeholders' Seminar
(June, 2000). No information concerning the concept is available from the TGP feasibility study
(IDI, 2000).
After consulting the EIA Working Group it was agreed that the scope of environmental
assessment will be restricted to the following:
i.   environmental feasibility of the interface: general assessment of the ecological sensitivity
of the riverside near Mandinari (the mangrove habitas of Tanbi Wetland and Mandina
Wetland in particular);
ii.   general assessment of the effects of the required terrestrial link between river and airport:
currently there is no direct road - the EIA study does not include any site selection for road
construction.
SECTION A - PARTI: DESCRIPTION OF THE TRADE GA TEWAYPROJECT                            2]



3.1.2. Phasing of FEZ development
- In the initial phase (Figure 3./2), the zone will contain approximately 14,110 m2 of floor
area. A temporary service zone will be located immediately adjacent to the buildings so as
to minimise development costs; this will be relocated as the zone expands.
Distributor roads will be limited to those necessary to access the buildings of the initial
phase. The entrance road will be landscaped; the office buildings and Food Centre will be
screened from the industrial units by landscaping.
-  In its completed phase (:igure 3.13) the zone will contain approximately 250,000 m2 of
floor space, or more depending on building type. In the interim, it is proposed that a new
airport approach road be built to the south of the zone (the GCAA are considering such a
road at present) and that the GCAA's proposed Real Estate Development Zone (REDZ) be
relocated alongside this road. The purpose of these measures is to leave open possibility of
expanding this zone eastward onto a larger FEZ as proposed by the TSG Report of 1994.
3.1.3. Building types
In the initial phase, it is proposed to construct the following (see typical units, Figures 3.16
and 3./7):
- four standard type A industrial units (or eight type B units or a combination of these
depending on demand), total 7,760 m2;
-  an office building/teleport facility, total 4,350 m2;
-  a Food Centre of 1,600 m2; this unit will provide a number of incubator units ranging in
size from 200 m2 to 600 m2 to start-up companies in food technology and innovation;
- an entrance building to accomodate security, administration, customs inspection and
maintenance staff. It will also be capable of accomodating a marketing suite for the FEZ.
3.1.4. Infrastructure
The initial phase infrastructure will include the following elements:
-  asphalt roads with 1.5m wide concrete footpaths around the immediate development area;
- further gravel roads to delineate the next phase of development and to access any
necessary areas of the site;
- concrete service yards;
- street lighting;
SEcTION A - PART I. DESCRPiTiONOF THE TRADE GATEWAYPROJECT                     22



- landscaping;
- a system of open channel storm drains to discharge to an agreed outfall location;
- a network of water mains fed from an elevated storage tank;
- a power generating facility to consist of 2 no. 650 kW diesel plant with associated oil
storage;
- foul drainage system services by aerobic treatment units with outflow to irrigate the
landscape zones;
- fire hydrant mains
- telecom network
- a solid waste disposallcollection zone
- fencing and gates to Phase l.
The feasibility study provides no detailed descriptions of buildings and infrastructure in the
completed phase. By comparing figures 3.12 and 3./3, one can understand that the expansion will
include a fill-up of the road grid with the same type of buildings described for the initial phase.
Figure 3.13 shows that additional buildings will complete the airport site: a main entrance
building and customs area, and a possible future Amenities Building (with restaurants,
conference, marketing and training rooms).
3.2.   Development plan for the BUND RoAD SITE
3.2.1. Site Location
- The proposed site of approximately 34 hectares is located at the northern end of the larger
area bounded by the Bund Road, in order to minimise disturbance to the more
environmentally sensitive southern part of the area (Figure 3.74).
- As with the airport zone, this site is planned on a regular grid of distributor, access and
service roads and rectangular sites, capable of accomodating a variety of building types,
from small standard advance factories and warehouses to large detached custom-designed
manufacturing plants and office buildings. A service zone for power plants, waste treatment
plants, gas and other utilities is located at the outer perimeter of the zone .
SECTiON A - PARTI1: DESCRIPTiON OF THE TRADE GATEWAYPROJECT                         23



3.2.2.  Phasing
-  In the initial phase, the zone will contain approximately 7,160 m2 of floor area. Distributor
roads will be limited to those necessary to access the buildings of the initial phase and to
access the service zone. The entrance road and perimeter to the adjacent public roads will be
landscaped.
-  In its completed phase, the zone will contain approximately 70,000 m2 of floor space, or
more -depending on building type.
-  It is important to note that even in its completed phase the Bund Road free zone will be
restricted to ± 34 hectares located along the Banjul - Serrekunda highway. The IDI-study
does not consider any further development in the environmentally most sensitive area, south
of the FZ limits shown in Figure 3./5.
3.2.3.  Building types
In the initial phase, it is proposed to construct the following:
- four standard type A industrial units (or eight type B units or a combination of these
depending on demand), total 7,760 m2;
- an entrance building to accomodate security, administration, customs inspection and
maintenance staff. It will also be capable of accomodating a marketing suite for the FEZ.
3.2.4.  Infrastructure
The swampy ground conditions will require considerable preparation before being suitable for
development, resulting in a "lead-in" period of 1-2 years and extra development costs.
The initial phase infrastructure will include the following elements:
- lateritic filling of the Phase 1 site including surcharging to accelerate settlements;
-  further lateritic filling to prepare the balance of the site for future development;
-  asphalt roads with 1.5m wide concrete footpaths around the immediate development area;
- further gravel roads to delineate the next phase of development and to access any
necessary areas of the site;
- block pavior service yards;
- street lighting;
- landscaping;
SECTIONA - PART1: DESCRIPTION OF THE TRADE GATEWAYPROJECT                        24



-  a system of open channel storm drains to discharge to an agreed outfall location;
-  a network of water mains fed from an elevated storage tank;
-  a power generating facility to consist of 1 no. 650 kW  diesel plant with associated oil
storage;
-  drainage system services by aerobic treatment units with outflow to irrigate the landscape
zones;
-  fire hydrant mains;
-  telecom network;
-  a solid waste disposal/collection zone;
-  fencing and gates to the whole site.
Any significant development of the Bund Road site that will generate additional traffic to and from
the port will possibly require the Bund Road to be widened and strengthened.
It is considered that for the Phase 1 development of this site the existing carriageway should suffice,
but that for future phases the carriageway should be widened by approximately 5 metres to provide
for traffic segregation at junction locations. The road will also require the provision of a more
fornal drainage system, safety barriers and pedestrian zones.

14.   DEVELOPMENT OF THE REMAINING 3 SITES!
4.1.   Assumptions with respect to development of the reservation sites
No detailed design nor site maps are provided by the IDI-study, for the sites serving as
reservation zones in the current TGP-context.
The following assumptions_ are considered by the Consultant for environmental assessment of
the TGP at those locations:
- for the Denton Bridge site the same site plan, area (34 hectares) and basic design of
the Bund Rd. site will be taken as a basis for impact assessment;
- for the Kuloro and the Sukuta sites on the other hand , where the available space is
more important, the design of the development plan for the Airport (final phase) will be
taken as the reference (160-200 hectares).
4.2.   Infrastructural requirements and restrictions
In addition, important results from the infrastructure audit done by IDI will be taken into
consideration:
Denton Bridge
Requirements with regard to water supply, electricity and gas, solid waste disposal are similar
to those described for the Bund Road site.
The drainage of surface water from the land will be complex. It is noted that the site will have to
be raised to permit the development of buildings at an elevation free from the dangers of
flooding. However, the existing natural drainage system will have to be respected.
There is no existing piped sewerage network available at the site. A treatment plant similar to
that described for the Bund Road site will be required.
The Denton Bridge site is strategically positioned on the southern boundary of the Serrekunda
highway, which has excellent capacity for additional traffic.This site is approximately 7km from
the Port of Banjul, versus 2krn for the Bund Road site.
SECT0oNA - PARTI: DESCRIPTION OF THE TRADE GATEWA YPROJECT                        33



Kuloro
There is no water supply available in the area. The nearest water main is in Brikama, some 8.5
km from the site.
The feasibility study suggests a stand-alone borehole supply on the site. However, agreement for
this would have to be obtained. It is considered that such a supply would be feasible and the best
option in the short term for this site.
The situation with respect to electricity is similar to that described for the Bund Road site.
With regard to drainage and solid waste this site is expected to have similar characteristics to
the Airport site.
This site is positioned some 20 km from the Airport. Furthermore, it will be necessary to
construct a new road some 2 km in length from the main highway at Kuloro to the site.
The last site (Sukuta) has not been considered in the IDI-audit. For most of the items the site
is comparable to the Kuloro site.
5.   LEGISLATIVE, REGULATORY AND POLICY CONSIDERATIONS
The following overview is based, to a large extent, on FAO/UNEP, 1996 - a recent report on
coastal area management in The Gambia . It has been adapted and completed with additional
information obtained during the study project.
The comments place emphasis on environmental matters, whereas the TGP feasibility study
(IDI, 2000) emphasizes the commercial and industrial context.
Before commenting the overview, the following relevant Operations Directives and Policies of
"The World Bank Operational Manual" can be mentioned as guidance of the EIA consultants:
- OD 4.01: Environmental Assessment
- OP 4.04:  Natural Habitats
- OD 4.20: Indigenous People
- OD 4.30: Involuntary Resettlement
- OP 4.36:  Forestry
SECTIONA - PARTI. DEscRiPTiONOF rHE TRADE GATEWAYPROJECT                          34



Ramsar Convention on Wetlands (1971)
The Convention on Wetlands came into force for Gambia on 16.01.1997. Gambia presently has
1 site designated as a Wetland of International Importance, with a surface area of 20,000
hectares: Baobolon Wetland Reserve (North Bank Division).
The country is currently listing two additional sites for designation:
Niumi National Park ( North Bank Division - 4,940 hectares)
Tanbi Wetland Complex (near Banjul, Westem Division - 6,000 hectares)
The National Environmental Management Act (NEMA ,1994) is the legal framework for the
control and management of the environment and for matters connected therewith.
The leading authority, the National Environment Agency (NEA), was created in 1993 to
coordinate the implementation of the Gambia Environmental Action Plan (GEAP) which was
adopted in 1992 . Following the approval and implementation of this action plan, the "State of
the Environrment Report" was thje first comprehensive report on the environment in The
Gambia. NEA coordinated the efforts of contributers from all the concerned government
agencies, and published the report in 1997.
Coastal and Marine Environment Working Group .The Executive Director of the Agency is
empowered by NEMA under section 16, to establish a technical working group where a matter
requires specialised technical consideration. The working group would advise the Agency and
carry out its duties according to the terms of reference laid down at its formation.
The current coastal erosion rate of 1-4 metres annually is a major environmental problem, which
has therefore warranted the creation of a multi-sectoral working group due to the complexity of
the problem.
Gambia Ports Authority
The jurisdiction of the authority is regulated by the Ports Act which provides for the
establishment of the authority and for the transfer to the said authority of certain port and
harbour undertakings of the government. For the coastal zone in question, the jurisdiction of the
authority includes the Port of Banjul including the shores and beaches adjoining thereto.
The Gambia Ports Authority is also the implementing agency of several international
conventions to which The Gambia is a signatory, the Protocol of 1978 relating to the
International Convention for the Prevention of Pollution from Ships, signed earlier in London in
1973. The Convention's main objectives are to preserve the marine environment by achieving
the complete elimination of international pollution by oil and other harmful substances and the
minimization of accidental discharge of such substances.
SECTIONA - PARTI: DESCRIPTION OF THE TRADE GATEWAY PROJECT                    35



The Gambia has also subsribed to the Convention for Cooperation in the Protection of the
Marine and Coastal Environment of the West and Central African region which aims to
treat the major problem of regional coasta erosion.
Chapter 10 of the "State of the Environment Report - The Gambia" gives a comprehensive
overview of this and all other international conventions The Gambia subscribed to.
Geological Unit
The unit is the government institution mandated to regulate the right to search for mine and work
other minerals and for other purposes relating thereto in accordance with the Minerals Act 1994.
All mining of sand, laterite, gravel and salt falls under the jurisdiction of the Minerals Act which
is administered by the Geological Unit, Ministry of Trade Industry and Employment.
The main issue relating to this Unit is monitoring of the beaches along the Atlantic Coast to
prevent illegal mining.
Department of Fisheries
The Department administers the Fisheries Act which is to provide for the management of
fisheries and development of the fishing industry in The Gambia. The provision of infrastructure
facilities for the fishing industry is a requirement under Section 8 (d) of the Act. Improper siting
of such facilities can, however, enhance the coastal erosion in certain areas Under Section 3.
Establishment Design and Facilities of the Fisheries Regulations Act, 1995 Fisheries
establishments should be located in areas which are "free from objectionable odours, smoke,
bushes, swamps, dust or other contaminants and are not subject to flooding". The artisanal
fisheries play a major role in beach pollution as most of the fish processing is done directly on
the beach.
Department of Parks and Wildlife Management (DPWM)
The conservation and national management of wildlife in The Gambia is administered under the
Wildlife Conservation Act (1977) by the Department of Parks and Wildlife Management.
Under Section 58 of this Act, the Minister may make regulations for the efficient control and
management of any natural park, national reserve or local sanctuary, preparing regulations for all
or any of the following:
- prohibition or control on cutting, clearing, burning or otherwise damaging or removing
any tree, bush, plant or other vegetation or any part thereof from any national park,
national reserve, or local sanctuary;
-  prohibition of human settlements and certain human activities disruptive of wildlife and
the natural environment in any national park, national reserve or local sanctuary.
SECTIONA - PARTI: DESCRIPTION OF THE TRADE GATEWAY PROJECT                    36



Moreover, environmental education with regard to wildlife was initiated by the DPWM in 1997
with the construction of an Environmental Education Centre at the Abuko Nature Reserve. Training
is conducted troughout the country.
Area Councils
The Local Government Act is an act to amend, consolidate and make provisions for local
government in The Gambia other than in Banjul. In addition to any functions imposed upon the
council by this act or any other law for the time being in force, a council may perform several
functions in respect of the area for which it is established under section 27 (1):
- prevention of soil erosion;
- prohibition, restriction and control of the cutting and selling of trees and forest products
and the management of such forest parks and areas as may be delegated and the planting
and tending of trees in general;
- the regulation of the disposal of refuse, the prevention and abatement of nuisances and
generally for the oversight of health and sanitation;
- the establishment and management of recreation grounds open spaces and parks.
The Banjul City Council which has the functions similar to the other area councils, is
empowered under section 32 to prevent or regulate a number of environmental issues, especially
the establishment and management of parks, and other places of public resort or recreation for
the use of the public and by section 52(2)(d) of the Local Government (City of Banjul) Act to
make bylaws for a number of purposes including the prevention of damage to the land.
Land Tenure in The Gambia
Two types of land tenure do exist in The Gambia. These two types or systems are the Formal,
that is leasehold and freehold and the other one is the Non-Formal; that is the Customary tenure.
Within the Formal System, the freehold tenure is the same as total ownership. Both private and
public freeholds do exist in The Gambia since independence none has been made, due to the fact
that it was widely believed to be a mistake, since it can create numerous problems to the
Government such as preventing government from being able to influence the future ownership or
occupation of land. The other system (Leasehold) on the other hand does not grant total
ownership. It is the result of a contract granting exclosure right to possession of land for a fixed
period shorter than the grantors interest. The grantor can be either private or public agency.
With regard to Non-Fornal (Customary tenure) in the coastal area, the ownership and
occupation of land was still based on the customary practices of the local people, until 1991,
when the Government of The Gambia signed into law four new land acts: the State lands act,
1990, the Physical Planning and Development Control Act, 1990, Surveys act 1990, Land
SECTION A - PART I: DESCRIPTION OF THE TRADE GATEWAY PROJECT                  37



and Compensation Act, 1990. Within this area, before the new land act was signed, most land
was generally owned by the village. The village headman or Alkalo was reponsible for allocating
such land to compound heads, who makes decisions about the cultivation or reallocate to the
member of their extended families.
Land Administration
The principal agency, responsible for land administration in The Gambia is the Ministry for
Local Government and lands within which, two departments are directly concerned with land
matters.
The Department of Physical Planning and Housing is responsible, among other duties, for
long term planning, design of lay-outs or subdivisions and the issuing of development permits
for all new developments as well as for changes in land use. It is also responsible for the
establishment of housing policy, research into technical aspects of housing as the administration
of building codes and rent control.
The Department of lands and surveys on the other hand has four basic functions. It is the sole
government agency responsible for national mapping. In addition to its responsibilities, it is also
responsible for all Cadastral Survey activities, which include the demarcation of new lay-outs
and individual parcels for which lease hold titles are to be granted by the State. Some of its
responsibilities also includes the creation, recording and control of rights and interests in land as
well as the valuation of properties for taxation purposes.
Apart from the Ministry for Local Government and Lands, the Justice Ministry also plays a vital
role in land administration. It runs the deed registry which records all transactions involving land
matters, also assisting with any legal matters.
Local Institutions such as the Area Councils (Municipalities) Divisional Commisioners, Seyfolu
(District Chiefs) and Alkalolu (Village heads) are traditional in nature and hence fall under the
juridiction of Customary law. Their role in land administration is therefore restricted to land
under Customary tenure.
Due to the centralized nature of government, the role of the Municipalities with respect to land
administration is limited to a small amount of development activity involving public services as
well as the collection of rates. They are also responsible, in the case of land held under
Customary tenure into a State leasehold.
SECTION A - PARTI: DESCRiPTION OF THE TRADE GA TEWA YPROJECT                        38






PARTII
DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE SITUA TION
1.   GENERAL CHARACTERISTICS
1.1.   Topography and geology
1.1.1   General data for the study area
The Gambia is located on a part of the West African tertiary sandstone plateau and is rather flat with
no hills higher than 75 metres above sea level and slopes facing the river. The Gambia is essentially
the valley of The Gambia River. The landscape, with its seasonally flooded plains and large
swamps, is soft-curved, except for the scarps formed by exposed ironpan. However, in spite of
these gentle slopes, surface runoff of rainwater is extremely common. In the east, narrow valleys
are separated by broad, low hills. In the West, lower and smaller hills alternate with depressions
that often form sand-filled plains.
The Gambia occupies the south-central part of a regional sedimentary basin that extends along
the coast of West Africa from Mauritania to Guinea (Conakry). The basis is known as The
Gambia Guinea Basin or the Mauritania - Senegal - The Gambia - Guinea Bissau - Guinea
Conakry Basin (MSGBC). Most of the land surface of The Gambia is formed from sandstone,
laid down during the late Tertiary era and known as the Continental Terminal. This is highly
weathered detrital sediment made up of layers of clayey sandstones of different colours with
intercalated discontinuous beds of quartz gravel, sand and clay. The claystones are commonly
kaolinitic. They are found within the stratigraphic sequence sometimes forming thick beds.
Continental Terminal series rocks or sediments are predominant and form the hills throughout
the country.
1.1.2   Characteristics per site
Bund Road
The Bund Road site is lying at sea level and swampy and it is part of the alluvial floodplain of
the River Gambia. The area consists of low mangrove swamp, mudflats and creeks linking
together towards the Polder Pump Station on Bund Road. The Bund Road site was created by
cutting of a part of Banjul bay by causeway and installing a pumping station to reduce water
level. While pumping was going on, a significant drying out of the area took place. After
breakdown of the pumps, the situation has reverted and the area has become more humid. The
site is flat and subject to inundation during the rainy season.
SECTIONA - PARTII: DESCRIPTiON OF THE BASELINE ENviRONMENT                    39



Denton Bridge
Denton Bridge area is situated between Serrekunda-Banjul Highway and the Atlantic Ocean. It
borders on the Oyster Creek coastal zone. This flat site, situated just above sea level, consists
mainly of mangrove swamps and creeks. Only a small part of the area, near Oyster Creek, is dry
and suited as building land.
Airport
The airport site lies about 30 metres above sea level in an area of very flat and well-drained
ground. The land is covered in low-density shrubs and bushes with grass and there are no
restrictions with regard to construction of buildings.
Sukuta
The area at Sukuta site is also flat, well drained, and suitable building land.
Kuloro
The area at Kuloro site is flat and well drained. The land is part of a large agricultural area and
is covered with uncultivated scrub, farmed zones, farm settlements and villages. There is some
forestation. The land is suitable for building.
1.2.   Physiographical characteristics of the coast
The coastline of the Gambia is about 80 km long, from the mouth of the Allahein River in the
south to Buniadu point in the north. From Allahein River to Cape Saint Mary facing the Atlantic
ocean, the coastline is about 56 km long, from Cape Saint Mary to Banjul point 13 km and Barra
Point to Buniadu point 11 km long. Seawards the coast is separated from the continental slope or
deep sea by the continental shelf of 80 km wide which is marked by the 200 bathymetric line.
The embayed sandy parts have been prograding since about 5500 years BP. The amount of
progradation varies at different locations but is generally of the order of 200-400m in the Batu
kunku area, and 500-800m in the Sanyang and Kartong areas. This predominantly sand deposits
both in the surf zone (present beach) and the raised or ancient beach (usually covered with
vegetation) have been grouped together as the Holocene coastal Beach Complex. At some points,
the beaches are very small (f.i. Fajara cliffs).
The raised beaches consist of yellowish medium to fine sands with distinctive black colouring as
a result of heavy mineral presence. These sands have been originally reworked by waves and
exceptionally high waters into series of broad, low ridges, parallel to the present shoreline and
later modified by aeolian processes.
SEcTIoNA - PARrII: DESCRIPTIONOF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                      40



The beaches are heavily eroded from Senegambia Beach Hotel towards Banjul. At some places,
sand bags form a small cliff of 2meters to prevent further erosion. Further south from the
Senegambia Beach Hotel, the beaches perceive less impact from erosion.
The shoreline of the River Gambia in the neighbourhood of Banjul is dominated by the Tanbi
wetland features: flat with water inlets into the mangrove area.
2.   CLIMATE AND AIR QUALITY
2.1.   Climate and meteorological characteristics
According to the Koppen classification the climate of the Gambia is a savanna climate (AW).
This Sudano sahelian type of climate is characterized by a long dry season from October to early
June and a short rainy season from mid-June to early October.
The meteorological characteristics in the study area have been studied by the Department of
Water Resources since about 50 years. Two stations are relevant for the area to study, namely the
station located at Banjul and the station located at Yundum. A number of relevant meteorological
parameters have been summarized in the Tables 2.1./i and 2.1./2 and in the Figures 2.1.11 and
2.2.12.
From the data reviewed it appears that from the meteorological point of view the alternative sites
show no differences of any significance. As a result the meteorological evaluation applies to all
sites.
As far as temperature is concerned annual variations are rather modest. The daily mean
temperature is somewhat higher in the wet season (up to 27-28°C) as compared to the dry season
(down to 24-25°C). Mean annual temperature is approximately 27°C.
Contrary to temperature, precipitation varies substantially as a function of the season. Highest
rainfall in August amounts up to an average of over 300 mm. Annual rainfall approximates 800
mm over the past 10 years. Apparently a small warming and decrease in rainfall could be
observed in time series of climate data covering 40 years. The unequal distribution of
precipitation over the year results in a water shortage for soil on a yearly basis and in the dry
season in particular. The PET (potential evapotransportation) in the study area averages 1.777
mm (Verheye and Camara, 1999). In the wet season on the other hand, precipitation is higher
than potential evapotranspiration. Moreover the heavy rain storms lead to run-off.
Humidity in the study area is high in the wet season with a mean ranging from 80 to 90%. In the
winter months mean humidity is at an ideal average of 50-60%.
SECTIONA - PAR nI: DESCRPrTION OF THE BASELINE ENviRONMENT                    41



During the wet season the wind usually blows from the north west side. In auturnn the most
common wind direction tums to west while in the dry season the predominant wind direction is
the north to north-east. There is generally a slight to moderate breeze resulting in annual mean
wind speeds of 2-3 mls.

2.2.    Air quality
Sources of air pollution
In general industry, power generation and traffic are the major contributors to air pollution.
Power generation and heavy industry are mainly responsible for SO2 emission. Traffic, industry
and heating provoke NO2 emissions. Suspended particles originate mainly from industry, traffic
and residential and commercial sources (TDRI, 1990), while also (semi) natural dust formation
may contribute. Other air pollutants such as volitale organic compounds (VOC) mainly orginate
from different kinds of traffic, oil refining operations and metal industries. In the whole of The
Gambia the sources of air pollution are very limited. As a result emission rates will be low, i.e.
the estimated per capita emission of 0.22 Ton/person of CO2 for The Gambia is at the lower end
as compared to other African Countries (World Resources, 1994)1 Power generation is rather
poorly developed. The main power plant is situated in the study area (Kotu). However the total
installed capacity of 23.9 MW + 11 MW is rather limited and will not lead to emission rates
likely to significantly affect air quality in a wide area.
In 1994 annual emissions of respectively 64.2 tons of CO, 412 tons of SO2 and 155 tons of NOx
were reported (Brown and Root Environmental, 1994). Actually there is no large scale industry
in the study area. The only industries that could be identified concern:
-  a brewery west of the Denton Bridge site;
-  a fish processing plant in the Denton Bridge site and west if the Bund Road site;
-  a groundnut plant nearby the Denton Bridge site;
-  very small wood, furniture, handicrafts, welding, construction and repair activities essentially
in the surroundings of Banjul.
Neither at the airport nor at the locations at Kuloro and Sukuta siginificant industrial activities
take place.
The existing industrial activities do not provoke emissions that would lead to an increase of the
irnmission level beyond the irmmediate vicinity of the sources.
Road transport may be considered to be the most important source of air pollution, in particular
in the study area including Banjul, Serekunda and the airport connection to the touristic places
' Another source however provides a per capita emission of 2.573 ton of COC/person/year, ranking The Gambia
among the highest per cpaita emitters in Afirca (Bubu P. Jallow, 2000)
SECTION A - PAR TII: DEScRIPTION Of THE BASELINE ENVzRONMENT                           45



such as Kololi, Kotu and Bakau. Nevertheless road transport is presently still fairly low (+ 3,000-
15,000 cars per day along the principal roads). As a consequence its contribution to air pollution
is assumed to be well within the acceptable limits. Transport emissions due to traffic related to
airport activities were calculated to be in the range of 24.0 Tons of CO, 3.6 Tons of HC and 12.0
tons of NOx (Belgian Airport Consultants, 1994). Aircraft activity at Banjul International Airport
is low compared with airports in developed countries. The total aircraft movements amounted up
to 7,123 in 1998.
Apart from the emission sources summarized above, some emissions may originate from
residential and commercial sources i.e. smoke from cooking fuels (fuel wood, butane, ...)
transport dust, paint and welding, bush fires, dumping sites, small generators ... None of these
sources have been quantified. Finally it should be added that in particular in the dry season road
and other dusts may be created naturally due to a lot of activities.
Actual air quality and carrying capacity
Based upon the evaluation of the actual sources of pollution it can generally be concluded that it
is expected that the degree of air pollution in the study area will be low, due to the small quantity
of industries, the limited power generation capacity and the relatively low traffic burden.
However, very locally at traffic congestion points i.e . Serrekunda road junctions, in port
unloading area and nearby dumping sites, air may be polluted in particular with dust particles.
Recently the Department of Water Resources in collaboration with the National Environment
Agency has started a program of ambient air quality measurements at 9 different sites. The
following parameters are included in the program: NO2, PM10 (particles with a diameter < 10
jim) and lead. The data obtained so far are summarized in Table 2.2./i. The provisional air
quality standards for The Gambia are also included in het Table as well as the most recent WHO
standards. From this table it appears that the NO, concentration in the study area on the average
ranges from 3.7 up to 28.8 jig/m3. The NO, concentration seems to be linked to the traffic
density on the one hand and to the population density (including cooking activities, small works,
etc.) on the other hand. The typical "background" concentration for not densily inhabitated areas
appears to be 5 jIg/M3. This would maximally be the concentration in the locations at Kuloro and
Sukuta. At the airport site, the concentration may be somewhat elevated; however, it is not
expected to be higher than 10 gg/rM3. From  the data obtained, it also appears that the
concentration at both the Bund Road and the Denton Bridge sites averages 5 [Lg/M3.

When considering the provisional standards and the WHO guideline, the NO, levels do not pose
any problem, the more since the standards are related to average concentrations over the year.
The carrying capacity at all locations is thus considerable and is at least 30g/Mm3. For the two
sampling sites considered the PM10 level appears to be quite high and varies considerably.
Neither the provisional standard from the Gambia nor the EU guideline are complied with.
However, particulate matter may well be of natural origin and the sampling place (i.e. nearby a
very crowded highway intersection at Westfield) may influence the result significantly. The
provisional standards may thus have to be reviewed. Nevertheless the PM10 load seems to be
substantial and requires further assessment.
The lead concentrations appears to be well below the relevant guidelines and standards, and
consequently air contamination from lead is not relevant.
Although no imrnission data exist on the SO2 concentrations, it can be expected that mean
concentrations at the different selected locations average from 5 to 10 pg/m3. When compared to
the standards, it is clear that the pollution degree for SO2 is very low, generally not reaching 10%
of the carrying capacity.

13.   WATER AND AQUATIC RESOURCESI
3.1.   Ground Water
3.1.1   Groundwater layers and reservoirs (Ceesay et al. - Humphreys et al.; 1987,
Humphreys et al., 1994)
Groundwater in the Gambia occurs in two aquifer systems, a shallow sand aquifer and a deep
sandstone aquifer. The Quaternary and Tertiary age shallow sand aquifer occurs beneath the
lateritic surface deposits. The deep sandstone aquifer of Upper Cretaceaus age comprises mainly
Maastrichtian sandstones. In the Kombo district, the shallow sand aquifer lies between 5 m and
90 metres below ground level.. Near Banjul, the deep sandstone aquifer occurs at a depth of
about 300 metres below ground level. Between the shallow and deep aquifers, a layer of about
200 m to 300 m, consisting of clays and shales with marl, occurs. Although there is a
considerable, positive head difference from the deep to the shallow aquifers, the sequence of
clays and shales acts as an aquiclude, hydraulically separating both aquifers.
Shallow sand aquifer
Lateritic deposits up to 25 metres thick and comprising reddish-brown lateritic clays, sandy clays
together with hard well-joined ironstone, overly the shallow aquifer throughout the Kombo area.
These deposits are in hydraulic continuity with the shallow sand aquifer. A sandy soil, I m to 2
m in thickness, covers the lateritic deposits. The lateric horizons forrm the unsaturated zone
through which infiltration occurs to recharge the underlying shallow aquifer system.
The shallow aquifer system occurs throughout the whole of the Kombo district and is up to 80 m
thick. It comprises predominantly sands and silty sands interbedded with silts and clays. This
sequence is underlain by impervious blue-grey clay, which forrns the base of the shallow aquifer
system. This shallow aquifer system is divided into upper and lower aquifers, separated by an
aquitard consisting of interbedded clays, silts, silty sands and sands. The upper aquifer is
phreatic, whilst the lower aquifer is semi-confined. Locally, it may be unconfined. The upper
aquifer is discontinuous but can be up to 1l m thick in places, while the lower aquifer is
continuous and varies between 5 m and 30 m in thickness, generally thickening towards the
south.
The aquitard, which separates the upper and lower aquifer units, comprises a layered sequence of
clays, silts, sandy silts and sands. This aquitard varies between zero and 50 m in thickness, although
generally it is between 10 m and 40 m. Thickness increases towards the south of the Kombo
district. Groundwater in this aquitard is stored under phreatic conditions and is in hydraulic
continuity with the upper aquifer. Since the piezometiy of the aquitard is a few centimetres higher
than that of the lower aquifer, vertical downward leakage takes place.
SECTiONA - PART/I: DESCRIPTIONOF THE ASELNEENVIRONMENT                        48



Recharge to the aquifer is mainly by infiltration from rainfall. A secondary source is lateral flow
from Senegal. Recharge to the shallow sand aquifer, as the result of infiltration of rainfall, occurs
throughout the Kombo area. The infiltration results in a recharge to the upper, shallow sand aquifer
by vertical leakage through the laterite. Further leakage takes place from the upper aquifer to the
lower, shallow sand aquifer through the intervening clay aquitard, depending on relative
piezometric levels. Additional recharge occurs form infiltration from latrines, soakaways and septic
tanks which are present throughout the region, with significant concentrations in urban areas in the
north of the Kombo district.
Rainfall records show a highly seasonal pattern. The seasonal nature of the rainfall means that
recharge to the upper shallow aquifer is only likely to take place during the months of July,
August and September. June is supposed to be a month during which the soil moisture deficit is
at its highest level. Due to a drop in rainfall, there has been a decrease in recharge over the last
15 to 20 years. This constrains the development potential of the aquifer. The significant decline
in rainfall in recent years could be due to global warming, although longer term records show a
cyclic pattern indicating that the recent decline could be within normnal variations.
The general direction of the groundwater flow is from the south to the north. Within the general
pattern of groundwater flow, there exist local features in the form of 'sinks and mounds'. Their
origin is not entirely understood but they are thought to be the result of a combination of diverse
factors such as recharge, permeability and transmissivity, topography and variations in lithology.
The Kombo groundwater mound for example is centred in the upper reaches of the Kotu and
Tangi catchments, coinciding with the highest topographic elevations of this area. Flow radiates
outwards to the Atlantic Ocean in the North and west, to the River Gambia in the east and to
Brikama and Gunjur in the south.
In the shallow sand aquifer, there is a lateral component of flow in the upper aquifer and aquitard
units, as demonstrated by the perennial discharge of groundwater into the Lamin stream.
However, as a consequence of the lowering of the phreatic surface in recent years, since 1980 the
stream has no longer been perennial.
Deep sandstone aquifer
The deep sandstone aquifer underlies the whole of The Gambia and most of Senegal. It is
confined by a 200-300 m thick sequence of mainly clays and marls but water levels may rise to
within a few metres from the soil surface. At Banjul, the aquifer is artesian and free flowing.
The total volume of groundwater stored in the aquifer is probably 650,000 m3 of which only
80,000 m3 is of good quality.
Within the Gambia, the aquifer does not receive recharge from rainfall. The main source of
recharge is from lateral through flow from Senegal. Another source is downward leakage
through the overlying aquiclude.
Groundwater moves from east to west at very low velocities.
SECTIONA - PARTII: DESCRIPTION OF THEBASELINE ENVIRONMENT                       49



3.1.2   Groundwater use (Humphreys et al., 1994)
At present, only the shallow sand aquifer is being exploited in The Gambia. This is not the case
for the deep sandstone aquifer. However, over the last 10-15 years, there have been significant
abstractions in Senegal.
There are two methods of exploitation of the shallow sand aquifer: hand-dug wells and
boreholes, the forner being suitable for small villages and communities and the latter for large
municipal supplies. Most hand-dug wells take their water forn the upper aquifer, whilst the
main public supply wells draw from the lower aquifer. In the North and Central Kombo
districts, borehole supply comes from 6 boreholes in the Fajara well field and 13 boreholes at
Salagi and Jambur well fields. Water is pumped to a treatment plant at Sukuta. Following
processes of aeration, pH adjustment and precautionary disinfection, drinking waters are
delivered by transmission mains to Banjul and the major urban population centres in the North
and Central Kombo Districts. In principle, the National Water and Electricity Company
(NAWEC) provides all drinking water in the Greater Banjul Area. Individual hotels however
exploit their own boreholes (e.g. Palma Rima, Senegambia, Kairaba and Mariatu Beach).
Drilling and exploitation of private wells falls under the jurisdiction of the Water Resources
Department. The location of the different wellfields is indicated on figure 3.1. /1.
The NAWEC boreholes at Fajara are located in a zone potentially vulnerable to salt water intrusion.
The same is true for the wells exploited by individual hotels in Fajara and Kololi at the Atlantic
Coast. The water quality from the NAWEC boreholes is monitored on a regular basis by NAWEC.
The authorities are not involved in monitoring of the private wells.
In 1994 a study was carried out which included an aquifer modelling (Aquifer Studies in the
Kombo District, Howard Humphreys Consulting Engineers). The model was constructed with
three layers, representing the hydrostratigraphic units of the upper and lower shallow sand
aquifers, separated by the semi-confining sand and clay unit. Simulations with maximal
groundwater abstraction in all wellfields were carried out using the long-term average recharge
rate, and a worst case using the worst 10 year period recharge rate. Results suggested that under
both scenarios a potential for saline intrusion in the long term would occur in layer 3. That
potential is rather slight in the base case. The results further indicate that abstraction from wells
in close proximity to sea boundaries increases the extent of the saline intrusion. Under the
"normal" recharge scenario, some of the hand-dug wells in the coastal areas and around
Serekunda could dry up or be faced to reduced water levels. Under the second, more pessimistic,
recharge scenario, most of the hand-dug wells north of Brikama would be likely to go dry. This
would have a serious impact on the water supply for many communities dependent on hand-dug
wells. Deepening of the wells may resolve these problems. In parts of the Kombo North district,
exploitation for urban water supply and for the hotels may already be in excess of available
resources, consequently causing intrusion of salt water. At present, water supply is a major
problem of the coastal area. The level of the groundwater is constantly sinking and there have
been water shortages since several years. This is mainly due to enormous use of water for
tourism facilities.
SECTION A - PARTII. DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                    50



Bund Road and Denton Bridge
There are no boreholes located on Banjul Island. Water supply to the Banjul area is provided by
Salagi and Jambur wellfields. Water is treated in the treatment works at Sukuta and transported
through transmission mains to large storage tanks in the Banjul area. There are storage tanks
located on Serrekunda Highway, near Bund Road and Denton Bridge.
Airport
There are two boreholes located in the neighbourhood of the airport site, but they are not in use
at present. However, plans exist to restart exploitation at these wells and create a connection to a
treatment works. There is a water supply main and storage facility on Serrekunda highway
nearby the airport. Water supply to is provided by Salagi and Jambur Wellfields.
Sukuta
The north-west and south-west corners of the Sukata site are marked by two NAWEC boreholes
(SS2 en SS3) of the Salagi wellfield. Sukuta site is an integral part of the Salagi wellfield area in
which precaution to prevent aquifer pollution is very important. Untreated water can be tapped
directly at the SS2 and SS3 boreholes. Treated water will probably have to be supplied from the
Sukuta treatrnent works.
Kuloro
There are no facilities to ensure water supply to the Kuloro site. Water supply could be provided
by establishing a stand-alone borehole supply.

3.1.3   Groundwater quality
Shallow Sand Aquifer
Except for low pH-values (5 to 6.5), chemical quality of the groundwater of the shallow sand
aquifer is excellent. Concentrations of total dissolved solids are generally low. Water of poor
quality, particularly with a high nitrate content, has been found in many hand-dug wells. This is
mostly the result of point pollution and does not reflect the chemical quality of the aquifer as a
whole. High nitrate contents or bacteriological pollution haven 't been encountered in the semi-
confined aquifer indicating that the aquitard provides effective protection against the movement of
pollutants.
In June 2000, one treated and one untreated groundwater sample was taken by the Ecolas team. The
untreated sample was tapped from borehole SS2 of the Salagi wellfield at Sukuta site. At the airport
site, a sample was taken of the water that is supplied from the storage facility on Serrekunda
