Groundwater quality at borehole SS2 meets the water quality standards except for pH, chloride and
nitrite. pH is slightly below the limit of 5.5 and chloride- and nitrite-concentrations are slightly
higher than standard values.
In the sample that was taken at the airport site, conductivity is exceptionally high, especially since it
concerns water supplied by a treatnent works. Values are comparable to saline water. As this high
conductivity can not be explained by high salt or anion concentrations, it is probably due to an
analysis error in the laboratory.
Deep Sandstone Aquifer
In most of the Gambia, chemical quality of the groundwater of the Deep Sandstone Aquifer is poor,
being characterised by high total dissolved solids and fluorides.
3.1.4   Sensitivity of groundwater for pollution
The phreatic sub-aquifer runs a high risk of pollution in urban areas where latrines are abundant.
The semi-confined sub-aquifer, which is used for all municipal supplies, has a minimal pollution
risk providing boreholes are constructed with proper sanitary seals.
Locally, sensitivity of groundwater for pollution depends on:
- depth of the water table : sensitivity increases as groundwater occurs more shallow;
- soil texture: coarse textured soils (sand, gravel) are more permeable for pollution than fine
textured soils (clay, silt);
- presence of impermeable layers (clay).
At the coastal sites, groundwater occurs close to ground level, but soil texture is mainly clayey.
At the inland sites, soil is coarse textured whereas groundwater occurs at a depth of one metre
below ground level or more.
At the coastal sites, the issue of saltwater intrusion should be indicated. In the greater Banjul
area, groundwater supply-demand equilibrium has apparently been reached. Further extraction
of groundwater could cause saltwater intrusion into the wells in the coastal zone. Increasing
saltwater intrusion will render adjacent fertile lands unsuitable for cultivation.
In general, contamination of groundwater resources is mainly caused by:
-  storage and transport of liquid products
- transport through pipes (leakage or ruptures)
-  accidents during loading or unloading (f.e. overfilling)
-  solid waste and septic tank effluents
SECTION A - PARTII: DEscRIPTIoN OF THE BSsELINE ENViRONMENT                        54



- discharge of (domestic) waste water into the ground;
- proximity of latrines to supply wells
- use of pesticides;
-  seawater intrusion.
3.2    Surface water
3.2.1   Watercourses and drainage areas (Ceesay et al. - Humphreys et al.; 1987)
The River Gambia rises in the Fouta Djallon mountains of Guinea, at an elevation of about 1,150
metres above sea level. After flowing through Senegal, it enters the Gambia at sea level,
meandering along the length of the country and discharging into the Atlantic Ocean at Banjul.
About 70 per cent of its catchment area of 77,000 square kilometres is situated at an altitude of less
than 1 00 m above sea level, about 30 per cent of its catchment originates from an altitude below 40
m above sea level. The River Gambia is tidal throughout the length of the Gambia and experiences
large scale saline intrusion, particularly during the dry season. During that period, the saline/fresh
water interface is normally between Kaur and Kantaur, some 200 to 250 km upstream from Banjul.
Apart from the major side rivers of the River Gambia, all the other tributaries are short-lived and
flow only during the wet season. Numerous river streams and bolons form a trellis-like drainage
pattem along both banks of the river. Except for the River Gambia and its tributaries, surface
waters in the study area are sparse and seasonal. Stream courses are generally poorly defined and
carry water mainly during the wet season. The Lamin stream at Abuko in North Kombo had
perennial flows prior to 1980. Its mean flow rate has ceased in recent years due to a lowered water
table as the result of groundwater pumping in the nearby Welingara wellfield. Another important
drainage basin in the Kombo North area is the Kotu stream, which temporarily contains water
during the rainy season.
Only the coastal sites are situated nearby surface water of permanent nature. Bund Road site is
crossed with mangrove creeks, linking together to flow out at a pumping station on Bund Road.
Denton Bridge mainly consists of mangrove swamps and creeks. The only part suitable as building
land borders at the coastal zone of Oyster Creek.
3.2.2   Flooding events
In some parts of the upper section of the River Gambia, flash floods are experienced in a few small
streams that have a built up or relatively imperrneable catchment. The resulting floods that flow
into the River Gambia generally occur after periods of wet weather or intense localised
precipitation. Consequently, in the lower and central sections of the river, where raised river banks
are non-existent, a significant portion of low lying areas is subjected to inundation and drying
associated with the semi diurnal tidal cycle. Close to the river, an elaborate network of creeks
separates the islets and islands in the lower en central river parts of the estuary.
SECTiONA - PAR nI: DESCRIPION OF THEBASELINE ENV7RONMENT                        55



Bund Road site is seasonally flooded through rainfall and runoff, which effects the salinity
regime. Field observations at different points in time showed no evidence of flooding at high
tide during the dry season. During a visit to the site, shortly after a downpour, trapped rainwater
could be noticed on the mudflats.
3.2.3   Surface water quality
Water quality information for surface waters in the Gambia is sparse. The River Gambia has a
saline front that extends approximately 130 km up river from Banjul during the wet season, but
extends to 250 km at the peak of the dry period.
At the Airport, Sukuta and Kuloro no permanent surface water is present in the near surroundings of
the sites. Therefore, there weren't any surface water samples taken at those sites. The area around
Bund Road site and Denton Bridge is crossed with mangrove creeks. Due to the presence of the
sea, water within the mangroves around Bund Road and Denton Bridge sites may have brackish as
well as saline characteristics. The surface water within these mangroves can be characterised as
saline water. Information on mangrove water quality at the Bund Road site is obtained from data
collected by Ecolas during June 2000. A water sample was taken in a mangrove bolon near the
pumping station on Bund Road. Analysis results were compared with Gambian (provisional)
"Water Quality Standards for Saline Waters". As standards for saline waters are not available for
all analysed chemicals, standards for surface fresh waters were used as a reference. Analysis
certificates are included in appendix 4.

without treatment will further deteriorate the quality of the water in the mangrove system.

3.2.4   Present surface water use
The Gambia River is the major source of surface water in the country. The use of surface water
for domestic purposes is limited by the position of the saline front. Surface water supplies are
therefore restricted to the eastern half of the country where the flow from the river remains fresh.
The only significant use in serni urban areas and villages is for domestic use (laundry) and for
animals.
Because of the intrusion of salt water in the lower part of the River Gambia, there will be no use
of surface water in any of the proposed sites, which are all located in the western part of the
country.
Seasonal rains are important for rainfed agriculture. Infiltration of the water into the soil
provides moisture required for crop growth. Runoff from rainfall is limited in the lateritic sandy
soils because they promote infiltration.
3.2.5   Major pollution sources
The most important source of surface water pollution is discharging of wastewater in local rivers or
mangroves. Contaminants, released with the wastewater, can influence the present quality of the
river- or mangrove-system.
The use of fertilisers and herbicides in agriculture and product spills can cause pollution of surface
water through run-off.
3.3.   Coastal dynamics and characteristics
33.1.  Sea water
Sea water temperature varies from 19'C in February to over 26°C during the rainy season.
Salinity of the sea water is around 35 ppt. In 1996 measurements show visibility of the water in
the mout of the river of 1 to 2 m in the upper layers. At the bottom, at a depth of about 8 m,
visibility was only a few centimeters. Turbidity of the river water is high all year round.
Specific data were collected during the EIA on the Third Banjul Port Project. A series of water
samples was collected by Portconsult in May 1993 to determine the concentration of suspended
sediments (SS). The average concentration of SS is roughly 100 mg/I and generally increases
from 20 to 50 mg/l at the surface to 150 to 200 mg/l near the bottom. The mean suspended
particle size ranges between 1 8 and 25 tm. The SS consists mainly of silt.
SECTIONA - PARTII: DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                        60



3.3.1.1. Monitoring results
Up to now, no systematic monitoring of the water in the river nor in front of the coast has taken
place, although plans for such monitoring have been drafted and approved. Following schedule
of monitoring in saline waters is foreseen.
location                   tourist beaches    offshore           fishing villages
# of sampling points      :5                  5                  5
sampling frequency (per year) 12              4                  2
The number of samples collected from coastal beaches declined in 1999. While 28 samples were
collected in 1998, 18 samples from 12 points were collected in 1999. The number of samples
collected ranged from 1 (Amie's Hotel) to 5 (Atlantic Hotel). The location of the sewage outfall
pipe at the Atlantic Hotel requires this more frequent monitoring. According to the agreed
monitoring system, 5 points are to be sampled 12 times a year at the tourist beaches. Parameters
measured include pH, nitrate, nitrite, dissolved oxygen (DO), BOD, total coliform and faecal
coliform. Not all the parameters were analysed in all the samples collected.
Individual data on the sample results are not available, only a general discussion on the results.
Parameters that were out of the Gambian provisional standards included DO (35%), BOD (50%),
total coliform (6%) and feacal coliform (9%). While BOD was not analysed in 1998, the quality
of the water with regard to dissolved oxygen and faecal coliform deteriorated slightly in 1999,
while in terms of coliform, it improved greatly. It is however not clear whether the sampling
points for the two years are the same.
The NEA has drafted regulations with respect to 'Environmental Quality Standards' in 1999.
These standards are provisional and apply a.o. to saline waters (see Table 3.3./1).
Table 3.3./1: Environmental quality standards for saline waters

In comparison to the water quality standards for surface fresh waters and groundwater, only a
few parameters are used for water quality monitoring in saline waters.
3.3.1.2. presentpollution
The beaches of The Gambia are, as yet, relatively unspoiled. At present, there are three main
sources of pollution. Fish processing within the artisanal sector is mainly carried out on beaches
where the dumping of fish rests and shells rapidly becomes a breeding area for flies and vermin.
It als creates an objectionable smell. Litter from the tourist industry often ends up on beaches,
especially plastic containers. Sewage outfall on the coast could become a major problem. There
also have been incidents of oil spills from ships and oil waste from intentional tank cleaning.
In the mangroves itself, coastal pollution arises from sewage outfall, polluted surface water
drain-off, and pollution from fish processing factory outlets. Until recently, most of Banjul's
liquid waste, including effluents from industries like the Banjul Breweries and tanneries, was
discharged in the river's estuary at the Bund Road side of town. Now most of the sewage and
wastewater is pumped through a 950m outfall, adjacent to the Atlantic Hotel, into the coastal
strip of the estuary.
The water quality in the coastal area has not been monitored in a systematic way. But as far as
information is available, no signs of serious pollution have been found in the coastal waters up to
now.
3.3.2. Sea sediments
The Coastal Beach Complex is an important geological formation. This complex includes both
the modem beaches (the surf zone) and older raised beaches that lie behind the surf zone along
the Atlantic Coast. Both beaches are predominantly fine to medium grained sands with
significant amount of heavy minerals (ilmenite, rutile, zircon) (National Environment Agency,
1997). The unconsolidated marine and aeolian sands are very susceptible to be thrown in
suspension and transported away.
Specific data were collected during the EIA on the Third Banjul Port Project. In general terms,
the sediments to be dredged around the Port of Banjul can be considered not to be contaminated.
SECTIONA - PARTII: DESCRIPTIONOF THE BASELINEENVIRONMENT                       62



3.3.3.  Tidal regime
Tides in the Gambia are of the semi-diurnal type with two daily high and two daily low water in
each lunar day. Tidal ranges are low. At Banjul for example tides of highest magnitude or spring
tides are 1.6m whilst neap or lowest tide is 0.7m. Consequently tidal currents along the coast are
generally weak (less than 0. 1 rm/s) with the exception of the Gambia River Estuary where tidal
currents exceed 1 rn/s.
3.3.4.  Littoral regime
During the ICAM Project in the Gambia (FAO/UNEP), data were collected on bathymetric
profiles and tidal streams of the sea coast.
The prevailing wave direction from the Atlantic Ocean is the NW swell which is the most
important factor in explaining the littoral regime. Because of the general north-south orientation
of the coast this swell induces a major south-going sediment movement, particularly south of
Bald Cape to Allahein River. Between Bald Cape and Cape Saint Mary (oriented SW-NE)
however, the littoral drift goes in the opposite direction, northwards. Between Cape Point and
Banjul the coast is oriented W-E and east-going littoral drift is being experienced. In the north
between Barra Point and Buniadu Point the drift is southerly along the North-South oriented
coast. Littoral drift along the south coast of Bald Cape has been estimated to 100,000 to 250,000
m3/year based on the Sangamar spit development at the mouth of the River Saloum. East of the
Cape Saint Mary littoral drift is between 30,000 to 100,000 m3/year. The coastal watersheds
including the River Gambia and its estuary contribute insignificantly to the sediment budget of
the coast which creates the serious erosion problem.
In the River Gambia estuary an inward sediment transport is experienced. This estuary serves as
a sediment sink rather than a source of sediment for the coast. Material is transported westwards
towards the river mouth from upstream while coastal current moves material eastwards into the
estuary.
3.3.5. Around Banjul
Specific data were collected during the EIA on the Third Banjul Port Project. The tidal stream,
according to various studies perfonned by Portconsult and by the Danish Hydraulic Institute is
oriented parallel to the alignment of the bathymetry around the New Banjul Jetty. Currents range
from near zero at slack tide to a maximum of 1 m/s. The main driving force of the currents is the
tide, and the effect of freshwater run-off from the Gambia River is very small.
Within the harbour of Banjul, siltation is taking place on different places with rates up to
50cm/year. The volume of sediment deposited in the harbour area during the past 25 years is
roughly I million m3. Overall, the rate of sediment deposition in the harbour area ranges between
SECTION A - PARTII: DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                    63



25,000 and 40,000 m3/year. Only a fraction of this is actually located in areas where navigation
activity requires to maintain a rrminimum depth.
3.3.6.  Coastal erosion and sedimentation
The Gambia has been identified by UNEP, as one of the ten most vulnerable countries to sea
level rise due to global warming. The infrastructure development in the coastal zone has
accelerated in recent years and it is believed that the unplanned tourism development has been
endangered by the coastal erosion.
If no protective measures are taken, erosion will continue at those places already under attack.
The problems are most severe along the North Coast of Kombo North. Most of the anti-erosion
measures in place have become ineffective. If no measures are taken, the Old Muslim Cemetery
will disappear completely; the Christian cemetery is likely to be affected, the radio station SYD
will probably be destroyed, and land and property on the North side of Banjul will be lost.
Erosion will continue at certain stretches along the west coast, notably at the beaches between
Bald Cape and Kololi Point and at the cliffs of Fajara and Bakau. In this section tourist resorts
and residential premises will eventually be threatened. Locally, agricultural land or patches of
mangrove along creeks running parallel to the coast may disappear if the protecting beach ridge
or spit is removed by erosion. This problem will most probably become worse due to sea level
rise.
The process of sedimentation poses some local problems, notably at the end of the Ferry Pier at
Barra. Some sedimentation takes place at the northern end of Banjul Port. This sedimentation so
far does not pose a serious obstacle to the operation of the port. Limited dredging is done in the
port of Banjul. The dredge spoil is disposed of in the open waters of the Gambia river estuary.
These activities do not affect in any significant way the process of coastal erosion or
sedimentation.
3.3.7.  Impacts from coastal erosion
Coastal erosion is probably the most devastating enviromnental hazard along the Gambian coast
destroying tourist facilities, cultural and historic sites and buildings. The annual loss of land
along the coast is estimated to 2.5 to 3.0 ha. Coastal erosion also results in the washing away of
the buffer zones which once acted as barriers to low lying coastal plain behind the beaches. The
new Banjul to Serekunda highway which has opened to traffic in 1990 is less than 50m from the
water mark and prone to severe flooding. Coastal erosion enhances sea water intrusion, creates
adverse impacts on the socio-economy of The Gambia in the areas of tourism, fisheries, housing,
industry, agriculture and mining. It also has been estimated that about one third of the six fish
landing facilities and processing facilities are been threatened by coastal erosion.
SECTON A - PART HI: DEScRiPTION OF THE BASELINE ENvIRONMENT                    64



In a study conducted in 1996 with the Aerial Videotape-assisted Vulnerability Analysis (AVVA)
technique, a detailed analysis of vulnerability to sea level rise, and adaptation strategies have
been identified. Future erosion rates have been projected by applying the Bruun Rule, and future
total land loss due to inundation in response to global warming and accelerated sea level rise has
been determined. The sea level rise scenarios considered are 0.2 m, 0.5 m, and 1.0 m per century.
Inundation is estimated to be about 92.32 x 10(6) m(2) for a 1.0 m sea level rise, 45.89 x 10(6)
m(2) for a 0.5 m sea level rise and 4.96 x 10(6) m(2) for a 0.2 m sea level rise. The greater part
of this area lost will be wetlands and mangrove systems important for fish spawning areas and
habitats for wildlife. Shoreline retreat is estimated to vary between about 6.8 m in cliffy areas to
about 880 m for more flat and sandy areas based on the Bruun Rule. Population and physical
structures at risk have been determined. Attempts have been made to report this loss in monetary
terms, but firm figures are not yet available. Only one unit of the coastal zone has been
evaluated. In this unit, it is expected that the capital city will be completely lost through both
erosion and inundation within 50 to 60 yr with a total of 42000 persons displaced. Lands and
physical structures to be lost are estimated at US$ 217 million. Response strategies and
adaptation options identified include: innovative sand management, building and rehabilitation
of groins, construction of revetments to protect important areas, construction of sea-
walls/bulkheads, public outreach and awareness, building regulations and urban growth
planning, wetland preservation and mitigation, and development of a coastal zone management
plan (Jallow et al., 1996).
4.  SOIL AND SEDIMENTS
4.1    Pedological characteristics
The surface geology of The Ganmbia is entirely Upper Tertiary and Quatemary. The Upper Tertiary
consists of mainly poorly consolidated sandstones, argillaceous (clayey) sandstone and claystone.
According to the World Map of Soils (FAO-UJNESCO, 1976), the soils located in the western part
of The Gambia and at the south of the River Gambia are indicated as ferric luvisoils (symbol LVf).
Fernic luvisoils correspond mainly to the tropical zone with a long dry season and are characterised
by the following lithology:
- Continental Terminal : sandstone, clayey schist, calcareous clay, sand and clay
- Pliocene and Quatemary: alluvial, marine and lagoon deposits
Most Luvisoils are well drained, but shallow groundwater may induce gleyic soil properties in
Luvisoils in depression areas. Luvisoils have favourable physical properties with granular or crumb
surface soils that are porous and well aerated. When containing a high silt content, Luvisoils may be
sensitive to slacking and erosion. Certainly on steep slopes, erosion control measures are required.
SECTIONA - PARTII. DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                   65



Coastal sites
Bund Road site is situated in the floodplain of the River Gambia in which alluvial material was
deposited. The site overburden soils are predominantly alluvium consisting of sands, silts and clays
to a considerable depth. The soil is strongly influenced by the occurence of mangrove. It consists of
normally permanently saturated and very fine textured clays and silty clays. When drying out it
may develop a high degree of acidity. The water table is very high or even on the surface during the
rainy season. Several soil samples were taken at this site. Sampling locations are indicated on
figure 4.1 /1. As expected, texture appeared to be clayey.
Denton Bridge site includes both mangroves with clayey soils and part of a sandbar that is build up
of soils of coarser material, mostly sand. The only part of the site that is suitable for building is
located on the sandbar. No soil samples were taken at this location.
Because of the high clay and moisture content, stability and carrying capacity at Bund Road site and
in the swampy part of Denton Bridge site is limited. The soils will be subject to significant
settlement when heavy loads are applied. As the settlement will probably be greater than acceptable
for construction tolerances, soils are not suitable for supporting building constructions without the
application of engineering processes.
Inland sites
The airport site, Sukuta and Kuloro are situated on the inland side and up river from the present
mangrove swamps. Soil is mainly of sandy texture though it may include bands of coarser material.
Soil samples were taken at the airport site (figure 4.1./2) and at Sukuta site near borehole SS2
(figure 4.1./3). Texture is mainly sandy, mixed with some silt

4.2     Soil quality
In general, the tertiary sandstone plateau is rather poor for agriculture. Soil is dry and nutritive
value is low. Surface soil, mainly overlying laterite, consists of sand being extremely hard
inimediately below the dust covering, except in the rainy season. The river terraces are more fertile,
especially in the eastern part of the country where the salty tide does not reach. The soils at the
lower part of the river are less fertile.
Luvisoils however, are generally fertile with high mineral reserves and are suitable for a wide range
of agricultural uses. Structure deterioration may occur in Luvisoils with high silt content if they are
tilled in wet condition and/or with heavy machinery.
Information on soil pollution in The Gambia is not available. In order to get an idea about soil
quality, samples were taken at the Bund Road site, at the Airport site and at Sukuta site. Sample
locations are indicated on figure 4.1.11 for Bund Road, figure 4.1./2 for the airport site and figure
4.1./3 for Sukuta site. Results of analyses are shown in table 4.2./1. As there are no Gambian
standards available, measured values are compared to Belgian standards. Analysis certificates are
included in appendix 4.

In general, soil quality is rather good. Only at Bund Road site, near one of the drains that conducts
part of the waste water of the city of Banjul into the mangrove system (figure 3.2/1, picture 2), lead-
and zinc-concentrations slightly exceed Belgian Standards. The contamination that was found is
almost certain man-made and possibly due to the presence of a piece of metal. However,
concentrations are not that high that an immediate risk should be expected.
Contamination of the soil at the other proposed sites is also expected to be low, as there are no
major industrial activities. Possible sources of soil contamination in these areas are mainly
community development (dumping of domestic waste) and agriculture (use of pesticides).
Quality of sediments is not discussed, as it is not relevant in view of the proposed sites and
activities.
4.3.   Sources of soil pollution
Major sources of soil pollution are similar to sources of groundwater pollution:
- storage and transport of liquid products;
- solid waste and septic tank effluents;
- transport through pipes (leakage or ruptures);
- accidents during loading or unloading (f.e. overfilling);
- use of pesticides;
- discharge of waste water into the ground.
5.   NOISE
In The Gambia, actually no official noise measurement stations do exist. So far, very little
attention has been paid to the problem of noise burden. Nevertheless during our field recognition
trips it was observed that in particular in crowded areas such as Serrekunda and Banjul port noise
may be significant and disturbing.
The actual background situation in the study area in general and at the 5 different locations in
particular may with a high degree of confidence be deduced from an extensive measurement
campaign set up by the Belgian Airport Consultants (1994) for the enviromnental impact
assessment of the expansion and development of the Banjul Intemational Airport. The data
obtained averaged values for immission points of relevance to our study are provided in Table
5./1.

background noise level in the study area. This level can be considered as representative for

the impact of aircraft operations is less significant leading to noise levels of respectively 46.5
and 45.9 dB (A). The urban and suburban areas of Banjul are characterized by a low or
insignified increase in noise level (<3 dB (A)) due to air traffic.

SECTIONA - PARTII. DEScRIPr;ON OF:rHE BASELINE ENvIRONMENT                         72



When evaluating the noise levels with respect to the general standard from US-EPA inspired on
the ISO standard of Leq (24) < 70 dB(A), there seems to be substantial carrying capacity at all
sites. However, the 70 dB limit is the exposure level which should protect public health. It is
well known that noise at lower level may lead to physiological effects such as hypertention,
headache, stomach pain, ... and to psychological effects such as sleep interferences, decreased
working capacity, general stress .... Most psychological effects start occuring from 45 dB (A)
on. Therefore, taking into account the recommendations of the ISO, the WHO and the US-EPA
guideline, the following guideline values may be suggested to prevent noise becoming a negative
effect:
-  residential area: Leq= 55 dB (A)
-  industrial area: Leq = 65 dB (A)
When taking this consideration into account, it appears that substantial carrying capacity still
exists in all selected sites as far as industrial area are concerned. For residential use the area at
the airport needs caution.
6.  TERRESTRIAL AND FRESHWATER FAUNA AND FLORA
6.1.   Introduction
The description of the fauna and flora will be based on existing inventory and literature data and
updated with additional fieldwork. The following natural habitat sites and protected areas will be
studied in particular:
-    Tanbi Wetland Complex,
-    Tanji Bird Reserve and the Bijol Islands
-    Abuko Natural Reserve
-    Salagi Forest Park.
All areas mentioned above are situated in the Banjul Capital Area and can be directly influenced
by the project, depending on the chosen site of the Free Trade Zone.
The areas Niumi National Park and Baobolon Wetland Reserve are situated in the inland. They
will not be directly influenced, but they are linked to the mangrove complex above by means of
migratory routes and movements of birds.

Protected areas in The Gambia and the Banjul Capital Area
Currently, there are six protected areas under the management of the Department of Parks and
Wildlife Management (DPWM). The total land area covers 39,236 ha, which amounts to 3.4 per
cent of the total land area of The Gambia. The six protected areas are: Abuko Nature Reserve,
Tanji Bird Reserve, Baobolon Wetland Reserve (not yet gazetted), Gambia River National Park
(Baboon Island), Niumi National Park and Kiang West National Park. The DPWM aims to have
5.0 per cent of the land area ultimately protected, including samples of all major habitats within
the country (Danso et al., 1997).
The Baobolon Wetland Reserve is designated as Ramsar site. As a signatory to the Ramsar
Convention on Wetlands of Intemational Importance, an assessment of two further areas for
designation as Ramsar sites (Niumi National Park and Tanbi Wetland complex) is currently
being made to bring the Gambian total to three sites.

6.2.   Flora
6.2.1. General information -- The Gambia
The vegetation of the Gambia is best described as Savanna Woodland with grass and shrub
undergrowth. The vegetation in the most western part of the Gambia is tropical of the southern
Guinea Savanna and in the dryer eastern areas the vegetation is more of the Sudan Savanna
vegetation type. The vegetation categories or types are related to soil and other edaphic factors
currently/presently closed forest or closed woodland types. Much of the original forest cover is
destroyed through human activities, frequent and uncontrolled bush fires, uncontrolled and
indiscriminate felling of trees and land clearing for farming. Much of the vegetation consists of
secondary vegetation types.
The first vegetation of the Gambia is re-engaged annually by bush-fires. As a result the
woodlands are generally severely burnt each year destroying most of the undergrowth (shrubs
and grass) which has a high regeneration potential and gradually destroy some economic tree
species.
The frequent incidence of bush fires affects most economic timber tree species thus reducing
their economic values as well as their usefulness and serious affect the growth of many
regeneration species e.g. Borassuss sp and Elaseis sp. However, they have root systems in their
early stages of growth, which are strong enough to enable them resist sweeping under ground
fires.
From the environmental point of view the forest cover provides protection to soil against
desertification, wind erosion and improves rainfall percoulation and temperature moderation.
The upland forests vegetation contributes to socio-economic development because it provides
wood for domestic energy, construction and other building materials, food and local medicine.
6.2.2.  Flora and vegetation of the study area
6.2.1.1. Introduction
The vegetation types of the study area are mostly Savanna woodland which could be related to
the Southern Guinea type which is part of the Gambia. Where farming persists and is intense,
there is very little or no forest vegetation cover but remains typical of a landscape dominated by
widely spaced tree species of economic importance (provision of food income or soil
SECTIONA - PARTII. DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                     75



enrichment). The tree species composition of the study areas is mainly3 Parkia biglobosa,
Bombax bnonopozense, Khaya snegalensis, Acacia albida, Danillia oliveri, Pterocarpus
erinaceous, Terminalia albida, Prosopis africana, Piliostigma thonningii, Lannea velutina,
Combretum  micranthum, Detarium senegalesis, Parinari excelsa, Dialium guineense, Elaeis
guineenis, Cassia sieberana, Borassus ethiapun, Chlorophora regiaa.
The Shrub vegetation has some evergreen Climbers such as Landolphia heudelolii and Saba
senegalensis. Before independence, Gambia was endowed with dense forests. However, with
the increasing human population, the status of the forest as a closed woodland started declining
at a faster rate due to an unsustainable form of exploitation (such as extraction of wood and non-
wood products, expansion of agricultural activities and frequent bush fires).
Currently the forest cover of the study area has undergone changes from a primary succession
forest type to a secondary succession forest resulting to a lesser dense forest, with poor stands,
lower growth and incidence of undesirable grass and tree species. The tree species diversity has
also lowered per area. Under the worst scenario, the forest vegetation is completely destroyed in
favour of crop cultivation. Thus the original closed forest is today replaced by an open Tree-
shrub Savanna ecology with trees less than 20 meters tall with shrub undergrowth. Such ecology
is severely burnt every year.
The increase in human population has to a large extent, resulted in a transformation of the areas
to both residential and farm land. Such typical areas are the Old Yundum and Kuloro study sites.
The degree of transformation varies on proximity to settlement areas. Extensive clearing for
agriculture followed a shifting cultivation pattern of farming in the past with big trees retained
on the farmlands.
With the development of forest management strategies, for the effective management of forest
resources, the forest cover is categorised into forest parks, community forests, private forests,
protection forests, protected forests and state forest types.
Forest Parks are forest reserves managed by Forest Department. Community forests are
managed by designated communities who have customary ownership rights to the land. Under
the Forestry Act, State Forest Land can revered to Community Forest as the communities with
the tenure rights to such land areas express interest to establish a community forest. Private
forests are natural forests growing or planted on privately owned lands. Protected forests are to
be managed for the main purpose of maintaining or improving the local environment. Protected
forests constitute all the mangroves on riverine forest and are managed by Forest Department.
3 For the terrestrial zones of the study area a list of tree species is given in Appendix 4. This list shows the scientific
name, common names (in English and/or local languages), common use and comments (predominantly ecological or
economic value of the species).
SECTION A - PART 1: DEScRiPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                         76



State Forest are all other remaining forest areas managed by Forestry Departrnent, except if they
exist in National Parks or in Nature Reserves.
Currently the study area is best described to be part of the State Forest category even though some
areas are being developed for residential and farming purpose. In consultations with Forestry
Department, indications are that the study area falls outside the potential Community Forest areas.
Even though the study area falls within the State Forest category, its forest resources are
seriously degraded mainly through human activities such as illegal felling of trees and to some
extent through the influence of climate variations (drought, stress and decline in rainfall)which
affects much of Gambia's forest vegetation and other production systems.

6.2.2.2. Protected areas outside of the TGP project sites
Before describing the flora and vegetation of 5 possible project sites, a short description of the
important habitats in the larger study area is given in the present chapter. The natural areas
described here will most probably not undergo any direct effect from project development.
Nonetheless, according to the TOR indirect impacts of the project on these natural habitats have to
be identified (see point 1.4. of the Introduction).
Tanbi Wetland Complex as well as Salagi and Furuyar Forest Parks are located within or nearby
the development areas - a discription is given in the next paragraph (6.2.2.3).
TANJI BIRD RESERVE
protection status:    national reserve (The Gambia Gazette 1993)
size and location:    612 hectares - Western Division, Kombo South district, Atlantic coast
betweern Ghana Town / Bald Cape and Tanji village - Bijol Islands
offshore.
The reserve encompasses a section of the Tourism Development Area
and a fish curing site near Ghana Town.
general description and habitats:
Coastal area and Bijol islets (some 2 km offshore Bald Cape). The topography of the main reserve is
generally flat but rising at the roadside to approximately 10 m above mean tide level; gradual slope
to the litoral areas.
The reserve shows a wide range of habitats: Tanji river and estuary, mangrove and salt pan area
- seasonal freshwater swamp -- saline lagoon, cliff and reef near Bald Cape - wooded grassland
Idunes and dry woodland. The recently constructed transect of the Kombo Coastal Road between
Sukluta and Kartung crosses the reserve from NE to SW over a distance of more than 4 km.
The reserve is under DPWM rnanagement and with strong support from the "Tanji Birders", a
U.K. based NGO that supports conservation projects and promotes ecotourism..
As the reserve protects a patchwork of habitats on the flyway for migratory birds, more than 300
species have been recorded here.
BIJILO FOREST PARK
protection status:    state forest park (The Gambia Gazette : early 1950s)
SECTIONA - PART/II: DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                   78



size and location:    51.3 hectares - Western Division, Atlantic coast between Kololi (South
of the resort area) and Bijilo village.
general description and habitats:
Coastal forest that is operated much as a nature reserve , rather than for forestry production.
Bijilo , together with Pirang Forest Park, are the sole representatives of original coastal climax
vegetation outside the protected areas (Sayer, 1992). This small forest park gives protection to a
mature stand of Rhun Palm (Borassus aethiopum) - Besides, the park includes a densely
forested area with 40 large tree and shrub species and at least five vine species . The dunes near
the beach are covered in grass and low bush .
As the park is very easy to reach from the Atlantic Coast resorts, it is very popular with bird
watchers and other people interested in ecotourism.
Infrastructure includes a well-maintained series of trails; many trees are labelled to serve the
educational aspect of ecotourism.
ABuKo NATURE RESERVE
protection status:    national reserve (The Gambia Gazette 1968)
size and location:  105 hectares - Western Division, Kombo North district; outside the
village of Lamin, some 25 km from Banjul and less than 4 km from Banjul
International Airport at Yundum.
The main road connecting Serekunda and Brikama passes the northeast
edge of Abuko, where it forms a barrier between the reserve and Tanbi
Wetland Complex.
general description:
The reserve has been protected as a water catchment area since 1916. It was officially declared a
nature reserve in 1968.
In 1978, an southwestern extension of 29ha was added to the original 76ha. The reserve is
rectangular in shape, centred on the Lamin stream which surfaces within the lower half of the
reserve.
The Department of Parks ancl Wildlife Management (DPWM), with headquarters near the
protected area, is in charge of management, conservation actions and research. Abuko's
infrastructure includes trails, an environmental education centre, animal orphanage, and several
excellent hides for wildlife observation and photography.
SECTION A - PART 1: DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                    79



Abuko Nature Reserve is surrounded by a buffer zone of 105 ha (exactly the same surface area as
the reserve itself). It is officially installed and indicated as part of "S.A.7" (special area of
Abuko nature reserve) on the Land Use Plan 2000. Unlike the reserve, this buffer zone has not
been fenced so far; encroachrnent on this zone lowers its protective function (seefigure 6.2./i).
Vegetation and habitat tWpes
The central part of the reserve is composed of gallerv forest (riparian forest) which surrounds a
chain of 3 pools. Gallery forest is characterised by a closed evergreen canopy, fairly open
understorey and high incidence of lianas. The canopy can reach 30 metres in height. The species
diversity is rich, though may be dominated by Antostemma senegalense, Dialium guineense and
Saba senegalensis (DPWM  & The Ramsar Bureau, 1997). The dense forest progressively gives
way to Guinea savanna the further one moves away from the water course. The extension to the
reserve is grassland and regenerating open wooded savanna.
Despite its small size the reserve is home to a wide diversity of mammals, reptiles, birds and
invertebrates. This is due primarily to its variety of habitat types coupled with the fact that the
area was protected in a relatively intact state. As Abuko supports the last few hectares of riparian
forest, it houses several species that have all but disappeared from the rest of the country (Sayer,
1992 - see also chapter 6.3.1.) Gallery forest originally occupied several valleys leading into
Tanbi Wetland but most of these valleys have been taken over for cultivation. Less intact
remnants of this forest type can be found near Mandinari: Pirang Forest Park and in narrow
fresh water fringes along the river. Freshwater flushes or seasonal freshwater streams are a
condition for gallery forest - where river water is brackish (salty), mangrove occurs instead.
A serious problem with regard to conservation of the gallery forest consists in the fact that the
water table at Abuko has fallen markedly in the past two decades. This has resulted in the
reduction of surface water and many of the largest forest trees and oil palms have died.
According to Sayer ( op. cit. ), it is unlikely that this last remaining stand of forest will survive
for long, in spite of its complete protection.

:                                   B§ffcrzone ufe\zon
Figure 6.2./1: Abuko Nature Reserve and buffTe zone



BAOBOLON WETLAND RESERVE
protection status:  national reserve (not yet gazetted);
Ramsar site no. 860 (designation date: 16/09/1996)
size and location:  20,000 hectares - North Bank Division , it consists of six major tributaries
of the River Gambia between Salikeni and Katchang villages, more than 60
km east of Banjul Capital Area
general description:
The site is a tidal wetland complex which is located on the north bank of the River Gambia.
The Baobolon is now a valley which stretches over a length of more than 140 km. For about six
months of the year the water in the Baobolon area remains brackish. There is likelihood that the
marshes act as a flood control and sediment retention mechanism. The area's mangrove
ecosystem provides an important fish breeding ground.
Current uses in the reserve are predominantly recreational: birdwatching, wildlife observation
and canoeing. The tidal flats have been the subject of low cost communal dyking schemes for
freshwater retention and rice production.
vegetation and habitats:
The most important wetland types are: tidal estuary, mangroves, salt marsh, tidal flats and
occasionallyflooded savanna woodlands adjacent to the salt marshes.
The mangrove forests are characterised by Rhizophora racemosa, R. mangle and Avicennia
africana. The barren flats support Sesuvium portulacastrum, Sporobolus spicatus, Paspalum
vaginatum and Diplachne fasca. The riparian and fringing savanna includes different strata
composed of tall trees, small trees and grass species.
The complex forms an important habitat for many resident and migratory species at a critical
stage of their biological cycle..
[description based on the Ramsar Database, http://www.ramsar.org , last modified : February,
2000 ]
NIUMI NATIONAL PARK
protection status:  national park (The Gambia Gazette 1986); proposed Ramsar site.
size and location: 4,940 hectares - North Bank Division, Lower Niumi district which is part of
the northern frontier between The Gambia and Senegal; northeast from
SECTION A - PARTII: DESCRIPTIONOF THE BASELINEENVIRONmENT                     82



Banjul, on the opposite bank of the river mouth and approximately 10 km
from the nearest project site of the Trade Gateway Project (Bund Road site).
general description and habitats.
The mainland of Niumi National Park and the island of Ginak represent an important coastal
wetland complex, of transfrontier importance: it is proposed to form a joint Ramsar site and
Biosphere reserve with the Delta du Saloum National Park (Senegal).
It represents a good range of coastal habitats, including mud flats, salt marshes, mangrove
swamps, lagoons as well as dunes, coastal grassland and dry woodland,.... and has many natural
characteristics in common with TANBI WETLAND COMPLEX (see next paragraph for more
details).
A full description of vegetation, habitats and wildlife of Niumi NP is given in DPWM & The
Ramsar Bureau, 1997.
6.2.2.3. Description of the project sites
BuND ROAD AND DENTON BRIDGE SITES
Both sites are situated within the perimeter of the proposed Ramsar site Tanbi Wetland Comple-x
( seefigure 6.2./2).
Tanbi occupies an area of approximately 6,000 hectares, the majority of which is composed of
mangrove forest. The wetland complex is located on the southern shore of the River Gambia and
includes the area between the island of Banjul and Cape Point in the north and extends to Lamin
and Mandinari Point in the south.
(i)    Habitats and vegetation of Tanbi Wetland Complex
The following overview is a concise description of the wetland types, based on the recent "Ramsar
Wetland Study - The Gambia" covering Niumi NP, Baobolon Wetland Reserve and Tanbi Wetland
Complex (DPWM and The Ramsar Bureau, 1997). An unpublished draft of the 2000 update of this
study has been consulted as well4
Tanbi predominantly represents a coastal and estuarine ecosystem, so that it could seem more
convenient to describe the habitats in chapter 7 (marine biota). However, transition from coastal
4Ramsar Wetland Study, The Gambia 2000. Management plans for Niumi National Park, Bao Bolon Wetland
Reserve and Tanbi Wetland Complex. DPWM under the Dept. of State for Fisheries, Natural Resources and the
Environment in Collaboration with the Ramsar Bureau.
SECTIONA - PARTII: DESCRIPTIONOF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                       83



to inland (including freshwater) wetland types is gradual. In order to avoid a fragmentary
description of the complex it was decided to present the general overview in the present chapter.
The following table lists both the marine wetland types and the inland wetland, plus associated
vegetation types. The terminology used follows the Ramsar Classification system for Wetland
types.

- The coastal strip (north of the highway) between Cape Point and Banjul is a diverse mosaic
of dune vegetation, lagoons, spots of dune woodland and grassed woodland, salt and
freshwater marshes.
The majority of the total Tanbi area, south of the highway, is composed of mangrove forest
covering approximately 4,800 hectares. The mangrove vegetation grades to salt marsh, bare
flats and dry woodland or grassed woodland to the west and south, with agricultural uses in
the peripheral (fringe) zones.
-  The main mangrove species is Rhizophora mangle though R. harissonii, Avicennia africana,
Laguncularia racemosa and Conocarpus erectus also occur. The community types present
include overwash forest, fringe forest and scrub forest. In comparison with upcountry
mangrove forests the main height of the Rhizophora stands is rather low (6-7 m from soil
level).
(ii)    Habitats present at or nearby the proposed FEZ sites
In view of the environmental assessment of the proposed project, it is highly relevant to
determine the vegetation types that actually occur near the development sites.
Table 6.2./3 results from a close examination of the available vegetation map, for the areas
immediately surrounding Bund Road site and Denton Bridge site. The map is based on detailed
field investigation in the framework of the Ramsar study mentioned above. It has been drawn in
1997 and according to our owni field reconnaissance (May to July, 2000) still represents the
current situation very well.

AIRPORT SITE
The land at the Airport site is basically flat, level and covered with a vegetation typical of a
secondary succession consisting mainly of shrubs, trees and grass. There is no evidence of
clearing for farming, but from observation it is evident that the area in the past cleared of its
original vegetation. It is more of a disturbed open wood land with a heavy shrub under growth
and trees; many of which are less than 20 meters tall. The original forest ecology was more of a
closed forest woodland which is now lost more probably through farming, use of wild fires for
SECTiON A - PARTII. DESCRIPTiON OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                    89



clearing and hunting as well as through felling of economic and valuable tree species. The
ecology today is more of an open woodland consisting of secondary vegetation species. These
include Bombax bunoponsense, Daniellia oliveri, Terminalia albida, and Azadirachta indica..
The common tree species found growing in this area include Elaeis guineenis, Chlorophora
regia, Parkia biglobosa, Daniella oliveri, Landolphia heudelotii, Terminalia albida, cassia
sieberiana, Ficus spp., Lannea velutina, Combretum spp.,Pterocarpus erinaceous etc..
However, the most frequently occurring species are Parkia biglobosia, Elaeis guineenisi, Cassia
sieberiana, Bombax bunoponsense, Parkia biglobosa, Daniella oliveri, Lannea velutina and
Guiera senengalensis.
The most common regenerating economic tree species are Daniella oliveri and Terminalia
albida followed by the combriturn species e.g. Guiera senegalensis. Also present is a small strip
(nitch) of Oxytenanthera abyssinica (African Bamboo) but of a small size and poor quality. As
you move inwards from the side of the road leading to the terminal, there is evidence of
uncontrolled and illegal felling of trees of all species mainly for firewood, poles and post and
other light construction material. However, the most commonly felled species are Terminalia,
Cassia, Daniellia, Bombax and Pteracarpus species.
It is also very interesting to realise a certain pattern of tree growth established in the area.
Approximately 100 - 200 meters from the road side is a long stretch of well established Bombax
spp. regeneration stand of 5-10 cm stem diameter size that runs in the North - South direction
parallel to the Airport road. Such five or ten years old stand of Bombax is common within study
site. In between the pure stand of the Bombax regeneration, is a mixture of all kinds of trees and
shrubs but the most dominant species in all such situation is the Terminalia spp.. However, as
you get closer to the fence line of the Airport that runs parallel to the Banjul-Serrekunda
Highway, the Bombax species disappears gradually giving way to a hectrogeneous stand of
Pterocarpus, Parkia, Dialium, Daniella and other Combretum spp. e.g. Guiera senegalensis.
It is interesting to note that there is no evidence of grazing in the area. This is either because the
entry of livestock is highly restricted or cattle owners are not taking advantage of the pasture
available on this site. Therefore due to the absence of grazing, bush fires and the amount of
regeneration on the site, there is great potential for the vegetation and ecology in general to be
restored once more to a more primary ecosystem.
KULORO SITE
Kuloro site is mainly an agricultural production site being farmed for more than 30 years now
under short fallow periods. Crops commonly grown are groundnuts, findo and millet in a
rotation system. Currently farmers are clearing their sites in preparation for the coming rains. A
certain portion of the area on the site is around Mandina Ba is always set aside for grazing
SECTION A - PART IT: DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                   90



particularly during the cropping periods (personal communication with farmers). The end of the
crop fields marks the beginning of the fruit orchards close to the Touba-kuta village.
The ecology is a typical open shrub land with trees very sparsely distributed. The common tree
species in the study site include Khaya senegalensis, Chlorophora regina, Parkia biglobosa,
Elaeis guineensisi, Daniellia oliverli, Acacia albida, Pterocarpus erinaceous, Ficus capensis,
Spondias mombin, Bombax buonopozenses, prosopis africana. The common shrub species are
Guiera and Combretum, Terminalia etc. The most frequent species are Ficus, Bombax and
Parkia and the economic tree species include Elaeis, Khaya, Cholorophora, parkia, Daniellia,
Pterocarphus and Bombax. However, these species are not many on the site and as result the
economic and ecological values of these species could not be considered high. The tree species
are sparsely distributed within the site and the most regenerating species are the combretum
species most of which are cleared during the farmn preparation periods.
There are no Community forest management activities within this study site. However, it has a
high potential for plantation development even though this is very unlikely due to land pressure
on most customary land areas. On the Western side, is a well established Gmelina plantation
with few Tectona grandis (Teak) compartments. The plantation is mainly for supply of poles,
post and building material. This is the gazetted Furuyar Forest Park of 405 hectares.
SUKUTA SITE
A great proportion of the site is currently designated to agricultural development. On the eastern
side of the newly constructed coastal high way, individual property landed areas are designated
for Cashew plantation and orchard development as well as residential purposed. These proposed
areas covers a very large portion of the study site. Even though the Cashew plantation is not
fully established or developed, there is ample evidence of efforts to develop this proposed
Cashew plantation. Cashew plants are planted at 10 - 15 meters spacing in a hole area. There is
evidence of failures, which could be due to poor planting or animals browsing on the young
seedlings.
Next to the proposed Cashew plantation site further inwards in the western direction is a
privately owned fenced property being developed as mango orchard in combination sheep
fattening. Next to this private property is another proposed private farmland with cashew
planted on the fence line to provide live fence and permnanent boundaries. There is also evidence
of land used change from agriculture to residential between the end of the proposed cashew
plantations and the NAWEC boreholes on the Old Sukuta/Gunjur gravel road. There is also the
clearing of fanrlands in preparation for the coming rains.
The vegetation of this site is best described as Open Tree-Shrub Savanna type. The ecology of
the area until probably the last 20 to 30 years was a Closed Woodland Forest of the Guinea-
Savanna type. However, with time and through development much of the forest cover (natural
SECTIONA - PARTII: DESCRIPTiON OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                  91



vegetation) has been destroyed through human influence ( tree felling and bush fires) resulting to
what presently exists. The woodland in addition to bush fires and tree felling, is disturbed by
cattle grazing on the young shoot and regeneration.
There is evidence of good regeneration of Borassus ethiopium (rhun palm) in some parts of the
study site. This is a clear indication that this area was most probably dominated by rhun palm
and oil palm tree species with other trees species growing in between. If the regeneration could
be protected from bush fires and other forms of human and animal destruction, the area could be
a good source of construction material.
Other than the regeneration of rhun palm which is profusely growing in most parts of the study
site, there are other tree species. These include Parkia biglobosa, Borassus aethiopium, Ficus
spp., Elaeis guineensis, Chlorophora regia, Combretum micranthum, Cola cordifolia and other
shrub plants species.
The tree stand density of the economic timber tree species could be estimated to be less than 100
stem per hectare (based on ocular estimation). The most common of the mature (big) tree
species are Parkia biglobosa, Ficus spp., Piliostigma thonningii, Lannea acida and Cola
cordifolia. The common shrub species include Morinda geminata, Combretum spp. etc.
There are no Community Forests identified within this area and the only Forest Park closed to
the site is Salagi Forest Park. Salagi Forest Park is a Gmelina (Gmelina aborea) plantation of
262 hectares developed for production of poles, posts, fuel wood and sawn timber between 1980
- 1986 and a natural stand of rhun palm.
6.3.   Fauna
6.3.1. Present state in The Gambia and the Banjul Capital Area
Most larger mammals have disappeared from The Gambia but approximately
108 species of mammals still occur. These include the hippopotamus, three species of
antelope Cephalophus spp, bushbuck Tragelaphus scriptus, Temrninck's red colobus monkey
Procolobus badius tenminckii, red patas monkey Erythrocebus patas, Campbell's monkey
and serval Felis serval. Neither of them is endemic. Several species do not breed in the
country but occasionally visit from Senegal: for example, the lion Panthera leo and the roan
antelope Hippotragus equinus.
Forty-six reptiles are known in The Gambia. The slender-snouted crocodile Crocodylus
cataphractus has disappeared in the past decade, and the dwarf crocodile Osteaolaemus
tetraspis survives only at Abuko Nature Reserve. The coastal skink Chalcides armitagei is the
only vertebrate endemic to The Gambia although it is likely to occur in similar habitats in
SECTION A - PARTII DESCRIPTIONOFT;fEBASELINEENVIRONMENT                       92



Senegal. Several species of snakes found at Abuko are not otherwise known north of the
forests of Guinea. All of the amphibian species found in The Gambia (26) belong to the
savanna fauna ; neither of them is a closed forest animal.
Hunting occurs in the riverine wetlands and many species are taken; the Nile crocodile
Crocodylus niloticus and hippopotamus Hippopotamus amphibius have been hunted almost to
the point of extinction and are both endangered in The Gambia.
The wetland and mangrove areas have very rich avifaunas. Around 500 species of birds have
been recorded in The Gambia, of which 220 are known to be breeding within the country. A
systematic list is given by Morel and Morel (1990) and by Gore (1999). No threatened bird
species are listed in Collar and Stuart, named by Sayer et al., 1992.
The Tanbi wetland complex occupies an area of approximately 6,000 ha, the majority of
which consists of overwash and fringe mangrove forest. The predominance of mangrove
within the Tanbi Wetland Complex together with its proximity to the urban centers of Banjul
and Serrekunda limits the diversity of large mammalian fauna to be found. Manatee
(Trichecus senegalensis) occur in low numbers within the mangrove but due to persecution
through hunting, may be in danger of becoming locally extinct. The Atlantic humpbacked
dolphin (Sousa teuzii) does not appear to enter the mangrove complex but has been spotted
from the River Gambia on the eastern boundary and on the coastal strip to the north. The
green turtle (Chelonia mydas) grazes the seagrass beds (Phylodoce nodosa) in the shallow
marine waters. Marsh mongoose (Atilax paludinosus) is frequent throughout the complex and
the clawless otter (Aonyx capensis) occupies at least the southern part of the area towards
Mandinari Point.
The avifauna of the area is rich and diverse, with large numbers of both resident waterfowls
and seabirds, and both inter-African and Palearctic migrants (Department of Parks & Wildlife
Management, 1997).
The Tanii Bird Reserve consists largely of fairly open areas with stands of Gingerbread Plum
and a wide variety of others habitats. The sizeable wooded area harbours interesting species of
birds (Violet turaco, Green crombec, ...). In addition, the Tanji lagoon attracts large numbers
of terns and waders in spring and autumn, although these tend to be less numerous during the
remainder of the dry season.
Green vervets and Western red colobus monkeys occur in the more wooded areas. Around the
edges of the lagoon there are often large numbers of fiddler crabs (Ward, 1994).
The Abuko Nature Reserve houses many species of animals that have disappeared from the
rest of the country. The Abuko Nature Reserve contains the last population of the endangered
dwarf crocodile Osteolaemus tetrapsis in The Gambia, which is the northernmost limit of its
distribution. The virtual disappearance of Ostreolaemus tetrapsis is mainy due to forest loss
SECTION A - PARTI: DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELiNE ENVIRONMENT                 93



and the resultant depletion of their breeding habitat, pools in gallery forest. The Gambian
Wildlife Conservation Department has initiated a program to halt the decline of the crocodiles
with the development of an intricate irrigation scheme in the Abuko Nature Reserve.
The mammals are represented by antelope and duiker (5 species), 2 species of porcupines,
civets, mongooses, servals, genets, cane rats, squirrels, monkeys (Western Red Colobus,
Green Vervet Monkey, Monia Monkey and Red Patas Monkey) a.o. Reptiles too are well
represented, such as monitor and agama lizards, skinks, geckos. Snakes include the large
African python and the smaller Royal python, as well as venemous species such as Puff
Adder, Cobra and Green mamba.
The ornithological value of this protected area is very high, since many of the species are not
found anywhere else in the cciuntry. Around 270 species of birds are found within the Abuko
Nature Reserve. The reserve contains an intact pocket of gallery forest in which numerous
forest dependent species occur such as the Green Touraco, little Greenbul and the Yellow
Breasted Apalis (Jensen et al., 1980 ; Sayer et al., 1992).
The Salagi Forest Park is a Gmelina (Gmelina aborea) plantation developed for the
production of poles, posts, ftiel wood and sawn timber. No published data about fauna are
available for this area.
The Niumi National Park, contiguous with the Parc National du delta du Saloum in
neighbouring Senegal, protects small populations of manatee, crocodile, clawless otter, hyena,
bushbuck and duiker, plus various species of monkeys. Many species of birds can be seen,
especially waders and water birds. The Baobolon Wetland Reserve is a Ramsar site and
protects various aquatic mammal species, such as manatee (Trichecus senegalensis), clawless
otter (Aonyx capensis) and sitatunga (Tragelaphus spekii). The Baobolon complex forms also
an important habitat for many resident and migratory species at a critical stage of their
biological cycle. Resident and migrant waterfowls are present in this area.
The Niumi National Park and the Baobolon Wetland Reserve are both located outside the
Banjul Capital Area.
6.3.2. Fauna of the five nominated sites
The five nominated sites were visited on 28 and 29 June 2000: Bund Road, Denton Bridge,
Airport area, Kuloro area and Sukuta area. The description of the fauna of these five sites
will be based on published data and limited especially to the species of birds, except when
other data were avalaible.
SECTION A - PARTII: DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                   94



6.3.2.1. Bund Road - Banjul
The Bund Road area is included in Tanbi Wetland Complex (a proposed Ramsar site) and
consists of a tidal estuarine environment with open mud flats at low tide on the ocean side,
and ditches and flats with Rhizophora mangrove edged pools, infiltrated with city sewerage
on the other side.
Observations of the bird species were made by Barlow (named in «< Ramsar Wetland Study
- The Gambia)), 1997) and by Jensen and Kirkeby (1980) and are given in table 6.3./1.
The mammalian fauna at the Bund Road area must be limited due to its proximity to the
urban centre and the degree of activity in this area. Genets (Genetta sp.), Crawshay's hare
(Lepus crawshayi) may be found.
Crustaceans and molluscs occur in the ponds and rivulets. Njie (named in << Ramsar
Wetland Study - The Gambia )), 1997) mentions that the most abundant species of fish
occuring in a small stream within mangrove swamps at Bund Road was the Tilapia and
coincided with high turbidity levels.

6.3.2.2. Denton Bridge - Banjul
Denton Bridge area is included in the Tanbi Wetland Complex (a proposed Ramsar site)
and consists of coastal grassland/scrubland, brakish lagoons, mudflats and mangroves.
Birds to be seen in Denton Bridge area, especially at Toll Point, are listed in table 6.3./2.
The species of bird observed at Camalou Corner and Cape Creek are mainly the same as
mentionned from Bund Road (see above table 6.3./1).
The remark made about the mammalian fauna at Bund Road (see above § 6.3.2.1) may be
of concern for Denton Bridge area.
The invertebrate fauna consists predominantly of crustaceans and molluscs. Mangrove
vegetation has dense clusters of mangrove oysters (Crassostrea tulipa) attached to its
stems. Larvae and post-larvae of the pink shrimps were found in high densities, especially
at a coastal lagoon mangrove at mile 5 off Banjul Highway and behind the fish processing
factory, particularly at low tides. The species is reported to occur throughout the area as
larvae, post-larvae and juveniles.
Of all fish species observed in the area, tilapia predominates in numbers, followed by
mullets (Banana mullet Mugil bananensis, Flat head mullet Mugil cephalus) and leaping
African mullet Mugil capuri) (Njie, named in << Ramsar Wetland Study - The Gambia >>,
1997).

6.3.2.4. Kuloro site - south of Koloro
On the Western side is located the Furuyar Forest Park, a well-established Gmelina (Gmelina
arborea) plantation with few Tectona grandis (Teak) compartments. As there were no data on
bird species available for this forest park, data were taken from Kabafita, Bamba and
Nyambai Forest Parks (Jensen and Kirkeby, 1980) located at around 2.5 km NE of Kuloro
site. It concerns a tall dry woodland at mile 19-21 (31-34 km from Banjul) on the highway,
and large parts of Bamba ancl Nyambai Forest Parks are also Gmelina plantations (see Table
in Appendix 4.2.).
SECTIONA - PARTII: DESCRIPTION OF 7HE BASELINE ENVIRONMENr                                          100



6.3.2.5. Sukuta site - east of Salagi Forest Park
A   great  proportion  of  the  site  is  currently  designated  to  agricultural
development - cashew plantation and orchard. Vegetation of this site is best described as
open tree-shrub savanna type.
Observations of bird species were made by Jensen and Kirkeby (1980) at Sukuta Wood, a
woodland area 19 km from Banjul and 2 km SW of Sukuta on the Brufut road (see Table in
appendix 4.2.). The birdlife represents a mixture of species from the Guinea Savanna
woodland and from the Forest-Savanna Mosaic riverine forest.
6.3.2.6. General characteristics of the bird species observed
General characteristics of the bird species observed in the 5 localities are given below.
These general characteristics concern (Gore, 1990):
-the status and the degree of abundance,
-the breeding,
-the migration and movements.
STATUS AND DEGREE OF ABUNDANCE
The status and the degree of abundance of the bird species observed in the 5 nominated
sites are given in Appendix 4.3..
Based on the classification given in the table in Appendix 4.3. and the selective
observations made at the 5 sites (see above), it appears that the status of resident breeder
(RB) is dominant in Airport area, Kuloro site and Sukuta site, while in the Bund Road area,
palaearctic passage (PV) or winter visitors (WV) are present in force. In Denton Bridge
area, resident breeders and palaearctic passage or winter visitors are both represented.
BREEDING
The breeding season in The Gambia is generally considered to be during the rains, late
June-early November, and for most species this is approximately correct, but several
species have an extended season covering 8-9 months and breed in both the wet and dry
sesaons, while some nest mainly in the dry season and a very few may be found nesting
throughout the year.
SECTION A - PARTII: DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRoNMENT                   101



All passerines which feed their young on regurgitated seeds or a mixed diet of seeds and
insects breed during the latter part of the rains, between August and December. This
group includes most of the Ploceidae, the Fringillidae, Emberizidae and the Estrildidae.
Nearly all passerines which feed their young exclusively on a diet of insects start building
and laying in late May or early June so that hatching coincides with the start of the rains
in late June, when insect prey becomes abundant almost overnight. Breeding extends
through November and occasionnally into December. This group includes the
Campephagidae, Pycnonotidae, Laniidae, Muscicapidae and Oriolidae. The only non-
passerines which are strictly limited to this season are the Cuculidae, most of which are
parasitic on such passerines.
The breeding season for some non-passerines, e.g. water birds and those which feed their
young mainly on a diet of insects, is closely linked to the rains, though the water birds, in
particular, continue breeding for 2-3 months into the dry season, when the freshwater
marshes are still flooded. Of the other non-passerines, fruit-eaters generally start nesting
during the rains so that the young hatch at the start of the dry season when most trees
begin to fruit. The Accipitridae, Falconidae and most young ground-nesting species
breed exclusively during the dry season, starting shortly after the rains have ended and
continuing until April or even May.
MIGRATION AND MOVEMENTS
Four types of migrants can be distinguished in The Gambia. Most numerous are the
Palaearctic migrants. A great deal of research has been carried out on Palaearctic migrants
in Africa, and in The Gambia there is considerable data on one group, the waders, but less
is known about passerines and other visitors. The Gambia does not appear to be an
important wintering ground for many species and most Palaearctic birds can be considered
as passage migrants with a few individuals remaining through the winter.
Two forms of intra-African migration are evident: non-breeding visitors and migrant
breeders. The non-breeding visitors are present during the dry season from November until
May and the migrant breeders only during and immediately after the rains. For the migrant
breeders,   little   is   known    about   their   movements   during   the
off-season. Several species may be either resident or migrant; more work will have to be
undertaken before their true status is known.
Palaearctic Passage and Winter visitors
Nearly a quarter of the species on the Gambian list are birds which breed in Europe,
many of them in the far north of the continent, and migrate south along the western
continental seabord in late summer and autumn to spend the winter in sub-Saharan
Africa. The mainly southerly migration is from mid-September until mid-November,
SECTION A - PART II: DESCRIPTION OF rHE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                  102



with a peak in October. Passage migrants at this time are Spotted and Pied Flycatchers
(Muscicapa striata and Ficedula hypoleuca), Olivaceous Warblers (Hippolais pallida),
Willow Warblers (Phylloscopus trochilus), Winchats (Saxicola rubetra), White and
Yellow Wagtails (Motacilla alba and M. flava). The large majority of passerines move
further south to winter, only a few individuals remaining to overwinter but some,
particularly Nightingales (Luscinia megarhynchos), Redstarts (Phoenicurus phoenicurus)
and Subalpine Warblers (Sylvia cantillans), overwinter in considerable numbers, Sylvia
cantillans being one of the very few species to utilise mangrove as an important habitat.
The spring migration from February to April, with a peak in mid-March, is less
noticeable and it seems that the great majority of migrants overfly The Gambia on their
northwards migration.
The Waders
The Gambia is an important passage and wintering ground for Palaearctic waders. It is
difficult to say how many actually winter and how many stay for only a few days or
weeks en route to and from their breeding grounds in northern Europe. The wintering
range of most species extends over a considerable distance, some of them reaching South
Africa. Within this range individuals probably have their regular wintering sites, just as
they have regular nesting sites in Europe. The most abundant of the waders to be seen on
the mudflats in the vicinity of Banjul are Little Stints (Calidris minuta) and Curlew
Sandpipers (Calidris fer-ruginea). They are present from late July until mid-April with
maximum numbers in September-October and Marsh, clearly indicating that only a small
percentage winter as far north as The Gambia. Flocks are still present on the northward
spring migration at the end of April, occasionally in early May, and the first returning
birds arrive back again in the last days of July.
Intra-African Migrants
The regular dry season visitors appear to be birds which breed inland and probably
further north in the Sahel during the short rains and move to the coast and the borders of
the larger rivers to spend the off-season. They include some of the most brilliantly
coloured birds to be seen in West Africa: 3 species of bee-eaters, the Blue-cheecked
Merops superciliosus (possibly a migrant from Morocco, so that it could be classified as
a Palaearctic migrant), Carmine Merops nubicus and White-throated (Merops albicollis).
The migrant breeders arrive just before the start of the rains, mostly in May, and depart
after breeding. Most leave immediately after the end of the rains, sometimes earlier. The
SECTIONA - PARTII: DESCRiPTION OF THE BASELNE ENVIRONVmENT                   103



migrant breeders are: Marabou Storks (Leptoptilos crumeniferus), Wood Ibis (Ibis ibis),
Levaillant's Cuckoo (Ciamator levaillantii), Didric Cuckoo (Chrysococcyx caprius),
Pigmy Kingfisher (Ceyx picta), Senegal Kingfisher (Halcyon senegalensis), Nigerian
Little Bunting (Emberiza forbesi) and Rock Bunting (E. tahapisi). Their movements
outside the breeding season are not known.
SECTIONA - PARTII: DESCRIPTION OF IHE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                      104



7.   MARINE FAUNA A4ND FLORA 
The coastal zone of The Gambia is the scene of large areas of ecologically sensitive areas. These
include the area of mangrove swamps between Toll Point to Cape Creek as well as the Oyster
Creek and Stink Corner mangrove swamps, which form in large part the Tanbi wetland reserve,
which is proposed as a RAMSAR site. Further south, the Tanji Bird Reserve is a protected area.
Furthermore, Brufut wood, Solifor point, Tujering lagoons, the River Kakima delta en Kachuma
Forest, the Dau Dula to Kartong coastal forest and the Allaheim river mouth are the most
sensitive areas of the south coast of The Gambia (see Figure 7.11).
Detailed descriptions of the fauna and flora of the relevant mangrove and wetland areas are given
in the former chapter.
7.1.   Mangroves
The most relevant wetland and mangrove area with respect to the project development is the
Tanbi Wetland Reserve. Mangroves are generally recognized as an area of vital resources. They
serve as nursery and feeding grounds for many groups of aquatic organisms and are one of the
most fertile ecosystems.
The mangrove of the Tanbi Wetland reserve is intersected by a small coastal stretch at Mandinari
before continuing along the riverside in another mangrove area (Mandina). Discussions with
DPWM revealed the importance of this area at Mandinari Coast as corridor function for f.i.
larger bush mammals.
7.2.   Seagrass fields
Some seagrass fields can be found in the shallow waters before Tanbi Wetland, upon which the
manatees feed. However no detailed information is available on the extent of the seagrass fields.
7.3.   Endangered species
Among the important marine mammals, the manatee (Trichechus senegalensis), the hump-
backed dolphin (Sousa plumbea) and the green turtle (Chelonia mydas) must be mentioned. They
are either local or rare.
