7.3.1.  Status on the green turtles
Sea turtle nesting takes place during the wet season. Certainly, islands offshore such as these of
Bijoli are favoured places for nesting. Although nesting still takes place along the beaches on
mainland, it may be assumed that this has decreased considerably (according to Director NEA,
Fisheries Officers and others). Information on the actual number of nests is lacking. Therefore,
the Dept. Of Parks and ... has started a voluntary project together with NEA for collecting
information on the number and location of sea turtle nesting sites along The Gambian coastline.
This information will be collected through fisherrnan, volunteers, local people, etc. This project
will start this wet season (July up to November '00).
During the last years, also 2 leatherback turtles have been reported in The Gambian waters.
These are told to be also more common in the past.
Although turtle fishing is prohibited, it is seen by part of the population as a delicacy. Formerly,
sea turtles were known to be sold on 2 markets (amongst them Serekunda market). This is known
to be still going on to a much lesser extent. Although illegal, If fisherman are caught with sea
turtles, they declare it as accidental by-catch, and they make sure the turtle is dead before
bringing on land.
7.3.2.  Status on Cetaceans
Very little information is available on the status of the cetaceans in The Gambia. The Dept. of
Parks and Wildlife Management indicates the efforts undertaking under the Convention on
Migratory Species (CMS). In this framework collection is gathered on Cetacea in the West-
African Region. Different kind of dolphins are present. Bottlenose dolphin is frequently and
easily sought after for tourist sightings, offshore from Banjul. The status on the Atlantic
humpback dolphin has been questioned. A specific effort will be undertaken this and the
following years to collect information on its presence in the Gambian waters, again very much as
for the sea turtles, with the participation of others such as fisherman. Generally, bottlenose
dolphins are still quite common, << although they do not seem to frequent the Gambia River as
often as in the past») (quote Director NEA).
7.3.3. Sharks
Sharks are also present in The Gambian seawaters. Very unlike as may be thought, sharks are
more and more coming under pressure due to fishery activities, first as a by-catch, secondly as a
fishery activity (for shark fin export towards Asia). This is reported to occur along the West-
African coast. The extent of it in the Gambian waters is not known.
SECTIONA - PARrII: DESCRIPTION OF TIE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                       107



7.4.   Fish resources
Following description is mainly extracted from a very good and recent draft report of Mr. A.N.
Mendy (Department of Fisheries).
The Gambia lies between Lat. 13'N and 13°40'N and it is located within FAO Region 34, the
Eastern Central Atlantic Ocean Zone. The Gambia has a relatively small continental shelf area of
about 4000 km2 with approximately 70 km coastline5. The Gambian coastal zone is situated in
one of richest fishing zones of the world. This is on one hand due to the upwelling of new
nutrients from the deep waters into the euphotic zone, the retention of phytoplankton and
regeneration of nutrients in the upper water column due to stratification and the nutrient input
from the River Gambia. The phytoplankton growth attracts many pelagic fish which on the other
hand produces the main food source for predators such as sharks and cetaceans. Coastal
resources with regard to fauna is very rich and diverse.
7.4.1. Fish stock assessments
For the assessment of the fish stocks, The Gambia has the assistance of the FAO-NORAD
(Norwegian Aid Development) monitoring programme which runs since 1981 in The Gambian
waters. The research vessel <Dr. Fridtjof Nansen>> performs a yearly survey off North-West
Africa including Morocco, Mauritania, Senegal, The Gambia and on a few occasions Guinea-
Bissau. The main purpose is to map the distribution and estimate the abundance of small pelagic
fish in this region. The Norwegian Institute of Marine Research (IMR in Bergen, Norway) has
the main scientific responsibility in collaboration with scientists from collaborating countries.
These surveys are the only source of data information on marine fish resources of The Gambia,
and still predominantly the pelagic fish resources.
7.4.2. Pelagic fish resources
The latest years, there has been a considerable increase of pelagic fish resources from between
100,000 and 160,000 metric tonnes (MT) during the period 1992-1997 up to 510,000 MT in
1999 (see Table 7.4./I). The increase is in part due to the absence since the 1990s of pelagic
industrial fisheries in The Gambian waters, but also to changing nutrient conditions due to
upwelling (leading to enhanced phytoplankton growth and transfer along the trophic chain).
About 72% of pelagic fish consist of round and flat sardinellas, 26% are mainly carangids and
2% horse mackerel. The carangids and associated pelagic group comprises mainly bumper, false
scad, lookdown with small amounts of barracudas and hairtails.
5 Different figures appear with respect to the lenght of the coastline
SECTIONA - PARTII: DESCRIPTIONOF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                    108



7.4.3.  Demersal fish resources
The only extensive swept area demersal trawl survey conducted in The Gambian waters was in
November 1986 by a Spanish research vessel. Less comprehensive demersal trawl surveys were
incorporated in the 1992 and 1995 acoustic survey by R/V Dr. Fridjoft Nansen. Grouping these
results gives an indicative6 downward trend from above 40,000 MT in 1986 to 22,000 MT in
1995. As the demersal fish are the main fished resources at this moment, there is a clear need for
an updated extensive assessment of the actual stocks. This is planned to take place in September
- October 2000 in the framework of the Nansen survey.
Table 7.4.11: Estimates of demersal and pelagic fish biomass in The Gambia (metric tonnes,
MT)

1986         43,645
1992         30,000                              160,000
1995         22,000                              156,000
1996                                             122,000
1997                              -              113,000
1998                                             173,000
1999           _                                 510,000
Source: Dr. Fridtjof Nansen 1992, 1995, 1998 & 1999 cruise reports CECAF/TECH/87/87 - Exploratory
fishing cruisefor dermersal stocks in waters of The Republic of The Gambia (in: draft report Department
of Fisheries, 2000)
A set of conservative working maximum sustainable yields (MSY - Maximum quantity of fish
that can be loaded annually without harming the resource base) has been established from
various analysis: 200,000 MT for pelagic fish, 15,000 MT for demersal fish, 1,000 MT for
shrimp and 1,000 MT for cephalopods.
Demersal fish stocks are enduring the highest pressure and are the least known, while pelagic
fisheries have been largely unexploited since the 1990s and stocks have increased by a
threefold. The demersal fauna is extremely varied. Near the river estuary the most numerous
species are the croakers (Sciaenidae), grunts (Pomadasyidae), thread fins (Polynemidae) and
mullets (Mugilidae).
SECTION A - PARTII. DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINEENVIRON.MfENr                   109



7.4.4. Fish resources in the River Gambia
As the River Gambia is seasonally saline/brackish up to 200km inland, these river borders are for
this reason considered by some as an enlargement of the coastal zone. The River Gambia
remains however to be characterized as an estuary with bordering wetlands and mangroves
which makes it very suitable as a fish feeding and spawning site. The River Gambia is also
believed to have considerable fresh water fish species of economic potential further inland.
7.5.   Fisheries
7.5.1. Industrial fisheries
Industrial fishery operations involve large offshore vessels, nowadays completely in foreign
ownership. The Gambia has different agreements giving access to fishing vessels, such as the
EEC/Gambia Fishing Agreement (expired in June 1996), the reciprocal Fishing Agreement with
Senegal and the Japan Tuna Fishing Agreement. As The Gambia has no industrial fishing port,
catches are landed outside The Gambia7. Fishing methods applied are formerly trawling (stem
and beam), purse seining and long lining. Nowadays it is mainly trawling. The number of fishing
vessels licensed to operate in The Gambian waters have been drastically reduced8 from  102
vessels in 1993 to 19 vessels in 1997 (a level which is still maintained). This is supplemented
with an obligation for foreign fishing vessels to board a Gambian representative on the ship
(observer programme). This person reports daily by radio contact on catches made and makes 6-
monthly reports to the Department of Fisheries.
Despite these measures, illegal fishing (poaching) is a widely distributed practice. On the one
hand, the observers on the fishing vessels are paid by the foreign fishing companies.
Underestimating the catches made is worthwhile as they have to pay an equivalent to 10% of the
total catch (this originates from the requirement for landing 10% of the catch). On the other
hand, other means for inspection of illegal fishing trawls are limited. There is however an aerial
survey which is conducted under a collaboration programme with Luxemburg. The only fishing
vessel of The Gambian Navy is also used for inspection. In short, demersal catch figures should
be regarded as an underestimation.
6--~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
6giving the smaller extent of the 1992 and 1995 surveys
7 despite the fact that there is an obligation to land at least 10% of the catches in The Gambia, this obligation is met
by paying the required taxes
8 motivation would be to reduce the impact on the demersal fish stocks
SECTiONA - PARTII: DESCRIPTION oF THE BASELINE ENVIRoNmHET                         110



The industrial fisheries is nowadays mainly focussing on demersal fishes. There is a drastic drop
in industrial catch from over 20,000 MT in the late 1980s to early 1990s to just under 10,000
MT. This drop is partially attributed to the stopping of the Seagull Cold Stores.
7.5.2.  Artisanal fisheries
The artisanal fisheries sector has evolved to the use of highly mobile fishing crafts and improved
fishing techniques. The sub-sector supplies almost all fish found on tables of average Gambian
and is presently the single major supplier of raw fish materials to fish processing plants to The
Gambia. There are about 1,785 canoes countrywide with 30,4% motorization. There are about
1,686 fisherman of which a large part is foreign9 (40% general and up to 70% on the Atlantic
coast). About 200,000 people benefit directly or indirectly from artisanal fisheries related
activities. Different fishing gear is used such as gill nets, hook and lines, traps, and seine nets. As
well pelagic as demersal fish are captured. The landing of the (coastal) artisanal fisheries was
estimated at about 27,000 MT in 1998. Inland fish catch statistics are however not in the above
figures included. These are not collected due to logistical and manpower constraints. The bulk of
the coastal artisanal landings were mainly bonga (Ethmalosa fimbriata) an estuarine species. On
average about 20% of artisanal landings are processed traditionally (smoked, sun dried after
salting or fermentation). There is a gradual upward trend of artisanal fish catches, due to
improved fish catching methods and new entrants into the sector.
7.5.3.  Fish processing plants
There are 6 fish processing plants operating in The Gambia at the time of reporting. The National
Partnership Enterprise and Pelican are based at the Bund Road in Banjul, just beside the fishing
pier and the fishing harbour of Banjul. These are the two largest fishery processing plants. They
can both handle freezing of pelagic and demersal fish. Other plants only process dermersal fish.
The four other processing plants are situated near the Denton Bridge. Rosarnund Trade and
LyeFish when having crossed the Denton Bridge towards Banjul on the right side, Kendaka and
Barra before crossing the bridge towards Banjul on the left side. The Fisheries Departnent has
certified 6 companies to export fish towards the EU following the Hazard Critical Control Point
(HACCP) regulationl'. The processing is essentially cleaning and making filets, with
supplementary freezing. Emissions are therefore purely an organic load. There is no quantitative
information on the emissions coming from the 6 fishery processing plants.
Some of the fishery processing plants used to have own trawling vessels in operation. Nowadays,
they are completely dependant on the catches brought in by fisherman not connected to the
processing plants.
9 foreign fishernan (f.i. Senegalese) who are sedentary in The Gambia are considered as Gambians
10 Mr. A.N. Mendy of the Dept. Of Fisheries spoke about 5 processing plants certified for export
SECTIONA - PART II: DESCRIPTiON OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                      111



7.6.   Aquaculture
In the late '80's, a huge commercial aquaculture investment was installed in The Gambia as a
joint venture between a Norwegian company with a Gambian counterpart, SCAN-Gambia. A
foreign shrimp, tiger prawn (Peneaus monodon) was introduced as the species to be cultured. A
hatchery was built along the seaside (at Saniang Point) and 1000 ha of mainly mangrove was
cleared for culture at Pirang, SE from Yundum Airport and near the Gambian river. However, in
'92, the investment ran in financial troubles and stopped operating. Peneaus monodon escaped
however and can still be found in the catches of the fisherman in the Gambia River. This was the
only aquaculture operation in The Gambia.
The Fisheries Department is presently conducting a pilot aquaculture project in the Central River
Division. This is in the form of a research initiative in fish culture using riverine fishes in two
half hectare ponds.
7.7.   Oyster harvesting
Oysters are a specific catch, heavily related to the mangrove, as oysters grow on the mangrove
roots. The harvesting is done by shopping the oysters from the roots. However, in many cases,
the roots are themselves chopped so that the oysters can easily be detached from it"1. This not
only damages the mangrove, but also reduces the available roots for seedling oysters to attach to.
The Tanbi wetland complex is heavily encroached on the whole borderside, in part by oyster
harvesting. The pressure on the oysters can also be indicated through the main oyster size which
is found to be generally small around Banjul area as they have not the time to grow.
Most marine areas have besides the natural protection fimction other relevant functions, such as
coastal protection and partly fish spawning.
" Oysters are partly found under the water when harvesting, the fishing people can more easily be injured when not
chopping the root - on the other hand, oysters are sold in Banjul at 4 pm when people return home, so oysters need
to be harvested on the most efficient way to make them prepared at that time

8.  LAND USE AND LANDSCAPE - TOURISM
8.1. Introduction
The most recent land use plan and land use (survey) maps available have been consulted before
checking the present situation in the field:
o  Land use Plan 2000 (Dept. of Physical Planning, 1988, revised draft 1990): this is the map
component of the Greater Banjul Land use Masterplan12 , first commissioned in 1983.
Although a new revision started as early as 1995, the revised draft of 1990 still serves as the
only and most recent official planning tool upto now (Manneh, 1999; Abdoulie Manneh,
personal communication).
OL Land cover map: land use map drawn in 1997 and based on a survey an aerial photos of
1993 - available at the GIS centre of the NEA.(ArcView format).
A field reconnaissance trip has been organised by the consultant on 28 and 29 June 2000 ( see
alsofigure 8.2./1 for photographs taken during these field visits).
8.2.   Land use, Landscape and scenery
8.2.1.  Coastal sites (Bund Road and Denton Bridge)
Both sites are part of the proposed Raamsar site "Tanbi Wetland Complex". As stated in the recent
Ramsar-study (DPWM & The Ramsar Bureau, 1997), the proximity to the major urban centers of
Banjul, Serekunda and Bakau results in considerable pressures on the area. The area comprising the
two proposed sites is subject to agricultural and horticultural activity on the landward side. Along
the Banjul-Serekunda highway industrial development is rather important.
Furthermore, it is reasonable to expect that Banjul City will gradually expand into the polder
between Bund Road and the city, given the unmet demand for land in Banjul City.
12 also referred to as Greater Banjul Area Physical Development Plan (1990) or Greater Banjul Area Land use Plan
2000
SECTION A - PAR ni: DEsCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONmENr                    113



BUND ROAD SITE
Land use categories according to the Land use Plan 2000:
i swamp as part of S.A.5. (Banjul Bund Polder special area)
o open land (gardening, scrub)
o other areas: water (main channels linked to the pumping station)
o utility services: the Nawec station at the corner of Bund Rd. and highway
A zone with existing warehouses, along the western side of Bund Rd. and opposite the
project site, is indicated as industrial (factory).
From the roof of the pumping station on Bund Rd. one has an excellent view over the total Bund
Polder area.
To the Southeast of the station (and the creek) the area has conserved more natural characteristics
than to the west. Salt marsh and dense mangrove constitute an area indicated as "bird sanctuary" on
the location map drawn by IDI, 2000 (see Part I, Figure 3./4) - near the creek however, this part of
the polder presents an open, barren area without typical vegetation.
The northwestern part of the polder (picture 1 -figure 8.2./1.) shows a much larger dry area
some attempts to horticulture there, some fruit trees and also much rubbish scattered over a large
area. The TGP project site proper has the following main characten'stics:
-  NAWEC station (corner Bund Rd. and highway). Double electric line from the station
towards Banjul city - within the project bounderies and parallel to Serrekunda-Banjul
highway; a single one along Bund Rd. towards Port and Half Die residential area.
-  A waste tip near Nawec and extending more than 200 m along Bund
Rd.(picture 2). Nearest to NAWEC the waste is mainly sand, stone and
concrete: from the city council (abandoned project to fill up the mudland for a
car park). Highest present level at least 3 m above original soil level. The rest
of the (illegal, unauthorized) waste tip mainly contains household waste and
old cars. Between Bund Rd. and garbage: dense stretch of low trees (= visual
buffer) and a drainage channel with heavily polluted black water.
-  The central part of the project site shows a rather open area with grassfield and
scrub, mangrove fringe along the highway. Mangrove trees are lining the
eastemnost fork of the original bolong system in this part of the polder.

- The eastern part of the project site, beyond the mangrove remnants and
nearest to the city limits, consists of open land (barren flat with standing
water) - during the field visit a lot of dumped debris was observed in this part
(pictures 3 and 4, figure 8.2./1).
DENTON BRIDGE SITE
According to the Land use Plan 2000, the strip along the Banjul highway is zoned for a
combination of services, industrial development, hotels and institutions.
One of the purposes of the field visit was to detect a suitable place for zoning an industrial area
with more or less the same surface (34 ha) as the one at Bund Road.
Between a new ELF petrol station and Denton Bridge, a dry, sandy strip could be taken into
consideration for FTG development (see picture 5 ):
- This area has been estimated 100 m x 500 m = 50,000m2 = 5 hectares . The
width of 100 m comprises the strip of dry land (old dune) between
Serrekunda-Banjul highway and the coastal swamp zone that is present at this
point - on this dry land a few scattered trees can be observed: Baobab and
some solitary coconut palm trees.
- An electricity line crosses the site from near the highway at Denton Bridge
diagonally towards a fish processing plant located just west of the ELF station.
- Further towards the shoreline the coastal strip consists of young dunes, a
lagoon, some mangrove swamp and mudflats.
Further to the west and all the way till the junction Old Cape Road - highway, very dense
mangrove swamp reaches the road site.
From these observations the following rough conclusions seem obvious for Denton Bridge site:
(a) physical limitations exist for construction between the Bridge and ELF petrol station (34
hectares needed, only 5 hectares available) , and
(c) strong environmental limitations for development of the coastal zone to the west of Elf station.

The Airport site is a uniform piece of woodland, described in chapter 6.2.2.3., without any
buildings (see picture 6 infigure 8.2./i).
The two other sites (Sukuta, Kuloro) show a mix of open agricultural land, farms, wooded
savanna and open scrub savanna, and more or less developed settlements with houses and
gardens for horticulture. Picture 7 shows a typical view of the flat savanna land at Sukuta site. A
more detailed description of the settlement pattern, which is an important land use component at
Sukuta in particular, is given in chapter 10.
Both the Sukuta and Kuloro sites are located nearby a Forest Park: Salagi FP, respectively
Furyuar FP. The Sukuta site has been cut in two unequal parts by the recently constructed coastal
highway to Yundum airport.
8.2.3. Mandinari area
In addition to the description of present land use at the 5 nominated FEZ sites, special attention
is given to this area in order to assess the environmental feasibility of a trade interface between
the Gambia River and the Airport.
On the 10th of July 2000 a site visit was done to Mandinari village and coastal area, the mangrove
area at Kunkujang Jattaya and Kubuni (Mandina mangrove).
Road leading to Mandinari coast
There is a good road to the beach (see Ap,endix 5 - picture 1), this is now frequently used by
trucks who transport sand from the sand mine. Parts of the road from Lamin towards Mandinari
were still flooded when visiting Mandinari and could only be reached by 4W drive, this may
cause serious problems in rainy season (part of the reason is the shallow groundwater level).
Picture 2 shows the old sand dune before the fishing harbour, this old sand dune is situated left of
the sand mine and is still untouched, most probably because it is the natural way to the sea for
the fisherman, the old sand dune is rather a flat elevation with very little vegetation, typical
wading birds could be seen around, there are casual strips of land at the border of the sand dune
which are used for cultivation.
Mandinari fishing harbour
Picture 3 gives a view on the harbour of Mandinari. It is more a natural bay then a harbour.
There are no wooden or any other artifical structures providing access on land, the harbour is
only a not too large naturally sheltered bay, that can easily be reached from land crossing the flat
old sand dune. The bay itself is a small interruption of about 50m of the mangrove belt which
connects the mangrove from the proposed RAMSAR site of the Tanbi wetland with the
mangrove surrounding the Mandina Bolon. The mangrove belt from both sides are however only
small lints on the beach. The bay is densely populated with mangrove crabs (see picture 4).
SECTIONA - PARTII: DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINEENVIRONMENT                   120



About 80 typical fishing canoes were counted (see picture 3), some of them in bad state. One
fishing family was met on the beach, they were not from Mandinari but came from Barra and
were of Senegalese origin. Lateron, speaking with the elderly people in Mandinari, it was
understood that most fisherman are from Senegalese origin who settled in Mandinari (as
confirned in statistics). About 20 fisherman would be out in sea (noon), however, as it was the
beginning of the rainy season, a lot of the fisher-man left Mandinari to go fishing downstream the
Gambia River. In the dry season, over 100 fisherman would be at Mandinari itself Only 2 or 3 of
the original Mandika people at Mandinari would be active as fisherman.
Mandinari sand quarry
The sand quarry was visited. They only recently started mining the old sand dunes due to the
banning of sand mining in southern coastal sites along the Atlantic Ocean.The size of the quarry
is contradictory to the fact that this would be a temporary site and most probably not anymore in
use (comments from DPWM). Picture 5 shows a part of the sand quarry with a depth of about 4
meters in the old sand dune. 'This quarry is situated to the right of the fishing harbour of
Mandinari. The sand mine is at least 5 times the size of the quarry in the picture, and about 15
workers were busy loading a lorry. From the state of the road leading to the sand quarry, it can
be assumed that there are daily passages of trucks.
Impacts of sand mining at Mandinari
The bay at Mandinari used by the fishing people creates a small intersection between the linear
mangrove belt from Tanbi and Mandina. Discussions with DPWM revealed the importance of
this area as corridor function for f.i. larger bush mammals. The corridor is not only the mangrove
belt itself but also the more open area formed by old sand dunes (with vegetation) and mud slicks
(open, less vegetation). The sand quarry and its size create a significant irnpact on different
levels, possible change of groundwater profile with potential impact of saltwater intrusion
towards the hinterland, destruction of the vegetation on the sand dune, and the creation of a
significant barrier towards the crossing of fauna from the one mangrove to the other. Research
has shown that potential positive effects may arise for the avifauna. Most older sand quarries
become largely populated by birds. However, taking in view the main ecological function of this
area as a corridor for wildlife between the two mangrove areas, the sand quarry acts mainly as a
barrier. Supplementary, there is increased transport from trucks (formerly absent in this area).
Although the impact cannot be quantified due to shortage of data on fauna using this corridor,
the impact is seen as significant from an ecological point of view. It must be mentioned that
mitigation measures have been proposed to restore the site after excavation. However, no plans
for such restoration exist. The use of it can also be questioned (with what materials ? etc.).
SECTION A - PARTII: DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                   121



Mandina mangrove
At Kunkujang Jattaya, the mangrove was visited from the land side. As it was eb-tide, it was
possible to walk in the mangrove up to a canal very near the sea itself . A dike runs over 1 km
along the Kunkujang Jattaya village, separating the rice paddies from the mangrove (preventing
salt water intrusion) (see picture 6). The mangrove bordering the dike has disappeared along the
whole distance of the dike over a width of about 10 m, suggesting encroachment of the
mangrove. Otherwise, the mangrove is densily vegetated, with numerous air roots on the bottom.
8.3.   Tourism
All the nominated sites for FEZ development lie outside the Tourist Development Area (Land
use Plan 2000) which controls developments and activities within a strip along the Atlantic coast
from Kotu stream (north) and Allahein river (south). The National Policy for Tourism
Development (Ministry of Tourism and Culture, 1995) to a large extent focusses only on
developments in this TDA area.
However, the Plan mentions possibilities for diversification with alternative forms of tourism
such as opening-up localities where there are "cultural antiquities, nature parks, sacred pools and
bird sanctuaries".
When considering the TGP sites, this is particularly relevant with regard to Tanbi Wetland
Complex. In fact, the mangroves currently do attract a substantial number of tourists. Sightseeing
is part of the attraction but, more important, various parts of Tanbi wetland have long being
recognized as great birding sites . Both resident and foreign bird-watchers have been visiting the
area for several decades. Denton Bridge /Oyster Creek serve as a starting point for visiting the
bolongs by boat, so that this location may rightly be cited as a key site for eco-tourism
infrastructure .
In relation to this issue, it is important to remark that bird-watchers indicate 'Bund Road' as one
of their favourite spots. In fact, the road offers excellent opportunities to observe wildlife in the
pristine mangrove forest to the west of the road - the degraded Polder area between the road and
Banjul city is less attractive in this respect.
SECTION A - PARTII: DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMExT                    122



19-   ARCHEOLOGICAL, HISTORICAL AND CULTURAL VALUESI
Bund Road
There are no places of archeological, historical or cultural importance on the Bund Road. Until the
road was built after independence the area was a large expanse of water providing a variety of fish.
Only a few shell mounds show any sign of human activity and these would cease to exist with the
development of the site as a FEZ.
Denton Bidge
Except for a few scattered shell mounds, there are no sites of archaeological, historical and
cultural importance and as such the establishment of the FEZ will not be affected by the nation's
cultural values.
East of the bridge and Oyster Creek, several graveyards are subject to extremely severe coastal
erosion but these important places are situated well outside the proposed FEZ area.
Sukuta
With the exception of Nema Kunku, which has a sacred groove where circumcision and
initiation are performed, the FEZ and the surrounding villages do not have places of
archaeological, historical and cultural importance.
At the Airport and Kuloro sites no places of particular interest have been deternined .
SECTIONA - PARTII: DESCRIPTIONOF THE BASELINE ENVIRONmENT                    123



10.  HuMAN AND SOCIO-ECONOMIC ENVIRONMENT
10.1. Methodology of data collection
After collecting existing data from relevant reports, all five nominated sites have been visited.
For the 'terrestrial' sites (Airport, Sukuta and Kuloro), interviews with village heads of the
concerned communities were included in the field investigations. No regular settlements exist at
Bund Road or Denton Bridge.
10.2. Socio-economic conditions and activities at the nominated sites
10.2.1. Bund Road Area, Banjul
10.2.1.2.  Settlement - economic activities
The 135 hectares of the area that is today known as the Bund Road has been identified as a possible
site for the FEZ and is located on Banjul, the only habitable island on the estuary of the River
Gambia. This Island is almost surrounded by mangrove swamps, except for the stretch of sandy
land facing the Atlantic Ocean. 'The Bund Road area is mostly water logged and has experienced
constant flooding especially during the rainy season.
A road was build to connect Half Die area to the Serre Kunda Highway thus helping to ease the
flow of traffic on the highway itself. This road leads directly to the ports enabling the
transportation of goods from the ports to the Greater Banjul and beyond.
A pumping station was installed on the west side of the road to reduce the level of water on the
area between the road and the areas popularly known as Lasso Wharf and Crab Island. This
activity resulted in the formnation of patches of dry cultivable land. However the flooding of the
area recurred after the breakdown of the pumps.
The mangroves swamps coupled up with the poor soil that is usually inundated has made human
settlement impossible. Instead a handful of people from Banjul who have been hard pressed to
engage in farming have over the years cultivated the existing patches of dry land during the rainy
season and thus derive some economic gains from the harvests. They have been joined by a
handful of Jola squatters who burn and sell oysters collected from the nearby mangroves. This
activity is carried out only during the dry season. On the left-hand side there were exactly 4
squatters at the time of the investigation. Presently, that is during the rainy season, the squatters
SECTIONA - PARTII: DEScRIPTIONOF rHE BASELINE ENvIRONMENT                      124



have left and there are two farmers cultivating corn and coos who are actually working on land
they do not own.
The Bund Road area has experienced the sewage inflows from the city and this has encouraged
the breeding of shellfish and worm, which in turn are fed on by various species of birds. Thus it
has became a sanctuary for many bird. Tourism has been given a boost because many bird
watchers have come to the Bund Road area specifically to watch these vertebrates of varied sizes
and plumes of different colours.
There are no buildings on the site. On the other side of the road there are actually 4 completed,
operational warehouses and 3 uncompleted ones, an indication that reclamation and the building
of structures are feasible with the right technology and engineering know how.
10.2.1.3.Property - development plans
It is believed that the Bund Road area is state land with plans by the Banjul City Council to
make a car park there to help ease the traffic congestion in Banjul especially during working
hours. Although attempts were made by this institution to reclaim the land by tipping stone and
gravel on the site, this activity seems to have ceased. Today part of this site is being used as a
refuge dump. Indeed the indiscriminate dumnping of filling has made the site even more
unsightly.
The Bund Road is the only remaining open land that could be made serviceable to the people of the
island of Banjul and the greater Banjul area, that is if the right ingredients are applied. Such
ingredients would include the filling up of the area with lateritic soils to a higher lever and draining
the land of its waterlogged nature.
Another objective has been to preserve the habitat as one of the promotional tools of tourism.
This objective has been in variance with other developmental plans such as those of FEZ and
that of the Banjul City Council of building a car park
10.2.1.4. Water and power supply
Amenities such as water and electricity are already provided by NAWEC, with 2 one million
gallons water storage tanks, located about 1,500 metres from the Bund Road and the Nawec sub-
station located on the edge of the site on the junction of the Bund Road - Serre Kunda highway.
However the critical situation of electricity nation wide would necessitate alternate arrangement for
the provision of reliable supply for FEZ in the future.
SECTIONA - PARTII: DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                  125



10.2.2. Denton Bridge Area
10.2.2.1 Settlement - economic activities
The site extends from The Denton Bridge to the Old Cape Road junction spanning about 60
hectares. Coming from Banjul, the width of the site stretches from the Serrekunda-Banjul Highway
to the sea. The site includes the stretch of land that is included in the proposed RAMSAR site
(Tanbi Wetland Complex) is covered with swamps, creeks and lagoons due to the tidal nature of the
sea. Covered extensively with mangrove swamps, the wetness of the low laying land is further
increased during the rainy season when there are heavy down pours, for which the coastal areas are
well known. Thus not only fish but also a variety of birds find haven on this habitat that is natural to
them.
The nature of the area as described above has rendered it unsuitable for any form of human
settlement.
However a handful of people do engage in farming cultivating coos, groundnuts and rice during
the rainy season. . Although both men and women carry out farming activities only the women
embark on rice cultivation at the height of the rainy season during which time the wetness of the
land is conducive for the cultivation of this crop. During the dry season, a handful of Jola
women (squatters) burn and sell oysters collected from the mangroves located on the site and the
opposite side where the thick mangrove provide a breading place for oysters. Firewood is also
collected from the area making it possible for these squatters to burn the oysters and cook their
meals. Indeed the numerous shell mounds found few metres from the Banjul-Serrekunda
Highway are clear indications of the activities of these Jola women
Although this part of coast is adversely affected by erosion, a few of the local inhabitants are fully
engaged infishing to gain their livelihood.
Tourists take regular trips from the adjacent areas of Denton Bridge to engage in sport fishing, bird
watching and pleasure trips, meandering through and viewing the numerous islands in the area.
These activities help to promote tourism.
There are three fish processing plants on the site. The one near the Denton Bridge Police Station
is not functional. Fishing is usually done out in the ocean and the majority of their catches are
exported to overseas though they sell some of the fish and shrimps to the local people. The
building of these structures were made possible by reclaiming the land with laterite and gravel.
There are access roads from the main road to the places where the fish plants are located. There
are additional temporary structures engaged in some of the above mentioned activities. There is
also the recently built Elf Petrol station, which is placed on the Denton Bridge site, and is of
great help to motorists who could easily purchase fuel while plying the Serrekunda -Banjul
Highway going into Serrekunda and Bakau. This petrol station was made possible by reclaiming
the land.
SECTIONA - PARTI1: DESCRIPTiON OF 7HEBASELINEENVIRONMENT                    126



10.2.2.2.Property
This stretch of approximately 60 hectares is owned by the state though the area along the south-west
of Oyster Creek is controlled by The Gambia Ports Authority who claim that much occupancy by
most of the people and businesses are illegal. However the areas occupied by the fish processing
plant and about 20 hectares of the area occupied by the former Scan Gambia Company have been
leased.
10.2.2.3. Water and power supply
Utilities such as water and electricity are supplied by NAWEC from the water storage tanks on the
Serrekunda - Banjul Highway and the electricity by the sub-station located on the Bund Road
Serrekunda -Banjul Highway junction respectively. However the fish processing plants have stand-
by generators to enable them to cope with the erratic power cuts of NAWEC.
10.2.3. Airport Site (East of Airport)
10.2.3.1.Settlement - economic activities
Although the site has savanna-like features, that are flat land covered with grass, bushes, shrubs
and tall trees, there are no settlements. This is probably because the area is owned by GCAA.
However, people from nearby villages carry out cultivation of groundnuts and coos during the
rainy season. Women from nearby Yundum also collectfirewood from the site and beyond.
The existing airport has provided employment for people from the satellite villages of Yundum,
Lamin, Mandinari and Makumbaya. A handful of qualified and unqualified personnel from Banjul,
Greater Banjul area and Brikama are also employed thus enhancing, maintaining and improving the
services of the airport. The GCAA, various airlines and tour operators carry out on-the-job and
overseas training.The land on the airport area has many building including such as the modem
Banjul International building, Radville storage house, customs etc, an.indication that the land would
be able to sustain similar or even bigger construction thus enhancing the development of the area for
the FEZ.
10.2.3.2. Property - development plans (Gambia Civil Aviation Authority)
The total area of this site is approximately 400 hectares of land adjacent the present Airport, of
which some 200 hectares off the present airport access road, will be put into operation for the
FEZ. This fenced land owned by the GCAA (Gambia Civil Aviation Authority) was identified
on the firm belief that it would be more economically viable in promoting the aspirations and
objectives of the FEZ. This justified the placing of the 200 hectares of land as part of the master
plan for the development of the airport area to which a buffer zone will be added on all sides.
SECTION A - PAR TII: DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                  127



The master plan also includes 45 hectares for a REDZ (Real Estate Development Zone)
providing modem facilities such as a hotel, shopping mall and offices. The building of ware
houses and hangers and new access roads are among the added facilities that will be provided by
the GCAA. The proposed site incorporates the Radville cold storage, where the harvests from
theit farms are sorted and exported to other European countries notably Britain
10.23.3. Water and power supply
NAWEC is presently responsible for supply of electricity to airport, which is given top priority
ensuring twenty-four hours supplies of this facility. This is essential for the safe arrival and
taking off of airplanes on and from the runway and the servicing and convenience of passengers.
NAWEC is also responsible for the supply of water. The airport building also has modem toilets
with septic tanks and suck ways.
10.2.3.4.Roads
Presently the roads leading to the airport are in fairly good condition. The road between Banjul
and West Field Junction is a dual carriageway. It converges into a single lane after this junction.
Recently this road was resurfaced and patched thus easing the nightmare of travelling on the
former pot hole road to the airport. However there are plans to replace this single carriageway
with a dual one to the airport and beyond to Mandinaba.
There is also the near completed Coastal Highway from Kololi via Sukuta to Yundum where the
airport is located. This road will ease the traffic congestion in Serre Kunda and the Brikama
Highway as it would now cater for residence from Sukuta, Kotu, Kololi and other surrounding
villages. These roads will ensure the easy flow of passengers and goods to and from the airport
thus improving trade. However the other roads on the site and surrounding are still in poor state
and this situation is worsened during the rainy season when many of them are impassable for
vehicles and people
10.2.3.5.Attitude towards the project
On the whole many people welcome the idea of developing this part of the airport as a FEZ as
this would create employment not only for the people from the surrounding villages but the
country as a whole.
SECTiONA - PAR TII: DESCRIPTION OF rHEIBASELINEENVIRONMENT                  128



10.2.4. Kuloro
10.2.4. .Settlement - economic activities
Kuloro site, approximately 10 square kilometres long, is located in Kombo East, east of the town
of Brikama. The land is flat levelled and well drained, and covered with grass, shrub, bushes and
few scattered tall trees. Indeed a typical semi arid savanna type feature suitable for farming and
grazing.
There are many villages that would be affected by the Kuloro site. The most popular are Kuloro
proper whose main inhabitants were originally settled in the village of Tugina close by the river
and who had resettled on the present village about 40 years ago to take advantage of the activity
of the road. Today the village is found on both sides of the main south bank road going to the
provinces. Tubakuta, which is approximate 150 metres from the site, is west of the Furuyar
Forest Park. The Radville Farm that has an area of about 200 hectares is located in this village.
Of equal importance is the village of Mandinaba, which like Kuloro is on both sides of the main
road going to the provinces.
Buildings/Population
Although there are a handful of solid cement buildings, such as mosques and shops in these
villages about 80% of the houses are built of mud blocks.
The populations of these villages are relatively small with compounds ranging 270 for Kuloro to
150 for Mandinaba and Tubakuta respectively. Thus Kuloro has a population of approximately
6000 inhabitants. The households of these villages are invariably large, with families being Muslims
and polygamous. Some families have as many as 15 to 20 children especially when the parents have
passed 60 years. The males who indeed form the majority, head about 99% of these households.
The extended family system operates in these villages.
Occupation
Farming is the main occupation of the inhabitants of these villages and is done mainly during the
rainy season, which is from June to October. The males engage mainly in the cultivation of
groundnuts though coos is also grown. Some also engage in horticulture farming. Women also
take active part in farming, producing mainly rice and coos. Recently a handful of women have
also engaged in growing sesame.
The majority of the people engage in horticulture and animal rearing-claiming that the income
from groundnut cultivation is insufficient or not forthcoming and therefore needs to be
supplemented. Women from these villages engage in vegetable gardening and petty trading that
enables them to sell the produce from their gardens.
SECTION A - PARTII: DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONmENT                 129



The incomes derive from farming ranges from D6000 a year to DI 000 depending of the size of
the farm and the success of the harvest. Some of the inhabitants are employed on full or part time
basis on the 200 hectares Radville Farm though they complain that much of their farm land had
been taken up and that salaries on the farm are low.
10.2.4.2. Property
The land that was communal compnrses of farmland and settlements, which were owned by the
original settlers, who have passed down their rights of ownership to their descendants.
Land in the Proposed Project Area is 70%-80% owned by the village Madina Ba. Although
settlements are moving towards the area, no compound has as yet been allocated within the area.
Growth of the village has been gradual. Agricultural plots are never rented or sold , but are
instead "loaned-out", and must be retumed at the end of the season, although some families have
borrowed the same plots for years. Land disputes are rare and land sales are infrequent. People
from Banjul and Serrekunda seeking land is not a common occurrence.
Madina Ba is mainly a farming community, with men cultivating upland crops, mainly
groundnuts, while women are engaged in vegetable and rice production. Land is as yet not a
constraint, as additional land is reserved for agriculture, on the other side of the road towards the
river.
The rest of the land in the Proposed Project Area belongs to the village of Tubakuta, which is
mainly a farming village.
10.2.4.3.Education and health
Presently Tubakuta and Kulora have only one Lower Basic school each though these villages
and Mandinaba have nursery schools. However, there are Arabic schools in all villages and most
people and literate in the Arabic language.
Only a handful of the people of these villages have been exposed to westem type education with
a handful obtaining Lower Basic level. Higher levels like Upper Basic or Senior Secondary are
obtained from bigger towns like Brikama or Banjul.
These villages do not have Health Centres. They are visited regularly by a team of trained nurses
and drugs are dispensed to them. The Primary Health Care system sometimes helps them to
improve their health. However many turn to traditional healers and Marabout when they are very
sick and cannot find cure from the western type medicine. They also consult these Marabouts
when they believe that witches or evil spirits affects them.
SECTIONA - PARTII: DEScRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVRONMENT                   130



10.2.4.4. Water andpower supply
The people of these villages rely on wells to get drinking water and for domestic work. While the
village of Kuloro has hand pump wells provided by Caritas, Tubakuta has only one and Mandinabo
none. The people of Toubakuta complain that they are unable to get water from their locally dug
wells because the Radville Farm has six boreholes thereby distancing the water table..
There is no electricity in these villages though Kuloro was given a solar panel by EDF
(European Development Fund). Thus the inhabitants have to depend on other source of energy
such as kerosene and firewood.
10.2..4.5. Attitude towards the project
The inhabitants of these villages would welcome development project on the site. Indeed they
claim that they could allocate or sell land which could be used to set up enterprises and industries
that would create employment opportunities and improve their lifestyle. However some of the
villagers expressed concern that some developments might not be beneficial to the people in the
location.
10.2.5. Sukuta Site (East of Salagi Park)
10.2.5.1.Settlement - economic activities
The land is flat and well drained and has shrubs, bushes and trees in areas that are not cleared for
farming or horticultural gardens.
The most successful of these J^arns is the 85 hectares Radville farm that employs about 500
people from the villages of Nema Kunku, Sukuta, Old Yundum, Serre Kunda and other nearby
villages and towns.
The closeness of the site to the Airport makes it ideal for a FEZ. Moreover the newly constructed
Coastal Road that traverses the site will help to link and speed up the journey from the hotels
and residences from places like Kololi, Kuto and Fajara. This road took a large chunk of the
farmland that belonged to various individuals including Sukuta, sometimes dividing them in two.
There have been many hue and cries about compensation for farmland taken to make the new
road possible.
One of the first residents on the project site settled here only about twelve to fifteen years ago
with more recent owners coming in four to two years later. Many of the owners of these plots of
land are bureaucrats and businessmen still resident in places like Banjul, Greater Banjul, Western
Division and beyond. They often build small corrugated houses for their watchmen or caretakers
who look after the plots, watering the young plants on their farms especially during the dry
season. Today a few owners have constructed bigger stone houses on their farms.
SECTION A - PAR I: DESCRIPTION OF 2HE BASELINE ENVIRONmENT                  131



Buildings/Population
There are few solid structures on the site the most important being the Sukuta Treatment Plant,
Water Production Unit.
Recently more solid cement houses are being constructed in the area.
The population on the site is rather small with only one family having about 15 children and over 30
dependents. The other families on the sites have fewer children and dependents. However the
surrounding villages of Nema Kunku, Old Yundum and Sukuta are more populated with many
polygamous families having many more children. The youths and children outnumber the adults.
Nema Kunku for example has as many as 2700 households though the exact population of the
village is not known. These villages continue to attract people who want to move away from Banjul
and congested towns in the Greater Banjul area like Serre Kunda.
Occupation
The main occupation of the people on the site and surrounding villages is farming. Groundnut is
the most important crop being cultivated followed by coos and corn.
Horticulture is equally popular in this area and some have concentrated on mangoes production.
Some of the tree on the orchard are still young and might take about two years to mature.
Women especially from Sukuta engage in vegetable gardening. Although most of the products
are sold in the market in Serre Kunda some of them do cater for the hotels. One or two women
kafos have also engaged in tie and dye.
Most people also take up animal rearing, business or petty trading to enhance the incomes
derived from farming which sometimes exceeded D7000 depending on the size of the farn and
successful harvests of groundnuts, corn and coos. The sales of horticulture products have helped
to improve farmers' lives and ranges from D3500 to D600 a year. A handful of the inhabitants
from the surrounding villages have acquired up to senior secondary education and are therefore
employed as junior civil servants and in the hotel industry. Nevertheless many of them do take
up farming to help increase their incomes. The people on the site and surrounding villages do not
engage in fishing because they are far from the fishing villages
I 0.2.5.2. Property
Although the exact expanse of the site is not stipulated it is located in Sukuta, east of the Salagi
Forest Park and incorporates about 80% of the farmland and residential property of people from
Sukuta, Nema Kunku and Old Yundum. Indeed some of the property though bought from
individuals had been used as farmland for many generations. Thus the land on the project site
belongs to various individuals who acquired it by purchasing it from the owners or the alkalos.
SECTION A - PARTII: DEScpjpIrN OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMEAT                  132



Only one farmer whose original farn was divided into two unequal halves now lives on the
bigger part of his farm with his family. Land in this area of Kombo is comparatively more
expensive. Because of the newly completed Kombo Coastal Road, people would ask for no less
than D60,000 for approximately 40 by 40 metres plot of land, though this would have cost far
less five to ten years ago, before the road was constructed.
In Appendix 4.4. additional, more detailed information is given conceming land occupancy and
current land prices in the proposed project area which is bordered by Salagi Forest Park, Sukuta
village, Ker Dobaly, Madina Sey Kunda, Old Yundum and Jarbang.
10.2.5.3. Education and health
The project site has no schools and the inhabitants depend on those on surrounding villages.
Only Sukuta has an Upper Basic school. The other villages have only nursery, a Lower Basic
and Arabic schools.
Today, many more people send their children to western typed school, though Arabic/Islamic
schools also known as daras remain popular. About 90% of the population Gambian population
are Muslim though there are a handful of Christians in the surrounding villages. This indeed
justifies the high percentage of people who are literate in the Arabic Language.
The site and surrounding villages can boast of only one government health centre which is
located in Sukuta. The rest of the villages are visited regularly by teams of health personnel.
Drugs are administered by these teams and are usually affordable for villagers. Nema Kunku has
a private clinic but most times drugs expensive for the people. Cases of emergency or very
serious illnesses are referred to the health centres in Faji Kunda, Serre Kunda or the Royal
Victoria Hospital. The majority of the inhabitants still consult traditional healers especially
Marabouts when they feel they are affected by evil spirits or are not satisfied with the western
typed treatment.
Despite the existing farmlands and settlements on the site and villages there are many facilities
that needs to be improved on or put in place. These include telephones, water, electricity, health
centres, public toilets, car parks, community centres and other facilities that should help promote
a healthy living.
10.2.5.4. Water and power supply
The existing Sukuta Treatment Plant, Water Production Unit, has huge underground pipes
supplying many of the surrounding big towns with water. Although Sukuta and Nema Kunku do
have some standpipes, the inhabitants on the site and vicinity have not yet benefited from this
facility and have to rely on wells, which do not provide enough water because of NAWEC's
SECTIONA - PARTII: DESCRiPrToN OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                  133



boreholes and those of the Radville Farm that have lowered the water table. The latter has also
provided about four boreholes on the farm at Nema Kunku to ensure the availability of water for
the plants and crops during the dry season. One of the orchard owners on the FEZ site has
addressed this problem of water scarcity during the dry season, by having daily supplies of water
from tankers. The water is poured in barrels and women from the surrounding villages are hired
to water the up growing cashew plants.
Although there is the Sukuta Treatment Plant, Water Production Unit on the site that does have
electricity, the site and some of the villages have none. However Radville Farm, and parts of
Nema Kunku and Sukuta are supplied this much needed energy by NAWEC. The erratic power
cuts has caused Radville Farm to have a standby generator.
I 0.2.5.5. Attitude towards the project
While some people on the FEZ site and neighbouring villages welcome development as a
possible means of providing better job opportunities and improved life style, many fear that new
projects would deprive them of their farmland and property, cause pollution and overpopulate
the area with new migrants.
10.3. Synthesis of socio-economic conditions in the study area
The total study area is within Banjul (the Bund Road and Denton Bridge sites) and the pen'-urban
area of Banjul (Yundum, Sukuta and Kuloro sites).13  The line running from Cape Point south
through Serrekunda to Wellingara and Ker Dobaly marks an area of dense urban settlement and
commerce. The village of Madina Sey Kunda at the border with Ker Dobaly (and Old Yundum
several further kilometers south), marks the beginning of a very dynamic band of rapid
population settlement, sprawl of semi-finished cement foundations and newly finished
compounds, and intense land competition between housing and farming. From Madina Sey
Kunda, there are about 4.5 km of rural landscape (southwest by air) to Jabang, and 6.0 km
(southeast by air) to Yundum Airport. Between these extremes is a continuum spanning different
degree of market access and land scarcity. At one end is Madina Sey Kunda with relatively high
resident/land ratios, rapid population settlement, and close proximity to urban markets. At the
opposite end is Jabang with relatively low resident/land ratios and far proximity from urban
markets. The Yundum Airport area is between the two extremes. A further 10 km (southeast by
13 The peri-urban area comprises an area roughly triangular in shape extending from Cape Point (the northermnost
point and urban center) to Sanyang village in the southwest, amd to Pirang village in the south east, excluding
Serrekunda).
SECTION A - PARTI.' DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE ENVIRONMENT                  134



air) from Yundum Airport is Kuloro, which is even more rural than Jabang, and therefore has the
lowest resident/land ratio.
We note that Banjul and Serrekunda are "saturated" in this respect, and it is the combination of
high rents and land scarcity in those areas are driving urbanites to seek land in the peri-urban
villages, while migrants from up-river and abroad, especially young males, are seeking land in
peri-urban area for housing, wage employment, and business opportunities.
Thus, rural areas in the urban fringe are experiencing rapid settlement, and households are
experiencing considerable tenure insecurity stemming from land holding groups reclaiming land
for gift or sale to newcomers. Customary mechanisms of land allocation by the alkalos in
villages are giving way to a robust market of land rentals, purchases and sales, mainly of
residential property, in rural and urban areas. The physical urban frontier is expanding rapidly,
and through the land market, is rapidly transforming agricultural lands into sprawling
compounds, and villages into suburbs. Residential property or land for compound is bought and
sold in Medina Sey Kunda, less so in the area east of Yundum Airport, and not at all in Kuloro.
There is almost no active land market in Banjul (and Serrekunda).
Since the drought of the 1970s and 1 980s, and the decline of the groundnut industry, families in
the peri-urban area have placed increasing emphasis on vegetable production, (and on cereals,
such as groundnuts, millet and maize). Casual workers, mainly from up-river and the Southern
Senegal seek employment for building fencing, digging wells, gardening, and work on
commercial farms. Women are primarily responsible for growing and marketing the vegetables.
They report labor constraints in water lifting, cultivation and harvesting, but lack of capital for
irrigation wells and fencing appears to be the main factor constraining private expansion of
garden schemes in the peri-urban area. Expanding the size of village schemes would require
additional land which is held by men, and capital for irrigation which is scarce.
The impact of medium- and large-scale commercial farms on labor absorption and employment
is an important policy concern of the government. Horticultural crops, being relatively labor
intensive would theoretically represent an ideal combination for increasing labor employment on
smaller firms and households in the peri-urban area. However there appears to be a low rate of
commercial employment, as a sizeable number of workers are reported to be picked up by trucks
in the urban areas for work on the farns. It would appear that a substantial expansion of the
sector would be required before employment impacts are substantially felt in the villages.

PART III

|1.    ]IDENTIFICATION AND OVERVIEW OF THE POTENTIAL EFFECTS|
|   (SCOPING)l
In this section, impact identification is carried out and the general methodology to be used for
impact assessment is described. The aim of this scoping is to identify all impacts which appear to
be of potential importance at this stage. The list of impacts in not exhaustive for the following
reasons:
-    a number of project elements are only in the planning phase so that their characteristics and
as a result the exact importance of their environmental impact cannot be assessed.
-    it is necessary to limit the assessment to a manageable number of important impacts that
are selected by the expert team (Ahmad and Sammy, 1987)
The scoping exercise is provided under the form of an activity/impact matrix (Table 1./I).
Construction is treated separately although to date no or very little inforrnation is available on the
way of construction of most project components. Nevertheless, in the impact assessment section
important construction impacts will be considered. Even so in the impact assessment section, the
origin/cause of the impacts will be discussed. The assessment will be based to a maximal extent
on quantitative criteria. The assessment criteria which will be outlined and discussed in each
separate chapter will primarily be based on environmental quality objectives, basic values and
norms from the legislation of The Gambia whenever available. Given the importance of the
project, however, the assessment will also be carried out in relation to international quality
objectives and guidelines. Reference will be made in particular to the World Bank's Operational
Policies and Directives, as mentioned before (section 5 of Part I). It should be noted in this
respect that the terms "natural habitat" and "wetlands" do not appear in the joined matrix - they
are in fact covered by "wildlife", "flora", "freshwater aquatic life" and "marine resources". Other
assessement criteria which will be applied are e.g. surface loss, percentual modifications,
increase of diminution, ... The assessment output will be based on the following criteria:
- magnitude: referring to the quantum of change to be experienced
- extent: referring to the area which will be affected
SECTIONA - PART!!!: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THEEXPECTED IMPACTS  136



- significance: referring to the importance of the magnitude considering the
present situation
- special sensitivity: referring to region specific situations of sensitivity e.g.
protected habitats
For the assessment of the importance of impacts other factors such as reversibility and duration
will also be taken into account. Both direct and indirect effects will be considered.

2.   DESCRIPTION AND ASSESSMENT OF THE IMPACTS
2.1.   Climate and air quality
2.1.1. Methodology
In order to assess the impact on air quality in first instance, the emissions to be expected have to
be estimated and quantified. However, for the Trade Gateway project the quantification of the
emissions is often hampered by the fact that there is no information available neither on the type
of industries to be considered nor on the size and importance of the industrial activities and
production outputs. As a result, a number of assumptions will have to be taken into account.
However based upon the best estimate of the emissions, either the contributions to the emission
is calculated or the acceptable emission rates are forwarded. In a next step, the emission data
were fed into an air quality distribution model taking into account climatic characteristics of the
area to be studied. The immission contribution data were compared to the Gambian and
international guidelines to assess the importance and significance of the effects to be expected.
2.1.2. Impact assessment
Emission sources and emissions
Based upon the (limited) project information available the following emission sources can be
identified:
- emissions during construction
- power generation (2 x 650kW)
- industrial activities
* food (fish) processing
* light assembly incl. electronics and TV computer assembly
* electronics
* cotton spinning
- solid waste disposal
- increased traffic
SECTIONA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE ElPECTED IMPACTS  138



During the construction phase, it is expected that emissions of dust may occur. Since no data are
available on neither the construction methods nor the materials to be used, it is not possible to
quantify these emissions. However on the basis of our experience with construction effects it can
be stated that the potential negative effects of dusts may only be expected in limited areas (up to
a few hunderds of metes beyond the project area). Some mitigation measures (see further) may
minimize these effects. Furthermore the duration of the effects will be limited.
Assuming that fuel oil will be used for power generation (which is actually the case in The
Gambia) emissions can be roughly estimated. It should however be noted that emissions will
depend on the grade and composition of the fuel, the type and size of the boiler, the firing and
loading practices and the level of equipment maintenance. In a first phase, a power plant of 650
kW (maximally 1500 liters/day) would lead to the following emissions assuming the sulphur
content of the oil is maximally 1%'o: (EPA, 1985)
- SO2:                1.25 kg./hr
- NOx                 0.41 kg/hr
- particulate matter:   0.5 kg/hr
- Co                  0.038 kgDhr
- VOC                 0.0075 kg/hr
Since the industrial estate lay-out is merely conceptual and neither the types of industries nor the
size of plants is known, it is not possible to estimate emission figures with a sufficient degree of
confidence. However in order to have an idea about the capacity as far as plants are concerned,
emission factors have been gathered for some types of industries assumed to be operated in the
FTZ, namely fish processing, light assembly, electronics, cotton spinning and wood and furniture
production. The emission figures (EPA, 1985; TNO 1988) are summarized in Table 2.L./1

It should be obvious that the larger the construction area, the longer the construction period will

Immissions and assessment of impacts
In order to assess the impact oni the air quality the contribution of the FTZ activities to the
immission concentration has been determined, taking into account the assumptions made above.
The forecast the distribution of pollutants the Gaussian distribution model developed by the
VITO (1997) for multiple points and area souce applications has been used. Meteorological data
i.e. wind speed, wind direction and atmospheric stability classes fed tot the model originate from
the data summarized in section I1L2. Some typical conditions for the area have been fed into the
model in order to obtain immission concentrations under different meteorological conditions
Based upon experience fluegas temperature was assumed to be 80°C; stack diameter was
assumed to be 0.5 m and volumetric flow rate was calculated based upon the emission rate and
taking into account existing emission standards. For the traffic emissions a surface source of 5x2
km was taken into account. Calculations haven been carried out for the major general pollutants
502, NOx, CO, TSP and VOC.
