Construction phase
When comparing the expected immission concentration as a result of increased traffic during the
construction phase it is obvious that for none of the relevant components studied a significant
contribution is to be expected.
Nevertheless construction activities are expected to result in some air quality impacts. The lack
of information on the foreseen construction methods and other technical and organizational
features prevents a quantitative approach of these impacts at this stage. Some dust formation and
an increase in TSP during the construction phase are to be expected; this will have some
significance but it will be limited in extent.
Phase of operation
When considering the expected contribution to air pollution from the different emission sources
foreseen during the operational phase of the project, it appears that the figures obtained for all
parameters studied are quite low.
For SO2, produced from energy provision and industrial processes the contribution is estimated
not te exceed respectively 2 Vg/m3 in case of a 162 Ha FTZ and 0,7 ,ig/m3 in case of a 34 Ha
FTZ in the vicinity of the industrial plants. When comparing these values to the WHO guideline
and the provisional standard of the Gambia it is clear that the impact for this paramnater cannot be
considered to be of any significance. The contribution from the FTZ is lower than maximally 2%
of the safe levels and will surely not surpass the carrying capacity.
NOx is produced both by industrial activities and increased traffic. The maximal immission
concentration expected to result from the project (2 gg/m3) will also remain well below the safe
level of 40 11g/m3. Furthermore in all project sites carrying capacity is largely sufficient for NOx.
PMI0 levels may be subject to some concern in densily populated areas in the Gambia.
Nevertheless the contributions of the project are quite low (maximally 6 ,g/m3) in the vicinity of
the 162 Ha FTZ. The provisional standard will not be surpassed as a result of the project.
As far as CO and volitile organic carbons are concerned the immission contribution from the
project is expected to be very low and will not affect air quality, when considering for instance
that the guideline value for the most critical VOC (benzene) is 5 Ag/in3 (EU).
Apart from the emission of air pollutants possibly affecting heath, nuisance may be caused as a
result of odour. In particular the operation of fish processing industries and uncontrolled waste
dumping may be expected to emit odorous components. Provided proper mitigation measures are
foreseen (i.e. zoning, waste dumping organization) odour effects can be limited.
Irrespective of the location the emissions into the ambient air from the development planned will
be the same. As a consequence the contribution to air pollution will be the same for the different
selected sites. Since the meteorological conditons are quite similar for all sites the distribution of
SECTION A - PART III: /DENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTION AND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  143



air pollutants is expected to follow approximately the same pattern. Since all sites selected
appear to have a substantial carrying capacity no significant difference in air quality impact is to
be expected between the alternative sites selected.
2.2.   Water and aquatic resources
2.2.1   Methodology
Since information is lacking on both the construction method and the importance of the
industrial activities planned, a quantitative approach of groundwater use and subsequent effects
is not possible, neither can an estimation been made on the pollution degree of waste water.
Nevertheless, potential effects have been discussed in a qualitative way and some assumptions
were made on waste water production in order to the assess the needs for rnitigation.
2.2.2   Impact assessment
2.2.2.1 Groundwater
EFFECTS DURING CONSTRUCTION PHASE
Possible direct impacts of the development of the Trade Gateway Project on groundwater during
the construction phase are as follows:
- a temporary lowering of the groundwater table where construction works are carried out
below the water table;
- contamination of groundwater due to accidental spills of products used during construction,
for example oil and lubricants used for machinery, etc.
Temporary lowering of groundwater table
Works to be carried out below the water table, for example installing of water mains, discharge
drains, fire hydrants, foundations, etc. require a lowering of the ground water table through
groundwater pumping. The lowering of the water table is temporary and only takes place during the
period of drainage, which could take several months. The radius of influence caused by drainage
depends on soil perneability and extends when sand content increases.
At the coastal sites, the radius of influence (the area in which lowering of the ground water table
will be noticeable) will be limited, as soil is very clayey in most parts of the proposed locations. An
estimate based on required lowering of the water table and soil permeability leads to radiuses of
only a few metres. At the inland sites and the sandy part of Denton Bridge site, sand content of
SECTIONAr - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIP7IONAND ASSESSMENTOF THEEXPECTED IMPACTS  144



soils is higher. Therefore the radius of influence caused by drainage will become more extended
and could reach from a few metres up to a maximum of about hundred metres.
Lowering of the groundwater table can lead to soil settlements and subsequent subsidence of
buildings that are located within the radius of influence. As there are no buildings located within
the radius of influence that is expected at any of the proposed sites, there is no subsidence of
existing infrastructures to be expected.
Contamination of groundwater
Contamination of ground water during construction could originate from accidental spills of
products used, for example oil and lubricants for machinery. These accidental spills are difficult
to be quantified. If soils contain a considerable amount of sand or in case the water table is
shallow, spilled pollutants could migrate through the soil and reach the groundwater.
The coastal sites are mainly characterised by a shallow water table in combination with clayey
soils. Assuming only small amounts of oil, lubricants or other pollutants are spilled onto the
ground, and spills will be noticed quickly, possible contamination will remain local because of
the high content of clay, which inhibits fast spreading. The sandy part of Denton Bridge site is
more sensitive because of a combination of sandy soil and shallow groundwater.
At the inland sites, clay content of the soils is much lower, but the water table is less shallow,
only appearing starting from about one metre below ground level. Accidental spills in these
areas, if noticed in time, will only lead to local pollution as well.
In any case, pollution from accidental spills will be restricted to the upper sand aquifer and there
is no risk of contamination of the lower sand aquifer.
Because of the local character of possible groundwater pollution and the absence of hand-dug
wells in immediate surroundings of any of the sites, there is no risk of contamination of
groundwater supply in nearby villages.
EFFECTS DURING OPERATION PHASE
Possible direct impacts of the development of the Trade Gateway Project on groundwater during
the operation phase are:
-  a permanent lowering of the groundwater due to excessive extraction of groundwater onsite;
- contamination of groundwater due to storage, transport or accidental spills of products used
during production;
- contamination of groundwater due to disposal of solid waste;
- contamination of groundwater due to waste water infiltration;
SECTION A - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRrPTION AND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  145



Permanent lowering of groundwater table
Water supply at most of the proposed sites will not be provided by extraction of ground water on
site. NAWEC has a facility in the neighbourhood of the coastal sites on Serrekunda Highway,
consisting of two one million gallon water storage tanks. Probably a pumped main from these
tanks to the sites will be constructed to ensure water supply. The storage tanks are provided with
water pumped in from a treatment facility at Sukuta.
NAWEC also has a storage facility of some 1,000 cubic metres nearby the airport site. Water is
provided from the Sukuta treatment plant. Two existing boreholes are located in the proximity
of the airport site, but they are not in use at present. Plans exist to restart extraction at those
boreholes and create a connection to a treatment works.
The Sukuta site, bordered by boreholes SS2 in the north-west and SS3 in the south-west, is an
integral part of the Salagi wellfield. Untreated water can be tapped directly at the SS2 and SS3
boreholes. Treated water will probably have to be provided from the Sukuta treatment plant.
In the Kuloro area, there is no water supply available. The nearest water main is in Brikama,
some 8.5 km from the site. Water supply to this site would be provided by establishing a stand-
alone borehole supply on site. Dependent on the amount of water that would be extracted, there
could be a lowering of the water table in the surroundings of the borehole. As long as extraction
does not exceed recharge, there are no problems to be expected.
Since water supply will be provided from existing wellfields, lowering of the ground water table
at the proposed sites does not have to be considered as a direct effect of the realisation of the
Trade Gateway Project. On the other hand, an increasing water demand could lead to increased
extraction at Sukuta and Welingara wellfields, which are located in the near surroundings of
Abuko nature reserve. In recent years, a lowering of the water table at Abuko has been observed
and an increased extraction at the nearby wellfields could aggravate that phenomenon.
Contamination of groundwater
Storage, transport and accidental spills
Storage and transport of liquid products, transport of products and waste water through pipes, and
accidental spills during loading, unloading or production can cause groundwater pollution. Storage
and transport facilities are not specified in the project description. In general, as long as appropriate
precautionary measures are taken to prevent accidental spills/losses and storage and transport of
products is managed carefully, there is only a minor risk of groundwater pollution. Assuming only
limited amounts of pollutants will end up in the environment, groundwater pollution will remain
local. At the coastal sites, groundwater occurs shallow, but high clay content will prevent
spreading. At the inland sites, soils contain more sand, but groundwater occurs deeper. The most
sensitive area is the sandy part of Denton Bridge site, located near Oyster Creek.
SECTIONA - PART1: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  146



Measures to be taken to prevent groundwater pollution during operation will be discussed in the
management plan.
Disposal of solid waste
In the project description, no details are given about waste production or waste disposal.
For the coastal sites, there is a proposition to provide a number of waste collection and
consolidation zones from which licensed disposal companies can collect and transport the waste off-
site. At present, there is an illegal open dump located on Bund Road site.
At the inland sites, it is also proposed to provide a number of waste collection zones on site. Solid
waste will be collected by private disposal companies and transported off site. At present, waste at
the airport is dumped in open sites, which are not provided with any measures to prevent
groundwater pollution.
Considering the types of activities that will probably be developed in the Trade Gateway Project,
(semi-) solid wastes will mainly consist of:
- packaging materials (papers, cardboard);
- organic matter;
- glass;
- lubrication oil and hydraulic fluids from machinery;
- spent acid.
Taking into account that total production of the project will be no more than 30,000 tons a year, a
maximum of 3,000 tons waste a year will be generated. Dumping of waste in open sites is
unacceptable. Waste will have to be stored temporarily and collected regularly by licensed disposal
companies. Provided storage is managed in an appropriate way, there is only a minor risk of
groundwater pollution. If production and subsequently the amount of waste generated increases
significantly, construction of a legal dump could be necessary.
Precautionary measures that should be taken to prevent pollution from waste storage and disposal in
collection zones will be discussed in the management plan. Establishment of a dump will also be
mentioned in the management plan.
Infiltration of waste water
There are no piped sewerage networks available at the coastal sites. According to the project
description, sewerage networks will have to be constructed in combination with a stand-alone
treatment plant. No details are given with regard to the treatment infrastructure. Groundwater
pollution can be caused by leakage of sewerage drains and infiltration of effluent water into the
soil.
SECTION A - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  147



At the inland sites, sewerage will have to be treated in a stand-alone treatment plant or by means of
individual septic tanks and soakaways. The existing facilities at the airport dispose of a sewage
system and have septic tank installations. A private company empties the tanks from time to time.
Effluent of the proposed treatment plant could be provided as irrigation water into the landscaped
zones of the area. Another possibility is infiltration of the purified water into the soil, as a recharge
to groundwater.
As the information concerning industrial activities is very limited, assumptions had to be made
with regard to nature and extent of these activities. Expected wastewater emissions were based
on those assumptions.
Considering the proposed types of activities, apart from sanitary waste waters relevant emissions
are only to be expected from fish processing and electronics, light assembly and TV-computer
assembly. Wastewater generated by fish processing will mainly contain a heavy BOD/COD
load, while wastewater from electronics and light, TV or computer assembly will contain a lower
BOD/COD load, but could contain acids or solvents used for degreasing. Trading and
packaging, agricultural produce export and cotton spinning will only generate sanitary
wastewater.
As there is no information on the scale of the activities, the following assumptions were made.
As was mentioned above, total production will probably not exceed 30,000 tons a year.
Emissions from fish processing were calculated starting from a total production of 3,000 tons a
year divided over three plants:
- a cannery, capacity: 1,000 tons/year, waste water production: 5 m3/ton fish
- a deep freeze unit, capacity: 1,000 tons/year, waste water production: 5 m3/ton fish;
- a fish drying unit, capacity: 1,000 tons/year, waste water production: 1 m3/ton fish.
Waste water flow of electronics and assembly and average concentrations of the most relevant
pollutants for all activities were assessed based on figures of similar plants. Waste water
characteristics related to the different types of activities are shown in table 2.2./1.

As there are no Gambian effluent standards available, Belgian effluent standards for discharging of

pH                                  6.5-9                  6.5-9
BOD                    mg/I         25                     25

If the estimated BOD- and COD-concentrations of waste water generated by fish processing are
compared to the Belgian effluent standards, it is clear that treatment of the water will be necessary.
In case of electronics and light, TV or computer assembly, there seems to be no problem with
regard to BOD- or COD-loads. However, waste water generated by these kinds of activities mostly
contains acids and/or solvents as a result of what treatment will be necessary as well.
Depending on the total amount of wastewater being generated by the whole of the industrial estate,
treatment could be handled by means of septic tanks or in a treatment plant. If the total amount of
waste water discharged during one year does not exceed a total quantity of 10,000 m3, waste water
can be collected and treated in septic tanks, which will have to be emptied regularly. If the quantity
of water discharged exceeds the amount of 30,000 ni3 a year, a biological treatment plant will have
to be installed.
In any case, a sewerage network will have to be constructed to collect waste water and transport it
to:
- septic tanks which are emptied regularly by licensed companies;
- a discharge point where waste water will be infiltrated into the soil without treatment;
- a treatment plant in which water will be treated before discharging.
Infiltration of waste water into the soil without treatment will at first be the cause of local
groundwater pollution. There will be a rise in BOD- and COD-concentrations, pH values will drop
as a result of discharge of acids and groundwater will become polluted with solvents. In the long
term, when discharging goes on for several years, groundwater pollution will become more
extended as discharge will probably largely exceed natural attenuation. Special attention should be
paid to the presence of solvents in groundwater. Most solvents used for degreasing are quite
persistent and mobile in groundwater. As density of many chlorinated solvents is larger than
density of water, they tend to sink through the aquifer until stopped by an impermeable layer. Even
small gaps in the aquitard separating the upper and lower shallow sand aquifer could lead to
spreading of solvents into the lower aquifer, which is used for all municipal supplies. Pollution of
the lower aquifer is a matter of major concern at Sukuta site, which is part of the Salagi wellfield.
Contamination of the lower aquifer could jeopardise water supply.
SECTioNA - PARTII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRlPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  149



Treatment of waste water and infiltration of the effluent would reduce contamination of
groundwater, but would not exclude it. If treatment plants effluent only contains small amounts of
pollutants, groundwater pollution will be limited. Otherwise, if there is no monitoring of effluents
and considerable amounts of pollutants are still discharged, extensive groundwater pollution could
be the result in the long term. Therefore, standards for infiltration of effluents, as a recharge to
groundwater, and analysis of effluents are necessary. These measures will be discussed in the
management plan.
2.2.2.2 Surface water (fresh, estuarine and marine water)
With the exception of temporary channels or flows of fresh water during the rainy season, there is
no surface water present in the near surroundings of any of the inland sites. The coastal sites on the
other hand, are partly situated in mangroves. As mentioned in chapter 3.2.3, the surface water
within these mangroves can be characterised as saline water.
Fresh water is not considered, as there is no permanent fresh water present in the near surroundings
of any of the proposed sites.
Because of the intrusion of salt water into the lower part of the River Gambia, it is supposed that
there will be no use of surface water for sanitary or industrial purposes. Therefore, there is no
impact to be expected due to excessive surface water use.
EFFECTS DURTNG CONSTRUCTION PHASE
There is no substantial direct or indirect impact on surface water to be expected during construction
phase. The only possible, minor effect is contamination of mangrove water at the coastal sites
trough runoff of products that are accidentally spilled, for example oil and lubricants for machinary.
As the amounts of products spilled will be rather small, contamination will be diluted to a negligible
degree.
EFFECTS DURING OPERATION PHASE
Possible impacts on surface water during operation phase are:
- contamination of surface water due to discharging of waste water;
- contamination of surface water due to runoff.
DISCHARGING OF WASTE WATER
The most important impact on surface water during operation is related to the discharging of waste
water in local river-, estuarine or marine systems. Contaminants, released with waste water, can
influence the water quality of these systems. Sewage drains and treatment plants are provided in the
project description, without being specified.
SECTrOANA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONANDASSESSMENTOFTHEEXPECTEDIMPACTS  150



At the inland sites, there is no permnanent surface water present in the near surroundings of any of
the sites. Waste water will be collected in septic tanks or infiltrated as a recharge to groundwater,
with or without treatment.
The area surrounding Bund Road site and Denton Bridge is crossed with various mangrove creeks.
Waste water could be discharged in one of those creeks. Part of the untreated Banjul waste water is
already discharged into the mangroves of Bund Road polder through open drains (see chapter 3.1.3
of part II). The quality of the mangrove water near Bund Road site was discussed in chapter 3.2.3
of part II. The carrying capacity of the water is determined by COD-concentration. It can be
considered as zero as the actual COD-concentration already exceeds the standard for saline waters.
Discharge of waste water containing high loads of BOD and COD, acids and solvents will further
deteriorate water quality.
At Denton Bridge site, the only part suitable for building is located in the proximity of Oyster
Creek, which is the inlet to the ecologically very sensitive area of Tanbi Wetland. There are no data
available on the quality of saline water in Oyster Creek, but discharging of waste water containing
high BOD and COD loads, acids and solvents would gradually lead to pollution of the water, even
after treatment. In the long term, pollution could spread into Tanbi Wetland and disturb the
ecosystem.
RuNOFF
In the IDI-study was indicated that development of the Bund Road site would have to involve the
construction of a surface water runoff collection pond and a system sufficient to drain the site to the
nearby creeks. Runoff water could be polluted due to spills during production, transport, loading or
unloading. Similar to the construction phase, amounts of products spilled will mostly be small, and
contamination will be diluted to a negligible degree. Oyster Creek near Denton Bridge, is the inlet
to an ecologically very important area and even the smallest degree of pollution should be avoided.
2.3    Soil resources and waste
2.3.1   Methodology
Due to lack of detail in project description, effects can only be assessed in a qualitative way.
Potential effects have been identified and described taking into account the actual situation with
respect to soil quality and waste management.
Contamination of soil and groundwater are highly related. The possible effects considered below
are partly a repetition of the impacts on groundwater described in chapter 2.2.1.
SECTIONA - PART HI: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTION AND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  151



2.3.2   Impact assessment
2.3.2.1 Effects during construction phase
Possible direct impacts on soil during the construction phase are as follows:
- permanent alterations in soil composition and soil structure;
- soil contamination due to accidental spills of products used during construction, for example
oil and lubricants used for machinery, etc;
- soil erosion due to the clearance of vegetation.
SOIL COMPOSITION AND SOIL STRUCTURE
Ground works, soil excavation and soil storage, as well as elevation can cause serious alterations in
original soil structure. Working with heavy machinery and of piling of heavy loads leads to soil
compaction, which results in reorientation of soil aggregates. Soil structure becomes layered or
stacked and pore volume decreases. Fine textured (clay, silt) and saturated soils, which are found at
the coastal sites, are more susceptible to soil compaction than coarse textured (sand) and drier soils
of the inland sites.
Input of building materials causes abrupt changes in texture between input materials and original
soil. The textural transitions can result in local changes in soil permeability, nutritional situation
and ability of plants to grow.
Indirectly, the above effects can cause changes in the pattern of groundwater flow and soil use.
Ground works, elevations and excavations, and lowering of the water table as the result of
groundwater drainage  can  also  cause  settlement.   Settlement is determined  by  soil
compressibility and thickness of the concerning soil layer. Carrying capacity of wet and fine
textured soils is rather low.
Impact on soil composition and soil structure will be more pronounced at the coastal sites.
SOIL POLLUTION
Soil pollution during construction can originate from accidental spills of products used, for example
oil and lubricants for machinery. These accidental spills are difficult to be quantified. Coarse
textured soils are more susceptible to spreading of pollution than fine textured soils.
Assuming only small amounts of oil, lubricants or other pollutants will be spilled onto the
ground, and considering spills will be noticed quickly, possible soil contamination will remain
local in all sites. At the coastal sites, extent of soil pollution will even be more limited because
of clay preventing fast spreading.
SECTIONA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  152



SOIL EROSION
The main forces causing erosion are water and/or wind. Erosion can be prevented by covering soil
with vegetation (grass, shrub, forest). Presence of concrete cement (roads, buildings) also prevents
erosion. Erosion could lead to reduced land productivity and in addition to secondary effects, such
as stream sedimentation, which could cause disruption of natural hydrologic processes.
All sites are characterised by a flat relief, as a result of what erosion risk is rather limited. Parts of
the proposed sites that are selected for construction of buildings and other facilities will have to be
cleared from vegetation. As soil in build on parts will be hardened during construction, erosion risk
disappears. Cleared parts that aren't build on or hardened will have to be covered with vegetation to
prevent erosion.
2.3.2.2 Effects during operation phase
Possible impacts of the development of the Trade Gateway Project on soil during the operation
phase are as follows:
-  soil contamination due to storage, transport or accidental spills of products used during
production;
-  soil contamination due to disposal of waste;
-  soil contamination due to waste water discharge.
STORAGE. TRANSPORT AND ACCTDENTAL SPILLS
Storage and transport of liquid products, transport of products and waste water through pipes and
accidents during loading or unloading can cause soil pollution. Storage and transport facilities are
not specified in the project description. In general, as long as appropriate precautionaiy measures
are taken to prevent accidental spills or losses and storage and transport of products is managed in
an appropriate way, there is only a minor risk of soil pollution. Assuming only limited amounts of
pollutants will end up in the environment, soil pollution will remain local. At the coastal sites, soils
contain a lot of clay. Soils at the inland sites and the sandy part of Denton Bridge site are coarse
textured and will be more sensitive to soil pollution since contaminants will be able to spread faster.
Measures to be taken to prevent soil pollution during operation will be discussed in the management
plan.
DISPOSAL OF SOLID WASTE
In the project description, no details are given on waste production or waste disposal.
SECTION A - PART III: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPA CTS  153



Types and quantities of waste to be generated because of the development of the Trade Gateway
Project were described in chapter 2.2.2.1.
Dumping of waste in open sites, followed by burning or burial, is unacceptable. Waste will have to
be stored temporarily and collected regularly. As storage is managed in an appropriate way, there is
only a minor risk of soil pollution. As mentioned before, coarse textured soils such as at the airport
site, Sukuta, Kuloro and a part of Denton Bridge, are most vulnerable.
Precautionary measures to be laken with regard to waste disposal will be discussed in the
management plan.
DISCHARGE OF WASTE WATER
Characteristics of waste water and methods of collecting and treatment were described in chapter
2.2.2.1.
Infiltration of waste water into the soil without treatment will at first lead to local soil pollution. pH
values will drop as a result of discharge of acids and soil could become polluted with solvents. On
the longer term, when discharging goes on for several years, soil pollution will become more
extended and pollution will certainly migrate into groundwater. Treatment of waste water and
infiltration of the effluent will reduce soil pollution, but does not exclude it. The major concern
with regard to soil pollution as result of infiltration of waste water, is migration of pollutants into
groundwater and spreading of pollution through groundwater flow.
2.4.   Noise
2.4.1. Methodology
For the proper assessment of noise impacts, noise levels of machines brought into operation,
equipments to be used, exact locations of the noise sources etc. should be known. In that case
expected noise levels in the neighbourhood can be predicted using a calculation model taking
into account actual noise levels and physical characteristics of the site. Since to date only a
general preliminary lay-out of the facilities to be developed is available, this approach will not be
feasable. As a consequence the noise issue will be approached by determining the acceptable
noise outputs based upon noise quality criteria and acceptable noise levels.
2.4.2.  Impact assessment
Noise impacts may be the result of 3 major types of activities:
- the construction of the FTZ;
- the operation (activities) in the FTZ;
- increase in traffic related to the construction and operation FTZ.
SECTIONA - PARTIII. IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTION AND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  154



As far as the construction phase is concerned operation of machinery, groundworks and internal
transport will lead to an increase in noise levels. Taking into consideration that construction
activities will be carnied out in different phases on the one hand and will be rather limited in
time, the increase will be temporarily and limited. As a consequence noise during the
construction phase is not considered to provoke a significant impact with a relevant magnitude.
In order to evaluate the noise impact from the operation activities in the FTZ an inverse
calculation has been carried out. In other words it has been determined what level the noise
capacity may reach for each project site in order not to surpass acceptable criteria for the
neighbouring area. To that aim a simplified mathematical model has been used, taking into
consideration the following conditions/assumptions:
- each site is considere to be circular, the surface being known, respectively:
34 Ha for the Bund Road site and Denton Bridge sites;
162 Ha for the other sites;
- the sound transfer to the surroundings is calculated on the basis of the following formula
valid for point sources:
LP = Lw - 20 log r + 10 log q - 11  - D
Whereby
r = distance from the reception point to the source (in m.)
Lp = sound pressure level in dB (A) at a distance r from the source
Lw = noise capacity of the source concerned
q = factor of direction ( q=2 for sound distribution according to a semi circle)
D   reduction factor as a consequence of various factors, e.g. molecular air absorption, soil
effect, vegetation effect., in the model applied a reduction factor of at least 1.5 Db(A) per
100 m is taken into account (some vegetation).
The results of the assessment of the maximal noise capacity per project site has been determined
taking into consideration the condition that at a distance of 200m from the border of each project
site the sound pressure level is at maximum 45 dB(A). According to the ISO-R 1996 criteria this
level equals the allowable sound pressure in daytime for a quiet residential area. The date are
summarized in Table 2.4./i.
SECTiONA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICA TION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  155



Table 2.4./i: Calculated sound pressure levels
Unity                F7Z                 FIZ
Surface                       34 Ha               162 Ha
Sound capacity Lw in dB(A)     105.6              109.5
Noise level at 200m dB(A)       45                 45
The results from the calculations can further be used as a basis for the estimation of the maximal
sound capacity when the conditions and criteria used would change. For instance when the
criterium at the receptor point would be 55 dB(A) instead of the 45 dB(A), the maximum
allowable sound capacity can be increased with approximately 15 dB(A). When the receptor
point would be situated at a distance of 100m from the project site instead of 200m the allowable
sound capacity has to be reduced with approximately 3 dB(A).
When evaluating noise effects, the selection of a criterion of 45 dB(A) should prevent not only
impacts on human healt - supposedly protected below 70 dB - but also prevent psychological
effects such as sleep interferences, decreased working capacity, ... and as such protect human
welfare. It is from this point of view that the criterion of 45 dB(A) is selected (see also ISO,
1971), so that no significant interferences and impacts should be expected beyond a distance of
200 m from each industrial project site.
Both the construction and the operation of the FTZ will lead to an increase in traffic. Due to lack
of capacity data for the FTZ, it is not possible to estimate with a sufficient degree of confidence
the traffic increase. As a result the use of a model for traffic noise calculation (i.e. VABI
developed by the Dutch Reseach Institute TNO) will provide no added value to the impact
assessment. Nevertheless some assumptions can be made and based upon the assumptions made
and experience data on the noise impact of the traffic use on highways (ECOLAS, 1996) an
assessment can be made.
The assumptions on traffic increase from section III 2.1. are taken into account. For the 162 Ha
sites this would lead to an increase of traffic with:
- maximally 20 trucks and some 50 cars/day during construction
- maximally some 50 trucks and some 3000 cars/day during operation
For the 32 Ha site (Bund Road or Denton Bridge) maximally one third of these quantities may be
reached during operation. Average daily traffic figures (National Transport Plan 1997-2006)
amount up to 13420 vehicles at the Coastal road (Denton Bridge, Bund Road) and up to 3483 at
the airport road.
SECTIONA - PARTIII. IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTEDIMPACTS  156



On the average, on an hourly basis, it can be calculated (10 log (actual + additional )/ actual) that
the additional noise output (LAeq I h) from the increased traffic would reach during operation:
- 0.34 dB(A) at Bund Road
- 1 dB(A) at Denton Bridge
- 2 dB(A) at the airport site
Due to the actual lower traffic level increases would be somewhat higher (up to 3 dB(A)) on the
average at the other sites Kuloro and Sukuta. During construction additional noise output,
outside the immediate vicenity of the sites would be neglegible.
Based upon the assessment carried out it can be concluded that during construction noise
provided by local activities and indirect noise effects from increased traffic will be limited both
in magnitude and in time. Noise is not expected to create a burden of significance during
construction phase. This conclusion applies to all sites selected e.g. Bund Road, Denton Bridge,
Airport, Kuloro and Sukuta.
During operation, noise will be produced on the one hand from the industrial activities and on
the other hand indirectly as a result of increased traffic. From the calculations made it appears
that the sound capacity allowed to reach an acceptable sound pressure level at 200 m from the
site exceeds 105 dB(A). Taking into account that only rather small industrial units will be set up
in the FTZ it is not expected on the basis of the actual plans that this sound pressure level will be
exceeded neither in a 34 Ha FTZ (Bund Road) nor in a 162 Ha FTZ (other sites).
When considering the actual estimated noise levels at the different sites, some differences may
be noted. Indeed due to air traffic at the airport site the noise level is already increased up to 55-
56 dB(A). It can be calculated that the contribution of the operation activities of the FTZ will
only further increase this level with about 0.5 dB(A), in particular during daytime up to a
distance of 200m from the border of the FTZ. Nevertheless a value of 55 dB(A) being a
guideline value for preventing noise to be come a burden, the small impact may be considered to
call for some caution and zoning is to be advised. At all other sites actually characterized by a
lower noise level of approximately 45 dB(A), the additional sound pressure would lead to an
increase up to 48 dB(A), which is still well below the guideline value so that the impact of FTZ
activities is not to be considered being significant at these sites.
As far as the additional noise pressure from transport activities is concerned, again the actual
elevated value at the airport site calls for caution. Indeed, a 2 dB(A) increase is expected in the
neighbourhood of the FTZ access roads (up to 200m off the roads) in the airport area, leading to
some burden for residential living in this area. At the other sites, the actual carrying capacity
allows the predicted increase in noise pressure without giving raise to negative impacts.
SECTIONA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  157



2.5.    Terrestrial and freshwater fauna and flora
2.5.1.  Introduction and methodology
The impact prediction for 'fauna and flora' will be based on literature and general insight of the
experts. The assessment of environmental impacts is given for the construction phase and for the
operational one; direct effects (such as those resulting from land clearance) as well as indirect
effects are described . Concerning the effects on wildlife, the impact prediction is focussed on
birds (avifauna).
Except for a rough calculation of the predicted disappearance of natural habitats, the effects
have not been quantified due to the absence of precise information with regard to the expected
industrial development.
Plants and animals are capable of withstanding small or gradual changes to their natural
environment. However, drastic alteration by major construction for example, may alter the
natural conditions to the extent where the threshold for survival of a native species is surpassed,
causing it to ineffectively compete in its natural environment. Alteration of a natural site may
result in an irreversible trend of habitat degradation and species reduction in and around this site.
These losses of habitat in turn may lead to more specific ecological impacts such as wind and
water erosion, surface and ground water deterioration. Human activity may cause radical and/or
long-term changes to the habitat and habits of occupant plants and animals. Such changes will
endanger or destroy some individuals, will cause others to leave the area and may interfere with
animal reproductive cycles.
2.5.2.  Flora and vegetation
The possible impact sources with regard to flora and vegetation can be listed as follows:
(i)  direct effects ofproject development
- occupancy of land and habitat loss through clearance of the project sites;
- habitat fragmentation / isolation, as a permanent effect of the former;
- reduction of habitat quality, through the effects of various pollution sources;
(ii) indirect effects
- a possible increase of human pressure on natural habitats in the environs, through
encroachment and settlement in the project area.
For all 5 nominated project sites it can be stated that the expected air pollution levels will not result
in any phytotoxic effect on vegetation or crops.
SECTION A - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTION AND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  158



Emission of solid and liquid wastes, on the other hand, have to be considered for their possible
adverse effects, particularly at the coastal sites of Bund Road and Denton Bridge.
2.5.2.1. Effects as a result of the construction phase
Bund Road site
The proposed site , with a surface of approximately 34 ha, is located at the northern end of the
larger area bounded by Bund Road. The whole area of Bund Polder is included in the Tanbi
Wetland Complex, which has been recommended as a wetland reserve under the Ramsar
Convention, to which The Gambia is a signatory.
- The construction of the project will result in the clearance of an area of barren flats, scrub and
mangrove remnants, which represent a surface of approximately 10 % of the total area of Bund
Polder and approximately 0.6 % of the total area of the proposed Ramsar site.
As discussed in Part II (6.2.2.3. and land use: 8.2.1.), Bund Road site proper is a degraded part
of the original Polder area; it includes a Nawec distribution station, an extensive garbage tip and
other debris originating from the city of Banjul.
- Given the relativily low current habitat value of the project site, the direct effects of land
clearance and reduction of habitat quality can be evaluated as only slightly negative. The same
habitats are present in a much larger area of pristine mangrove swamps, southwest of Bund
Polder.
- On the other hand, the proposed development can also be considered as an encroachment on a
buffer zone between the highway and Tanbi Wetland. As a future industrial site, this zone in fact
loses its potential function in the context of wetland management of the proposed Ramsar site.
Denton Bridge site
The proposed site of approximately 34 hectares (same as Bund road, according to the assumption
made  in  this  EIA)  has  to  be  located  in  the  coastal  area  north  of  the
Serrekunda-Banjul highway.
- When the area between the bridge (at Oyster Creek) and Old Cape Road is considered, only
one sandy strip of land situated alongside the highway can be evaluated as possibly suitable for
development. There, the destruction through land clearance of one type of coastal habitat would
result in a rather small environmental effect. The area in question is more or less open, with low
scrub and a few trees [see Part II, 6.2.2.3. (ii)] - a much larger zone of comparable dune
woodland exists to the east of Denton Bridge. However, from our field investigations it appears
that this possible location near ELF petrol station covers a surface of 5 hectares only, whereas at
least 34 ha are needed. Physical limitations thus exclude further consideration of this area.
SECTION A - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  159



- At any other place between Denton Bridge and Old Cape Road construction of the project
would result in the clearance of several natural vegetation communities (dense mangrove
swamps, coastal shrubs, coastal lagoons, ...). This would result in inadmissible, very important
negative effects on the coastal ecosystem: see chapter 2.6. for further comments.
Airport site
Development of the Airport site requires the clearance of at least 160 ha of the present woodland.
For two reasons this effect on terrestrial vegetation is rather important:
- From the analysis of the present situation (Part II, 6.2.2.3.) it appears that this airport
woodland, due to the absence of intensive human use of it, has great potential for restoration
of the vegetation to a more primary ecosystem. This potential will be destroyed by industrial
development of the site.
-  The site represents the only rather vaste area of woodland amid agricultural land and shrub
savanna. Clearance of the entire wooded zone will result in a irreversible loss of forest
habitat near Yundum airport. In a broader context, it will lead to further habitat
fragmentation and destruction of an ecological link ("stepping stone") between Tanbi and
the Mandinari area (in the east of GBA), the forest parks and Tanji Bird Reserve in the west.
Kiloro and Sukuta sites
Large parts of both reservation sites for the Trade Gateway Project are currently designated to
agricultural development. The original vegetation of these areas has been destroyed through
human influence during the last decades. At Kuloro a typical open shrubland remains - the
Sukuta site is best described as 'open tree-shrub savanna', where a well developed 'closed
woodland forest' of the Guinea-Savanna type existed till 20 to 30 years ago.
- Both sites cover a much larger area than the 160-200 hectares which are required for a possible
development plan similar to the one at the airport.
- Given the rather limited ecological value of the present shrub savanna, clearance would not
constitute an important effect when it is considered as an isolated case.
Nonetheless, it would reinforce the adverse effects of habitat fragmentation. Development at these
sites would irreversibly reclaim land that nowadays still posesses a good reforestation potential.
This aspect could become more important in the near future, seen the increasing efforts to fight
against desertification in the Sahel countries.
2.5.2.2. Effects during the phase of operation
- The most important type of direct effects from an operational FEZ could be a reduction of
habitat quality, through the effects of various pollution sources.
SECTIONA - PART III: IDENTIFICATION. DESCPJPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  160



From chapters 2.2. and 2.3. we conclude that the risks are very low, on condition that all required
measures are taken to prevent unwanted leakage and other emissions. In case of accidents, the
expected impact on the natural vegetation will certainly be more important at the coastal sites than
at the airport and two other inland sites (Kuloro and Sukuta). In fact, at Bund Rd. and particularly at
Denton Bridge an accidental pollution is likely to affect other parts of Tanbi Wetland via the
existing channels and the influence of tidal movements.
- A significant development of the Bund Road FEZ will possibly require the Bund Rd.
carriageway to be widened by approximately 5 metres . According to the feasibility study (IDI,
2000) the necessity of this widening will be assessed once the site is fully operational. We
assume that this additional construction will affect the Polder side of the road only, so that no
effects on the pristine part of Tanbi ( = south side of the road) have to be considered.
- Indirect effects , caused by an increased human pressure on natural habitats, could occur at
Kuloro en Sukuta . There is good evidence that a 'mushrooming of settlements' will be
encouraged around the FEZ ( see below: socio-economic impacts - chap. 2.9.). The likelihood of
encroachment on the adjacent Forest Parks (Salagi FP at the Sukuta site, Furuyar FP at Kuloro)
will require proper preventive actions.
Increasing encroachment is to be feared particularly at Abuko Nature Reserve. There are reasons
to accept that FEZ development at the Airport site , together with the implementation of the
GCAA master plan, would further attract people to the surrounding villages - especially Lamin,
Banjulding and Yundum.
Consequently, the fate of Abuko NR should be considered as a major indirect issue, in the face of
accelerated development of the airport area. The unique vegetation is already degrading because
accelerated development of the surrounding watershed has reduced water inflow into the reserve.
The reserve is surrounded by a gazetted buffer zone that is heavily degraded because it lacks
protection. It goes without saying that any further human pressure could seriously affect the buffer
zone. The socio-economic impact analysis (chapter 2.9.) provides more information to estimate the
degree to which this increased pressure is probable.
Effects on other protected areas
Given the limited scale of the proposed development project, no adverse environmental effects
are to be expected at remote protected areas such as Tanji Bird Reserve and Bijilo Forest Park,
Baobolon Wetland complex or Niumi National Park.
2.5.3. Fauna
The description and assessment of the impacts in the 5 nominated sites will be carried out for the
construction phase as well as for the phase of operation. Assessment of the impacts will be
discussed especially with regard to the bird species, except when other fauna data were available.
SECTIONA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONANDASSESSMENTOFTHEEXPECTEDIMPACTS  161



2.5.3.1. Effects during construction
Possible major effects on avifauna (fauna) of the construction phase of the project will be linked
namely to noise and land clearance. Noise causes fright and escape reactions with birds (small
mammals), and clearance of natural vegetation may lead to a reduction of avifauna (fauna)
diversity due to the removal of cover and food supplies.
Bund Road site
Approximately 60% of the total surface of Bund Polder is already urbanised. The
remaining surface consists of the juxtaposition of barren flats and mangrove swamps,
which leads to a great variety of bird life. It is particularly important for migrant
waterfowls from Europe and for non-breeding African waterbirds.
The birds associated to this habitat will probably locate in the mangrove swamps situated
directly south outside the Bund Road area which are presently to a lesser extent
influenced by human activities (squatter settlements and garbage tips).
The proposed site has already an important noise level background due to the road traffic
(Bund road, Serrekunda-Banjul highway) and human activity in the surroundings (school,
offices and settlements). The noise charge caused by the construction of the project will
probably not have a significant negative effect on avifauna.
Denton Bfridge site
The coastal area north of the Serrekunda-Banjul highway supports large numbers of
African waterbirds and consitutes the entry point of fish and invertebrates that breed in
the mangrove but live adult lives in the sea. It is included in the proposed Ramsar Site
(Tanbi Wetland complex) and has a vital role in its ecology because of its communication
function. The negative effects through loss of specific habitats will probably be more
significant here than in the Bund Road site.
Though Denton Bridge is stretched along the Serrekunda-Banjul highway, the proposed
site is actually relatively protected from human activity. The noise charge caused by the
construction of the project will probably not be insignificant and will lead to a negative
effect on avifauna (fauna).
Airport area
The proposed site concerns a total area of approximately 200 hectares located within the
boundaries of the international airport of Banjul. It concerns disturbed open woodland
with heavy shrub, which will disappear with the construction of the project.
SECTIONA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTION AND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  162



The birds (and a few of the characteristics mammals) associated to this particular habitat
will probably locate habitat in the surroundings that meets their ecological requirements.
The negative effect on avifauna by noise will probably be insignificant because of the
considerable noise level background due to aircraft.
Kuloro site and Sukuta site
The proposed site for Kuloro or Sukuta concerns a total area of approximately
162 hectares of open tree-shrub savanna type. Agricultural and domestic activities are
present in both areas. With the clearance of the vegetation of the site, birds (and a few of
the characteristic mammals) linked to this type of habitat will probably colonise other
open shrub land located in the surroundings.
The noise charge linked to the construction of the project will probably be significant for
avifauna (and fauna) in these relatively quiet areas.
2.5.3.2. Effects during the phase of operation
Possible major effects on avifauna (fauna) of the phase of operation will be limited if appropriate
measures are taken to reduce the impact on the environment by the project. Appropriate
measures mean specific measures related to water withdrawal, air, noise, waste water and solids
emissions and also to the risk of pollution spreading by tidal action (Denton Bridge especially).
Synthesis
Effects on wildlife are predominantly linked to the construction phase of the project.
The most negative impact will probably be observed for Denton Bridge and to a lesser extent for
the other sites. Denton Bridge is located on the fringe of the proposed Ramsar site Tanbi
Wetland complex and has a vital role in the ecology of the wetland complex because of its
communication function. Denton Bridge constitutes the entry point of fish and invertebrates that
breed in the mangrove but live adult lives in the sea. How far the project will affect avifauna
migration or movements in Denton Bridge by clearance of natural habitats will relatively be
difficult to predict.
For the Bund Road site, the Kuloro and Sukuta sites, the birds (and a few of the characteristics
mammals) associated to this habitat will probably locate habitat in the surroundings that meet
their ecological requirements. For the airport area, the location will probably be more difficult
through the absence of the same type of habitat in the close surroundings.
SECTiON A - PARTIHI: IDENTIFICA TION, DESCRIPTION AND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  163



2.5.4.  Discussion of the effects in the context of the World Bank's Operational Policies
The Bank's policies on natural habitats (OP 4.04) stipulate among other things that:
(a) "the Bank does not support projects that (...) involve the significant conversion or
degradation of critical natural habitats";
(b) "wherever feasible, (...) projects are sited on lands already converted (excluding any lands
that (.. .) were converted in anticipation of the project)";
(c) 'for those cases where no feasible alternatives for the project and its siting in natural
habitats exist a comprehensive analysis demonstrates that overall benefits from the project
substantially outweigh the environmental costs".
From the impact assessment, it appears that a possible development at Denton Bridge is clearly
in conflict with these policies. In fact, the coastal strip is obviously a critical natural habitat
which would be degraded in a very significant way.
The policy quoted as (b) applies to the Bund Road site. The selected site is already degraded -
the technical feasibility of a restoration is dubious and restoring would certainly require huge
financial efforts.
The natural habitats of the 2 reservation sites (Sukuta and Kuloro) have since long been
converted to farmland.
The same statement to a lesser extent applies to the Airport site, although a spontaneous
restoration of the wooded savanna (due to the absence of major human influences) can be
observed at this site.
2.6.   Marine and coastal resources
2.6.1. Methodology
The potential impacts from the project on the coastal resources are very different with the
alternative locations. Only two out of the 5 possible locations are within the coastal zone area.
This is the Bund Rd. site and the Denton Bridge site. The three other sites will not create direct
effects on the coastal zone. Therefore, potential impacts on the coastal zone will be mainly
discussed for these two sites.
The three other sites may have indirect effects on the coastal zone and its resources. Indirect
effects can be caused mainly by increased port operations and maritime traffic, the construction
of upriver basic wharves and the construction of an interface between the Gambia River and the
SECTION A - PARTHII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  164



land at Mandinari which will create more traffic on sea and on land, and specifically in the
coastal zone area between the Tanbi Wetland Reserve and the Mandina mangrove area.
Although the potential impacts of the FEZ on the coastal zone may be restricted, the pressure on
the coastal zone is very high, mainly due to coastal erosion. Coastal erosion is so threathening to
the coastal area of The Gambia as a whole, that it is found necessary to discuss the potential
impacts of this coastal erosion on the FEZ development for the Bund Rd. site and the Denton
Bridge site.
2.6.2. Direct impacts from the Bund Rd. and Denton Bridge sites
Potential impacts which do not occur with the development of the FEZ but are generally present
with developments in the coastal zone are:
* impacts of dredging,
* impacts of (operational or incidental) oil or chemical spills,
impacts from coastal engineering works,
These are not expected to take place due to the absence of activities on the beach or off-shore in
sea, necessary for the development of the FEZ.
In this way, no impacts are expected to take place which will affect the marine biodiversity by:
* harming vulnerable marine communities (breeding locations, benthos populations, seagrass,
endangered species),
damaging/modifying relevant marine communities
* reducing or changing fish population.
Potential impacts on the coastal functions of these two sites are:
* natural habitat destruction,
* groundwater take-up enhancing saline water intrusion
* waterfront drainage
* emissions of the different industries and services
These are in fact not impacts on the coastal resources and are therefore discussed in the different
chapters on impact assessment related to surface water and land-use.
SECTiONA - PARTIJII: IDENTIFICA TION, DESCRIPTION AND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  165



2.6.3. Loss of spawning and feeding area
For one site, the Denton Bridge, some valuable mangrove and wetland will receive another
landuse by which this area loses its ecological function as spawning and feeding area. The effect
of this on the global Tanbi Wetland ecosystem is only relative in the sense that a percentage
surface is lost for these functions. However, this loss is irreversible and may only be mitigated
by compensation measures. Although the area at Bund Rd. is also within the Ramnsar delineation,
this surface has not a function for spawning and feeding for fish and estuarine life. Therefore, the
impact on the coastal resources is only expected to take place at the site of Denton Bridge.
2.6.4. Impacts from increased port operations and maritime traffic
FEZ development will lead to increased maritime traffic and port operations, independent from
the FEZ location. Assuming a yearly increase in export through FEZ of 30,000 tons (compared
to actual export of 70,000 tons), this will lead to a maximum increase of 1 ship (assuming a
mean tonnage of 1,000 tons per ship) each week. In 1994, there were about 16-18 ships calling
into the port of Banjul every month . A one ship increase will create a relative small contribution
to the emission of ship discharges and use of anti-fouling substances. The impact of this will be
negligeable.
Port operations are mainly dominated by import (yearly about 600,000 tons import and 70,000
tons export). An increase of 30,000 tons of export signifies a less than 5% increase of the global
port operations (import and export). Therefore, the impacts related to port operation such as
generation of waste, run-off of pollutants, will increase only to a very small extent. This impact
is therefore considered to be negligeable. These kind of impacts are also mainly related to import
and lesser to export.
If an increase in traffic would lead to enhancing the capacity of the port, connected to further
deepening access channels through dredging, then potential impacts may be significant, which is
not the case with the FEZ development.
2.6.5.  Impacts from basic wharves upriver
The Interface at Mandinari may be considered as an upriver wharf. Therefore, the same kind of
potential impacts on coastal resources are to be expected as these at Mandinari:
* erosion and accretion effects
increased traffic and hinder for passing fauna
eventual dredging for creating an approach channel
SECTiONIA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONANDASSESSMENTOF THEEXPECTEDIMPACTS  166



2.6.6.  Impacts from the interface at Mandinari
Establishing the interface at Mandinari will increase the traffic and eventually lead to the
dredging of a channel for approaching the harbour. The natural habitat consists of a sand-silt bay
without vegetation. Eventual establishment of facilities for the boats on the beach are not
considered to damage this natural enviromnent significantly. The coastal strip is only sparsely
used for agriculture. The sand dunes and mud flats are not suited and therefore, there is no risk in
agricultural abuse from this area. This risk may however exist for the mangrove borders
somewhat further from the coast.,
2.6.6.1. Erosion and accretion effects
Just like wooden protection which is installed to combat coastal erosion, the deposition of sand
after any (wooden) structure will occur, and thus also just behind the basic wharves which are
planned to be built upriver.
The model established during the 1994 EIA for the Third Banjul Port Project indicates that
bathymetric irregularities, either artificial or natural (piers for example) interact with the tidal
flow, causing pockets of low flow in the lee of these topographic disturbances. In these areas, the
disturbance of the tidal flow results in a higher siltation rate. The model indicates that narrow
elongated obstacles aligned in the current direction does not affect the siltation noticeably, but
any obstacle at an angle or perpendicular to the flow causes an increase of siltation in the lee of
the obstacle. The piles of the wharves do not interact significantly if all the piles are arranged in
a series of rows parallel to the current direction. If the rows of piles are at an angle relative to the
current, the flow is more disturbed and siltation increases around the wharf.
These effects are local and depend on the direction of the currents. A large area of the river bank
situated from Tanbi Wetland up to Pirang is very shallow up to 20 meters into the river.
Generally, deposition of sediments occur, and the construction of the wharves will not create
more than local changes in the bathymetry, once the construction is finished (for the construction
phase, see dredging).
2.6.6.2. Increased traffic and nuisanceforpassingfauna
If the interface connection would be established at Mandinari, it would increase the traffic and
consequently disturbing the function of the area as a corridor between Tanbi and Mandina
mangroves. As the new sand mine is actually being used, the most prominent effects of the
increased traffic have already taken place. The cumulative effect of it with the transport from the
interface will therefore be smaller, but still depend on the absolute frequency of the trucks that
will pass through the Mandinari village. Transport of agricultural products from upriver will
however organize itself during specific moments in the day (or even the week) when trucks will
load the specific goods and transport them to the FEZ, f.i. at the Airport Site. The number of
SECrrONA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONANDAASSESSMErTOF THEEXPECTEDIMPACTS  167



trucks to be expected is not known. The effect will be dependent on the timing of the transfers
during the daytime (not morning or late afternoon, which are the preferred activity moments for
wildlife). If the number of trucks will be less than five, a small nuisance effect can be expected.
Compared to the impact of the quarry functioning as a barrier for passing fauna, the increased
traffic related to the interface is expected to be small or negligeable depending on the time of the
transport and the number of trucks which are loaded.
2.6.6.3. Dredging
The Mandinari shoreline is very shallow. Nearshore, the depth of the Gambia River is less than
2m for about 2 km into the River. Then it suddenly drops sharply to depths up to 12m. The wider
shallow area is due to the Banjul island which creates a deposition of sand along the shoreline of
the Tanbi mangrove and further along the Mandinari shoreline. Creating an interface will
demand eventually a further deepening for creating an entrance channel towards the Mandinari
harbour, depending on the type of boats wanted at Mandinari. If larger canoes are aimed at, this
may not pose a problem as they are already using the Mandinari shoreline. If a channel is
needed, this will demand a continuous dredging of the entrance towards Mandinari beach. This
will have a direct impact on the benthic fauna present such as demersal fish, shrimp, etc.
Dredging will increase turbidity which will enlarge the area where impacts on demersal and
pelagic biota can be expected. From the EIA on the harbour of Banjul, effects of construction
dredging were calculated for mean currents of 0.6 m/s. It is expected that currents near
Mandinari f.i. are smaller. The calculations showed that a turbid plume will be reduced to
background turbidity levels (solid particulate matter concentrations of 100 mg/l) within a
distance of 50m from the dredge site.
The channel is not advised, but if needed, dredging impacts are expected to occur only locally in
an area of 10 to 50m around the dredged site, predominantly on the benthic fauna. No impacts
are expected on the Tanbi wetland, or the mangrove of Mandina Bolon.
2.6.7.  Potential impacts from coastal erosion on FEZ development
The Bund Road Site is just besides the Serekunda Highway, very near the Syd Radio Station
where dramatic erosion takes place. The site is only 50m from the coast. A study conducted by
Haskoning forecasts a shoreline retreat of 3-4 m every year without protective measures. This
poses a threat of affecting the site within 14 years after construction if no measures are taken to
protect the coast.
The Denton Bridge site is somewhat further from the coast but on the other hand also very near
the tidal influence, about 10Om. The same conditions apply to this site, although the expected
impact of the erosion on the site may be delayed due to the further distance from the shore.
SECTION A - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTION AND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  168



Forecasts by Haskoning predict a yearly shoreline retreat also ranging between 3 and 4 m. This
means an actual impact possibly 25 years after establishing the site.
2.6.8.  Conclusion
The impacts of coastal erosion on the FEZ development are expected to be more serious when
FEZ location would be at Denton Bridge or Bund Road then adversely the impacts of the FEZ
development on the coastal resources. Direct effects of the FEZ development on the coastal
resources are not expected. However, some indirect impacts may occur which are mainly related
to the establishment of basic wharves or the interface at Mandinari. These impacts are considered
to be small or negligeable when the necessary precautionary measures are taken.
2.7.   Land use
2.7.1. Methodology
Firstly, a comparison has been made between the legislative context and the proposed development
plan.
Secondly, a rather general, qualitative assessment of predicted impacts on land use and tourism
is given for the coastal sites and for the other 3 sites.
At all sites, except for the Airport , the Dept. of Physical Planning should make arrangements prior
to the actual preparation of any development. In fact, none of those sites has been designed for
industrial development by the Greater Banjul Land use Masterplan (Land use Plan 2000).
2.7.2.  Coastal sites
2.7.2.1. Land use
FEZ-development will not create adverse effects on human settlements, as the selected sites are
unsuitable for housing. The "squatters" using the Bund Polder area were granted only temporary
permnits to work out of the area. Therefore, no compensation would be necessary should the
Government ask these people tc leave the area. Further comments on this issue are discussed in
chapter 2.9.: effects on the socio-economic environment.
Altough the designed buildings are not very high, the completed industrial estate will certainly
have a visual impact in the flat Bund polder area.
2. 7.2.2. Tourism
- As pointed out in Part II, chapter 8.3., the development of Free Economic Zones is unlikely to
affect environmental quality at the important TDA (Tourism Development Area) .
SECTIONA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONANDASSESSMENTOFTHEEXPECTEDIMPACTS  169



- Quantification of the impacts on eco-tourism is difficult in the absence of any official statistics
concerning its current importance. However, knowledgeable sources in the industry estimate
that, for the past season, close to 400 tourists visited The Gambia exclusively for bird watching,
and another 1,000 included a visit to one of the bird watching sites in their itinerary. In addition,
an equivalent of about 50% of the above would visit the sites during the off-season. These
estimates are only for "organized" tours, and do not therefore include tourists who make private
arrangements to visit the sites.
- It is noteworthy that Tanbi Wetland is the favourite site for bird watchers, and its loss would
drastically reduce the already limited "products" that are available in the Gambian tourist market.
Nonetheless, the potential benefits of a successful FEZ far outweigh the potential "costs" arising
out of the loss of a limited area of Tanbi (Bund Road site) at this stage of The Gambia's
development. All the same, it is suggested that a very small proportion of the revenues from the
FEZ be set aside to develop other natural habitats, such as Tanji Bird reserve.
- Development at Denton Bridge site would be far more precarious in this respect. The bridge is
the main gate into the proposed Tanbi reserve. It is the point of departure of tourist excursions
into the Tanbi Reserve, up Oyster Creek to Lamin Landing, Mandinari Point and beyond, thus
sustaining a growing eco-tourism industry. The area, also a FEZ at Denton Bridge, would be
prejudicial to these activities. Furthermore, any pollution emanating from the FEZ, through tidal
action, would spread throughout the Tanbi Reserve ( for details see above, chapters 2.2., 2.3. and
2.6.).
2.7.3.  Inland sites
At the Airport site development would be entirely within the airport concession, causing no
incompatibility with planned land use .
At Kuloro and Sukuta the main item, linked to the required changes in land use, will be
compensation of the loss of farmland and resettlement . These issues will be discussed later on:
see chapter 2.9..
2.8.   Archeological, historical and cultural values
From the description of the baseline situation it appears that no places of archeological, historical
and cultural value occur at the 5 sites considered for FEZ development.
At Bund Rd. and Denton Bridge a few scattered shell mounds would possibly cease to exist with
the development of these sites.
SECTiONA - PARTHI.: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRPPPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  170



2.9.   "Man" and his social-economic living conditions
2.9.1. Methodology
The assessment of the impact on socio-economic living conditions is based on an analysis of the
existing conditions, an estimation of expected changes in land use, settlements, employment
possibilities and all types of socio-economic infrastructure. The results of interviews with local
people and their representatives were included to evaluate the expected changes as well as the
attitude of local people towards the most important tentencies.
This chapter evaluates the predicted economic effects of the TGP, the effects on living quality
(settlement, housing, ...) and the use of resources.
2.9.2.  Socio-economic effects at the coastal sites
In general, it can be stated that the positioning of these sites would not encourage squatters'
settlements or resettlement on or near the sites. This is because adjacent to the Bund Road site -
along the other side of the Banjul-Serrekunda highway - the cemetery and two structures have
been destroyed by the sea, the rest of the buildings are being threatened by sea erosion. The
other side of Bund Road site is bordered by swampland which environment is not conducive to
human settlement. Denton Bridge site has a similar story and the existing factories can help us
make a projection that there would not be any settlement because of the swampy nature of the
land and the gradual sea erosion. Although these areas might attract job seekers, it is unlikely
that they would cause social problems such as vagrancy or prostitution.
Bund Road
Settlement and compensation
-  There are only a few squatters (about three or four) at Bund Road who usually build
temporary shelters made of sticks and cardboard. Indeed these squatters are seasonal settlers
coming only during the dry season to sell oysters to motorists who use the Bund Road when
commuting to and from Banjul.
-  During the rainy season these temporary shelters are abandoned and the patches of arable
land in the area cultivated with millet, groundnuts or maize depending on what the handful of
non-resident farmers want to grow.
-  The oyster sellers and farmers occupy this area, which could legally be considered as state
land. Thus the subject of financial compensation does not apply. In the event of establishing
this area as FEZ, these people would automatically move out when serious activities
commence and thereby cease to benefit economically from the area.
SECTION A - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  171



Economic effects
- Although people will not settle in the area because of the nature of the land and the environment,
this use of area as a FEZ would attract workers from Banjul and Greater Banjul area especially
from the towns of New and Old Jeswang, Serre kunda , Kanifmg and Bakau.
-  If the land is reclaimed and industries built some people would seek employment thereby
alleviating the high unemployment stand in the country. Petty vendors (selling cigarettes,
food, drinks, etc. ) will also frequent the area to service the employees of the surrounding
vicinity.
- Although already part of Bund Road is presently used as a refuse dump, this could be
discouraged and land reclaimed as has happened in the Campama area. New dump sites could be
identified.
- With the building of industries the area will cease to be a haven for bird, thereby having a
negative impact on tourism as a good number of tourists visit The Gambia for bird watching.
Nonetheless, the effect on eco-tourism will be minimal if development is restricted to the
planned FEZ-zone near the highway, as is expected. (see also chapter 2.7.).
Transport effects
Both the construction and in particular the operation will lead to an increase in transport. Under the
assumption made in chapter 2.1. full operation at Bund Road could result in an increase of 1,000
cars and 10 trucks/day. Taking into account the actual traffic density along the coastal road, this
would result in an increase of about 7% which would not create services problems. Only in the
direction of the port, a minor traffic burden may be attributed to the project.
Denton Bridge
From the baseline study we concluded that the nature of the area makes it unsuitable for any
form of human settlement. Although some people are engaged in seasonal farming, oyster and
firewood collection, they do not occupy the land on a permanent basis. Indeed, occupancy of this
area is illegal without a lease permit from the Government (as in the case of the fish processing
plant and petrol station).
From the foregoing chapters we conclude that the expected environmental impacts as well as the
possible effects on eco-tourism of industrial development near Denton Bridge are so important
that the socio-economic implications are purely academic.
Transport effects will be in analogy to the effects described for Bund Road.
SECTiONA - PARTIll: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  172



2.9.3. Socio-economic effects at the inland sites
In the cases of the Airport, Sukuta and Kuloro sites, the surrounding areas have already been
established settlements or ownership and thus do not make room for resettlement or squatters.
We can presume that social negative impacts like prostitution, unemployment, vagrancy will be
minimal if it happens to come into existence. At present these named activities and occupations
exist mainly in the areas where hotels, nightclubs and bars are concentrated, that is in the TDA.
These project sites would attracts migrants ( both good and bad) only if the industries offer very
high wages that are easily comparable to those paid in other parts of the country.
Airport
Economic effects
The economic lives of the people in the environs of the airport area will be improved because of
the various jobs that the project would create. This in turn will further encourage other income
generating activities, e.g big and petty trading, transportation and entertainment.
Settlements and effects on housing
- With regard to the possible settlement of workers during construction phase , the experience
so far in The Gambia, with large infrastructural developments (such as the Banjul Seaport
extension) and for large commercial agricultural farms (such as Radville Farms), has been
that sizeable number of workers are picked up by trucks and transported in and out of the
work areas, daily. These workers generally live in the peri-urban areas of Banjul, commonly
known as The Greater Banjul Area. These peri-urban areas remain the dormitoy
towns/villages, in which people still live very much in communities. In these settings,
relationships remain generally close and are based largely on personal acquaintances. The
communities are headed by a religious leader, typically an hnam, with the Alkalo as the
village administrative head.
- The villages of Banjulding, Lamin, Makumbaya, Mandinari, Busumballa, and Old and New
Yundum encompass the Airport site. The main access road running from Banjul to the rest
of the Western Division and beyond branches at Yundum to the Airport.
