- Over the past years villages have expanded both in breath and depth largely due to a
relatively high rate of immigration of Gambians from the urban areas and from up country
and abroad. The establishment of the Airport area as a FEZ that will eventually include the
Site B area, as well as the implementation of the GCAA master plan will further attract
people to the surrounding villages, especially Lamin, Banjulding, and Yundum. Limited
interviews with residents indicate that wanting to occupy a house was the single most
SECTIONA - PARTIHI: IDENTIFICA TION, DESCRIPTIONANDASSESSMENTOF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  173



important reason for moving reflecting largely the urban characteristics of the three villages.
Housing was followed in declining order of importance by employment, land for fanning,
and better access to utilities. However, for a substantial proportion of those who made two
moves, employment was the most important reason for the first move, followed by housing,
land for farning, and other.
In summary, households tend to move to urban areas of Banjul and Serrekunda seeking
employment, then later on a second move changed addresses to the peri-urban areas on the
edge of the city in search of land for housing and/or farming. Secondly, households in the
immediate pen'-urban area were the most dynamic in terms of settlement.
The search for job opportimities in the FEZ will encourage people from Banjul, Greater
Banjul and the Western Division.
Traditionally, most compounds in the surrounding villages are occupied by family members
but there has been a gradual increase in people having tenancy status. This trend will
continue with the establishment of the FEZ especially at Site B of the area. Although there
are some improved structures (cement block building) with improved living conditions in
villages like Lamin and Banjulding for example, there are more of mud buildings catering for
people from the low income bracket.
The FEZ project will probably encourage compound owners to build better houses to attract
tenants.
Much of the surrounding and remaining farmland in the villages of Yundum, Lamin,
Banjulding, etc.... presently those owned by families will be sold and developed to supply
housing and residential properties to workers and their families in FEZ area at the airport.
This is likely to increase competition for the land, and to raise the general level of rent in the
area.
If these areas are not properly monitored and planned then it would lead not only to
overcrowding but congestion, poor sanitation and both water and air pollution.
Use of resources
The present airport has sufficient water and electricity supplies. The use of septic tanks
helps to take care of wastes from toilets which are regularly emptied by private sanitary
companies. Refuse is also collected by the municipal authorities and, more recently, by
private companies.
The implementation of the GGAA Master Plan and the FEZ area will require increased
supply of electricity and water to cater for the added industries and businesses..
SECTIONA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THEEXPECTED IMPACTS  174



NAWEC sub-station in Yumdum supplies electricity to Yundum Airport and small areas of
the surrounding villages of Yundum, Banjulding and Larnin. Indeed the rest of these
villages, Mandinari and Makumbaya are without mains electricity supply, and households
depend on alternative sources (such as candles and hurricane lamps) for lighting. Cooking is
done with firewood This has been the situation for years as demands from an ever increasing
population, coupled with NAWEC's supply constraints, render the electricity problem
almost intractable. The expansion of the airport, the implementation of the GCAA Master
Plan and the establishment of the FEZ will require increased electrical installations which in
turn would help provide this much needed facility to surrounding villages.
* NAWEC also supplies piped portable water from boreholes to the airport and selected areas
(mainly along the main roads) of the surrounding villages of Banjulding, Yundum and
Lamin. Private water connections are available from the system. There are also NGO-
sponsored hand-pumped wells in some of these villages (about 5 in Lamin and 2 in
Banjulding).
For Mandinari and Makumbaya, almost all the households depend on hand dug wells for
portable water. In most cases, the wells are maintained even where public standpipes exist,'4
for emergencies. The increase in population in these villages has led to a rise in the number
of wells in use and this has in turn lowered the groundwater level, which will be lowered
fiuther if more powerful water supply facilities such as boreholes are sunk. Even today
villagers complain that they have to dig deeper to get to the water and some wells dry up
during the dry season and have to be re-dug. However with more reliable water supply
many of these wells will be abandoned.
T There are no irrigatedfarms except for the lowlands that are flooded by the Abuko stream
during the rains, and the Government-sponsored Yundum Women's Vegetable Garden.
During the rainy season there is farming of groundnuts, maize and millet.
- The refuse from the airport complex and facilities, and the Radville horticultural products sorting
and storage plant are usually dumped in open sites within the airport premises with potential to
cause health hazards.
Like the airport, the surrounding villages of Yundum, Banjulding and Lamin do not have
designated refuse dump sites. Instead, refuse is collected and disposed of by individual
households within their compounds through burning or burial. The airport terminal building and
other structures within the airport premises and modem cement block buildings in Yundum,
Banjulding and Lanfin with piped water have modem toilets which empty into septic tanks. The
tanks are emptied by private companies with tankers, the contents of which are dumped in the
Kotu oxidation pond. After settlement, the waste water empties into four ponds which
14 Owing to the limited capacity these systems do not allow for private water connections.
SECTIONA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  175



eventually drain into the ocean. All other compounds in the area, which are in the great
majority, are serviced by pit latrines. Bathrooms comprise fenced enclosures which drain into
compounds and roads. These facilities work well for small households. On the other hand
overcrowded compounds are cause for concern as the pit latrines fill up fast and stagnant water
from bathrooms act as breeding grounds for mosquitoes.
Thus the increase in population which would result in the establishment of the FEZ would
increase health hazards if precautions were not taken to address the situation. One possible
solution would be the building of a sewage treatment plant as indicated the GCAA Master Plan.
Pressure on Abuko Nature Resene
Except for the land opposite the Abuko Nature Reserve, which is cultivated by women both during
the rainy and dry seasons, occupancy of the land around this place is almost completed.
Although the reserve is owned and managed by the government, it is reported that villagers make
attempts to encroach. Some of the land surrounding the Reserve is still vacant and it is possible that
the increase in the work force of the FEZ at the airport will lead to an increase in the demand for
and the construction of new houses in the area.
The Abuko stream, which runs through the reserve to the fields across the road, provides enough
water to "irrigate" the women's rice and sweet potatoes fields in the rainy season, and vegetable
gardens in the dry season.
Tourists, school children and other visitors, which population will increase with the rise in the
number of flights to The Gambia and increased activities at the FT Z, usually frequent the Abuko
Nature Reserve.
Transport effects
In chapter 2.1, an assumption was made on the expected transport increase. In particular in the
phase of operation, a substantial increase is expected (per day about 3,000 cars and 30 trucks).
This would mean that traffic at the airport road (actually 3,483 units/day) would almost double.
Apart from the noise effects described in chapter 2.4., this is, however, not expected to lead to a
burden, given the capacity of the road. Only at the port of Banjul, the increase in traffic (export
increase) could create some additional burden.
Kuloro
- The establishment of a FEZ on this site will lead to loss of farmland (groundnuts, millet, and
maize) resulting in the need :for some compensation to be paid to landowners. As in the case
of Tubakuta, the local inhabitants will be deprived of their livelihood. However the FEZ if
properly planned could improve the lives of the people and might bring amenities like
electricity, pipe borne water from boreholes, proper sites for the disposal of solid wastes
disposal.
SECTIONA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATlION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  176



- Employment opportunities will be created for the people not only of Kuloro, but Toubakuta,
Brikama, Pirang and beyornd. This in turn will encourage the mushrooming of settlements
around the FEZ area thereby providing the required workforce that should sustain the
institutions and businesses in the area. With better income many more permanent and durable
houses will be constructed.
- Government and the private sector will encourage better health and educational facilities
which are likely to attract people from the neighbouring villages and from afar.
At present the establishment of the main road has led to the increase of the population and
the expansion of villages such as Kuloro and Mandina Ba. With the establishment of the
FEZ, trading activities could also. Tourism and tourism related activities could be
encouraged especially with the help of the river.
The increase in transport is not expected to create a burden, taking into account the capacity
of the roads and the actual low traffic density.
A clear disadvantage of the establishment of FEZ is that it would lead to the dispossession of
farmland from households with little alternative forms of income. In that case, cash
compensation may not be sufficient.
Sukuta
Financial compensation to people in this area is likely to be huge, particularly for residents to
the north of the new Kombo Coastal road. This is likely to be the case for landowners of the
rest of the land (to the south of the highway), which is farmland with heavy investments in
tres crops in some areas.
Although one could try to spell out possible compensation, it is difficult to even estimate the
value of the land ( see also Appendix 4.3. for details) and it would be the task of a quantity
surveyor to do this. Moreover I believe that giving an estimated compensation would be
grossly misleading, because whereas an individual might ask for an exorbitant sum for a
piece of his/her land or property, a quantity surveyor or government might offer a sum of
money that the owner might reject. A case in point in The Gambia is the proposed payments
that are presently being offered by government to people whose houses and property would
be affected by the Westfield- Mandinaba road that is due soon.
- Depending of the type of industries put in place, the establishment of a FEZ will create jobs
for the inhabitants of Old and New Yundum, Sukuta, Nema Kunku, Welligara, Madina Sey
Kunda, Banjulnding and Brufut. Although most of the farms are services by hand-dug wells,
boreholes exist is some of the larger farms, and two NAWEC Pumping stations border the
site to the west. The FEZ could improve facilities and create jobs.
SECTIONA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATIONi, DESCPJPTIONAND ASSESSMENTOF THEEXPECTEDIMPACTS  177



- The new Kombo Coastal road the soon to be expanded Banjul- Mandina Ba Highway will
make the area much more easily accessible and will firther encourage settlements and
resettlement of the work force into the area.
- NAWEC will supply electricity as it does to most areas in the Banjul and Greater Banjul
areas. With the establishment of the FEZ other facilities like schools, clinics and telephones
will be put in place. Its proximity to the Airport, the Hotel industrial areas and the fishing
villages Brufut, Gunjur and Gunjur will help to boost tourism and create more jobs for the
people.
- Although the disposal of solid wastes is done individually e.g the existence of pit latrines
and the burning or burying of refuse finner arrangements could be made with companies of
sewage treatment plants could be put in place.
- According to the local people, activities of the FEZ must ensure that the industries do not
pollute the air or the soil that would eventually affect the health of humans and the
surrounding vegetation.
- The increase in transport is not expected to create problems given the capacity of the roads
and the actual traffic density which is quite low.
2.9.4.  Human Health and safety
The most important effects potentially affecting human health are the deterioration of air quality,
adverse effects in the quality of drinking water, inproper waste disposal and noise. In order to
identify the potential health impacts reference will be made to the changes or effects to be
expected in the abiotic environment. As far as air pollution is concerned, a slight increase of the
levels of SO2, NOx, TSP and CO is expected during operation. National and international
guidelines for protecting health will however not be approached so that no pressure on human
health is to be expected. During construction dust emissions may occur, resulting to some
nuisance in the immediate vicinity of the construction works. Proper mitigation measures for
preventing dust emissions should minimize those effects. The project is not expected to lead to a
substantial deterioration of drinking water quality. However, as indicated in the chapters on
ground water and surface water, measures should be foreseen in particular to prevent ground
water pollution. During construction and during operation noise levels will slightly increase.
Based upon the calculated levels to be expected it is unlikely that noise burden will take place at
a distance of 200 m from the project site. Some preventive mitigation measures should however
be applied (see mitigation measures for noise).
In general the burden on human health is expected to be low; furthermore no differences within
this respect are expected to occur between the different sites.
SECTiONA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONANDASSESSMENTOFTHEEPECTEDIMPACTS  178



As far as safety is concerned, from the (general) lay out of the project there does not appear to be
major risks justifying contingency plans. Indeed, the industries to be set up (i.e. small food
industries, electronic industries, warehousing ...) are not known to provoke major safety risks
taking into account the limited (if any) use of dangerous components and the absence of complex
(chemical) processes. In view of the lack of any further detail on the industries to be set up, no
more detailed analysis of safety risks can be carried out.
Anyhow taking into account the absence of inhabitants nearby the different sites, safety effects
on the neighbouring communities are not expected for any of the sites.
During construction safety risks for workers related to any construction work may exist.
However, since no further infonnation on the construction method nor the materials to be used is
available it is not possible to go into further detail.
SECTIONA - PARTIII: IDENTIFICATION, DESCRIPTIONAND ASSESSMENT OF THE EXPECTED IMPACTS  179






PARTIV
MITIGATION MEASURES
The mitigation measures, as part IV of the Environmental Impact Assessment, are presented here in
a concise way. We refer to section B for more details ("Environmental management plan, mitigation
and monitoring plan").
1. AIR
Construction phase
- water spraying to the surface to prevent dust formation and dispersion;
-  rinsing of tires of trucks when leaving the construction area to prevent distribution of dust;
- installation and control of speed limits in the construction zone;
-  elaboration of a working schedule so that timing of different operations matches in order to
prevent large areas being subjected to dust dispersion;
- foresee in the development of a paved road system before erecting houses and other
constructions in town development and industrial development areas.
Phase of operation
- creation of a buffer zone (200m) industrial sites and residential sites;
- industries likely to provoke nuisance such odour nuisance, and dust should be situated at the
far end (away from residential areas) of the industrial estate;
- development of emission standards for industrial installations;
- a monitoring point should be establised to control air quality in the surroundings of the
development.
SECTIONA - PARTIV: MITIGA TION MEASURES                                     180



2.   WATER
2.1.   Groundwater
Most of the mitigation measures proposed to reduce the impact on groundwater quality are at the
same time measures to reduce the impact on soil quality.
Measures during the construction phase:
- reduction of accidental spills through preventive measures, to be specified when
construction methods are known;
Measures durine the operation phase:
- reduction of leaks, spills and losses through preventive measures, to be specified
when operation and storage methods are known;
- siting of liquid product storage tanks on an impervious surface with a low wall to
collect possible spills;
- siting of process unit areas on impervious surfaces to control product spills or losses;
- siting of solid waste collection zones on impervious surfaces;
- avoiding placement of storage tanks, process units and solid waste collection zones in
the near surroundings of hand-dug wells;
- issuing permits with specified effluent standards for discharging to land;
- quality control of industrial effluents;
-  monitoring groundwater quality.
2.2.   Surface water
Measures during the construction phase:
-   reduction of accidental spills to avoid contamination of runoff water.
Measures during the operation phase:
- reduction of spills during production, transport, loading and unloading to avoid
contamination of runoff water;
- providing efficient waste water treatment;
- quality control of industrial effluents;
-  issuing permits with specified effluent standards for discharging in saline waters;
SECTIONA - PARTIV: MITIGATIONMEASURES                                            181



- monitoring quality of saline water receiving discharged waste water.
3.   SOIL AND WASTE
Most of the mitigation measures proposed to reduce the impact on soil quality are also measures
to mitigate impact on groundwater.
Measures during the construction phase:
- reduction of accidental spills through preventive measures, to be specified when
construction methods are known;
Measures during the operation phase:
- reduction of leaks, spills and losses through preventive measures, to be specified
when operation and storage methods are known;
siting of liquid product storage tanks on an impervious surface with a low wall to
collect possible spills;
- siting of process unit areas on impervious surfaces to control product spills or losses;
- siting of solid waste collection zones on impervious surfaces;
- issuing permits with specified effluent standards for discharging to land;
- quality control of industrial effluents;
- introduction of quality standards for soil.
4.     NOISE
A proper zoning should be elaborated:
a buffer zone of at least 200m should be foreseen between the borders of the industrial
estates-area and the residential area; these buffer zones should be planted with vegetation
leading to a reduction in noise;
the installations producing most noise should be erected at the largest distance from
residential areas and other noise-sensitive areas;
the installations producing most noise should be protected by a favourable implantation with
regard to other buildings
SECTioN A - PART IV: MITIGA TION MEASURES                                        182



5.  FAUNA EN FLORA
The following items concerning mitigation of negative effects on vegetation and natural habitats
will be worked out in Section B:
- Appropriate landscaping of the project sites
- Careful engineering design of the possible widening of Bund Road
- Compensation for a part of the proposed Ramsar site, Tanbi Wetland Complex
- Improvement of the buffer zone around Abuko Nature Reserve
- Preventive actions against encroachment on Forest Parks in the study area
No mitigation measures can be proposed for development of the coastal area near Denton
Bridge. A compensating measure would be required but a zone of similar habitat and ecological
importance is hard to find in the coastal zone of The Gambia.
6.   LAND USE, ARCHEOLOGICAL, HISTORICAL AND CULTURAL
RESOUCES
The land use Plan 2000 has to be updated to provide a legal basis for development at all
nominated sites (except the Airport site).
7.   MARINE AND COASTAL RESO>URCESI
General mitigation measures are described in Section B of this report:
* precautions concerning dredging;
* monitoring in Tanbi Wetlands
* monitoring of sea water quality
* human resources development, eduction and sensitization

PARTV
KNO WLED GE GAPS AND UNCERTAINTIES IN
ASSESSMENT
Since most of the components of the Trade Gateway Project are in the planning or feasibility
phase, it is obvious that the description of the project elements is not available in a sufficient
degree of detail to allow the drafting of a detailed Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA).
The lack of detailed data for both the construction phase and the phase of operation is a major
reason for uncertainty in assessment leading to rather general assessment without quantification
possibilities in some instances.
The major additional gaps in knowledge and uncertainties in assessment for each of the
environmental issues studied are listed below.
11. AIRI
- in general the lack of any description of the construction phase and operation capacities
precludes quantification of emissions;
- immission data are very scarce;
- emission data from actual industrial activities are lacking.

Groundwater

- information on groundwater abstraction during operation phase;
- the amount of groundwater needed during operation is not known.
Surface water
-  a detailed description of process operations and waste water treatment is lacking to
estimate waste water emissions more accurate;
- it is not known weather waste water will be discharged into surface water or to land

be monitored to make assessment of impacts and finally protection of these communities
possible. Also monitoring of seawater quality is very sparse.

PART VI
FINAL ASSESSMENT OF THE PROJECTAND
COMPARISON OF THE AL TERNA TIVES
Upon identification of the potential impacts, an assessment has been carried out in order to
determine the magnitude, extent and significance of the impacts, taking into account special
sensitivities of the regions concerned. It should be recognized that the present impact study is
general in nature and that a number of gaps in knowledge preclude a detailed analysis of a
number of impacts or lead to uncertainties in assessment. The major drawback is the lack of a
sufficient degree of detail on the project description.
Within this view and for reasons of clearness of the comparisons the impacts have been
classified according to the following schedule:
o    :      no effect of significance or no relevance
+    :      slight positive effect: this is an improvement of the existing situation for a specific
impact with limited magnitude, extent and significance
++          moderate positive effect: this is a significant improvement of the existing situation
for a specific impact leading to surpassing the criteria used and characterised by a
clear magnitute or extent
slight negative effect: this is a deterioration of the situation for a specific impact
without surpassing the criteria set; the impact can generally be mitigated and is
reversible or limited in extend and magnitude
moderate negative effect: this is a deterioration of the situation for a specific
impact giving raise to surpassing the criteria used: it is characterised by a clear
magnitude or extent; however mitigation may lower the effect
high significant negative effect: this is a significant deterioration of the situation
for a specific impact characterised by a large magnitude and extent
very important negative effect: this is a significant deterioration of the situation
for a specific impact characterized by a large magnitude and extent and
irreversible in nature without mitigation possibilities.
The assessment has been carried out in a quantitative way as far as possible and the assessment
criteria include Gambian standards, international guidelines, environmental quality objectives,
SECTION A - PART VI: FINAL ASSESSMFNT OF THE PROJECTAND COMPARISON OF THE ALTERNATIVES  187



loss of surface, modifications etc. The criteria used have been outlined in each chapter of the
environmental impact study.

As far as air pollution is concerned, it is expected that in the vicinity of the project air pollution
will slightly increase. Some dust may be emitted during construction while operation will lead to
an input of in particular S02, NOx, TSP and CO. The effect will, however, be low and can be
mitigated to a large extent. When comparing the location alternatives proposed, there does not
appear to be a significant difference with regard to air pollution impact, background levels being
equally low and meteorological conditions being similar.
During the construction phase, it is not expected that impacts of significance will occur with
respect to ground water or surface water.
Potential lowering of the groundwater table will be temporary and the radius of influence will be
rather limited in all sites. Because of the local character of potential ground water pollution
during construction and the absence of hand-dry wells in the immediate surroundings of any of
the sites, there is no risk of contamination of ground water supply.
SECTION A -PART VI: FINAL ASSESSMFNT OF THE PROJECTAND COMPARISON OF THE ALTERNATIVES  188



During operation existing water supply can be used for all sites, exception made for the Kuloro
site, where a stand-alone borehole should be foreseen. Provided preventive measures are taken,
the risk for ground water pollution will be limited. However, proper treatment of waste water
will have to be put in operation when waste water discharges become significant. Infiltration of
waste water and resulting ground water pollution may be considered to be more significant at the
Sukuta site in comparison to the other site, since this site is situated in a well field.
Surface water pollution during operation can only occur at the coastal sites of Bund Road and
Denton Bridge. Even when treated waste water effluents will affect significantly the water
quality of the receiving waters of the mangrove ecosystem. At Denton Bridge, further spreading
and pollution of sea water is likely to occur, however, dilution will limit significance of this
effect.
The assessment made for groundwater also applies to soil. Minor effects are expected during
operation. Attention should be paid, however, to the stability issue at the coastal sites.
Since all sites are rather flat, no significant erosion risks are expected to occur.
In particular during operation, a considerable amount of waste may be generated. It will have to
be stored and collected in a proper way. When a dumping site is constructed, precautionary
measures should be taken to prevent effects on soil and groundwater. In that case and taking into
account that the quantity of waste is not assumed to surpass 3.000 Tons/year the effect is
considered to be slightly negative for all sites.
When properly managed, waste should not be a major sources of concern in any of the sites.
During construction some intermittent noise output may be expected, without creating a burden
of significance. During operation noise will be produced from industrial activities and from
increased traffic. Based upon our calculations it is not expected that noise contribution from
industrial activities will be significant beyond 200 m from the FTZ border. Traffic will increase
noise levels with 0.5 up to 3 dB(A). Although the expected noise output during the phase of
operation is limited, due to the actual elevated levels at the airport the guideline value of 55
dB(A) may be surpassed. This will not be the case at the other sites.
Industrial development of the coastal area near Denton Bridge would result in inadmissible,
important negative effects on the coastal ecosystem.
The impact will be less important at Bund Road, where an already degraded part of Bund Polder,
adjacent to the highway, will be cleared for site preparation. In comparison with the three purely
terrestrial sites, the possible impact of accidental pollution on the vegetation is more important at
this site.
Generally, there is no significant impact to be expected on the seawater quality or the marine
resources. There is a moderate negative effect on the marine resources at the Denton Bridge site.
SECTiON A - PART VI: FINAL ASSESSMENT OF THE PROJECTAND COMPARISON OF THE ALTERNA TI YVES  189



Mangrove and wetland will have another landuse by which this area loses its ecological function
as spawning and feeding area. T he effect of this on the global Tanbi Wetland ecosystem is only
relative in the sense that a percentage surface is lost for these functions. Other effects are
expected to have no significance or relevance or only a slight negative one. The seawater quality
will not be changed due to the FEZ development, with the exception of the Denton Bridge site,
where some emissions from f.i. fish processing factories may end up through tidal action in the
coastal zone, the Oyster River and its tributaries. Other effects are similar for each site, as they
are produced indirectly through the development of the interface at Mandinari or the basic
wharves upriver. These structures may create erosion and sedimentation effects in a local range
around the developments.
The impacts of coastal erosion on the FEZ development are expected to be more serious when
FEZ location would be at Denton Bridge or Bund Road then adversely the impacts of the FEZ
development on the coastal resources. However, some impacts may occur which are mainly
related to the loss of spawning and feeding area, emission of fish processing facilities, the
construction of an interface at Mandinari and the construction of basic wharves upriver. These
impacts are considered (with the exception for Denton Bridge) to be small or negligeable when
the necessary precautionary measures are taken. Conclusive, Denton Bridge is not advised from
the viewpoint of marine and coastal resources. Other sites will create only an indirect slight
negative effect.
At Yundum airport an island of regenerating woodland will be destroyed for FEZ development.
Indirect effects on Abuko Nature Reserve could arise when the industrial site would attract
settlers in the Lamnin area.
At Kuloro and Sukuta the actual ecological value of the shrub savanna is lower. Anyhow,
clearance of this vegetation would reinforce habitat fragmentation. Moreover, both sites still
have a good potential for reforestation.
Most effects on (avi)fauna are linked to the construction phase of the project. The most negative
impact would probably be observed at Denton Bridge, fewer and less negative impacts are
predicted at the other sites.
For the Bund Road site, the Airport area, the Kuloro and Sukuta sites, the birds (and a few of the
characteristics mammals) associated to this habitat will probably locate habitat in the
surroundings that meet their ecological requirements. For the airport area, the location will
probably be more difficult through the absence of the same type of habitat in the close
surroundings.
Denton Bridge on the fringe of the proposed Ramsar site Tanbi Wetland Complex has a vital role
in the ecology of the wetlands because of its communication function. Denton Bridge / Oyster
Creek constitutes the entry point of fish and invertebrates that breed in the mangrove but live
adult lives in the sea. Furthermore, this coastal strip is particularly important for migrant
SECTION A -PART VI: FINAL ASSESSMENT OF THE PROJECTAND COMPARISON OF THE ALTERNA TIVES  190



waterfowls and for African waterbirds. The destruction of this imnportant coastal habitat would
probably be difficult to compensate by another one on The Garnbia territory. Consequently, the
selection of this site would better be avoided .
Both Bund Road and Denton Bridge sites are unsuitable for human settlement. Development would
not require any compensation of illegal settlement or present activities. At Kuloro and Sukata on the
other hand, the loss of farmland and residences would involve huge financial compensation of the
residents. Finally, no settlements exist at the airport site which is owned by the Gambia Civil
Aviation Authority.
Industrial development at Denton Bridge could create adverse effects on a growing eco-tourism
industry as this site is the main gate and point of departure of tourist excursions into the Tanbi
reserve.
The project most probably would create various jobs and improve economic conditions for a lot
of people. The beneficial impacts seem most obvious at the airport site: the socio-economic
study revealed a variety of expected improvements with regard to employment, housing,
agricultural activities,... Furthermore, there are good reasons to accept that FEZ development
would further attract people to the surrounding villages, especially to Lamin, Banjulding and
Yundum. This in turn could create an increasing pressure on Abuko Nature Reserve.
As far as human health is concerned, both some rather positive and slightly negative effects may
be expected. These will be approximately the same for all sites. The increasing economic activity
may be accompagnied by an increase in income as such indirectly leading to better health
conditions. On the other hand, the project will lead to some air emissions and an increase in
noise levels nearby the sites. Although these impacts are expected to be low, some burden is not
to be excluded totally.
CONCLUSION
Prior to this EIA, the Trade Gateway Project Task Force clearly selected two sites as the most
favourable with regard to Free Zone development in the short or medium term.
Indeed, from an initial comparative analysis made as part of a feasibility study it was concluded
that the two sites worthy of primary consideration are the Bund Road and Airport sites. The
remaining sites were considered to be unsuitable in the short or medium termn, due to
environmental sensitivity, location, lack of infrastructure or issues of resettlement, or a
combination of these factors.
Nonetheless, these reservation sites (Denton Bridge, Kuloro and Sukuta) have been considered
in the environmental assessment of the TGP. All environmental items have been described albeit
in a more general way.
When the comparison of all alternatives is made again after a full environmental impact assessment
of the proposed project elements , the following conclusions can be drawn:
SECTIONA -PART VI: FINAL ASSESSMENT OF THE PROJECTAND COMPARISON OF THE ALTERNATIVES  191



(1)  Apart from moderate effects on habitat and wildlife, solid waste production and noise, the
Airport site appears to be a very suitable place for FEZ development. The assessment
shows minor effects in the other environmental compartments, while a significant
improvement of the social and economic conditions of local communities may be
expected. The fenced land is owned by the Gambia Civil Aviation Authority, so that no
compensations of private landowners is required prior to project development.
Mitigation measures concerning solid waste handling and noise have to be considered
carefully. The loss of a rather undisturbed woodland habitat is irreversible. However,
sufficiently large strips of the existing woodland can easily be saved all around the
construction area. It may be expected that woodland birds will use this buffer zone as
stepping stone to other forest areas.
(2) The Bund Road site lies within the boundaries of a proposed RAMSAR site but its current
environmental quality is clearly degraded. Clearance of the remaining vegetation would
not have a major influence on the ecological value of Tanbi Wetland, though the
opportunity to use this strip of land as a natural buffer zone between the Banjul-Serrekunda
highway and the core of the future reserve is lost when development will take place (=
moderately negative effect of changed land use). This effect can be mitigated by foreseeing
a scrub buffer zone between the industrial estate and the rest of the polder. A slightly
negative effect on eco-tourism will probably be limited to the construction phase.
Furthermore, waste water effluents of an operational FEZ could affect the water quality of
the Bund Polder channels. Even if the present water quality is bad (mainly through
discharges from Banjul city), the possible effects of surface water pollution deserves
special attention.
In conclusion, this alternative is suitable for FEZ development at short distance from the
city and port of Banjul. There will be no need for compensation or resettlement of private
owners and the project would not involve the conversion of a critical natural habitat.
(t) With regard to industrial development at Denton Bridge, the conclusion of the
environmental assessment is the most negative of all. Inadmissible effects on the coastal
ecosystem are predicted . Moreover, an industrial estate nearby the main starting point for
tourist excursions into Tanbi Wetland could have a permanent negative effect on eco-
tourism. Summarizing, it may be stated that the expected conversion and degradation of
this northern part of Tanbi Wetland Complex excludes this alternative as a possible FEZ-
site if environmental considerations are taken into account.
(4) Both Kuloro and Sukuta sites are flat and well drained areas. The available surface is
largely sufficient for development and - apart from groundwater pollution risk in the
Sukuta well field area - only slightly negative environmental effects are expected.
Consequently, these sites may rightly be considered as suitable reservation sites for future
SECTIONA -PART VI: FINAL ASSESSAfENT OF THE PROJECTAND COMPARISON OF THE ALTERNATIVES  192



development. On the other hand, the potential for reforestation is equally high. A clear
disadvantage of the establishment of a FEZ area would be the dispossession of farmland
and huge financial compensation of many private owners of residences, especially at
Sukuta. The low feasibility of project development in these two areas is thus primarily
based on socio-economic considerations, including the absence of basic infrastructure (e.g.
water supply) at Kuloro.

SECTION B
ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT, MITIGATION AND
MONITORING PLAN
1.   AIR QUALITY AND NOISE
Apart from some elevated concentrations of particulates in heavily crowded areas with dense
traffic, air quality in The Gambia is good. The Trade Gateway Project, as it is conceived, is not
expected to result in a significant contribution to air pollution. Indeed, emissions estimated from
energy provision, industrial activities and traffic appear to be quite low. As a consequence, the
impact on air quality will not be important.
Nevertheless, any deterioration of this valuable environmental resource should be prevented. To
that aim, below a number of mitigation measures are provided along with some management and
monitoring issues, the latter rather for general use in The Gambia than specifically for the Trade
Gateway Project.
Air quality management in relation to the project should be focussed on the following issues:
- mitigation of emissions;
- regulatory control;
- monitoring.
a. Description of the measures to be foreseen
Mitigating air pollution
1. Measures during the construction phase
-  water spraying to the surface to prevent dust formation and dispersion;
- rinsing of tires of trucks when leaving the construction area to prevent distribution of dust;
- installation and control of speed limits in the construction zone;
- elaboration of a working schedule so that timing of different operations matches in order to
prevent large areas being subjected to dust dispersion;
SECTION B: ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT, MITIGATIONAND MONITORING PLAN           194



- foresee in the development of a paved road system before erecting houses and other
constructions in town development and industrial development areas.
2. Measures for operation
- creation of a buffer zone between industrial and residential sites
-  industries likely to provoke nuisance such as odour nuisance and dust should be situated at
the far end (away from residential areas) of the industrial estate;
Regulatory control
Immission standards are actually being endorsed in The Gambia. However emission standards
for air pollutants are still lacking. In view of an increase in industrial activities it is advised that
emission standards are established for the major air pollutants:
- S02
- NO,
- TSP (PM IO)
- co
Monitoring
An air quality monitoring point should be established according to the following terms:
-  site: 0.5-1 km north of the industrial estate border
- substances to be monitored: SO2, NOx, PM<10, CO
- monitoring frequency: monthly during 24hr
- other recordings: wind speed, wind direction
b. Estimation of the environmental impact reduction
The measures during the construction phase will considerably reduce dust formation and dust
emissions and as such prevent nuisance to residents in the neighbourhoud. The measures
foreseen during operation are also intended at reducing possible nuisance to residents.
SECTIONB: ENVIRONMENTAL MANA GEAfENT PLAN, MITrGATIONAND MONITORING PLAN      195



c. Responsibilities and costs
The mitigation measures foreseen should be enforced to the companies erecting the industrial
facilities. The mitigating measures foreseen are good practice measures and will not lead to extra
costs.
Regulation and monitoring are tasks of the Government and more in particular of the National
Environment Agency
For the development of emission norms technical assistance may be needed (this could also
provide a basis for the elaboration of different types of emission norms). The cost is estimated at
50,000 USD.
Since the cost for installing and operating of an air quality monitoring station appears to be too
high in relation to the significance of the impact of air pollution to be expected, it is advised to
include it in the monitoring programme actually being carried out. An additional cost of 20,000
USD/year can be put forward.
2.   WATER RESOURCES
The main goal of the surface water quality management plan is to set a framework for the
development of the Trade Gateway Project, which guarantees the protection of the surface water
quality, i.e. negative impacts are at an acceptable level and no irreversible damage is caused.
2.1    Description of measures to be foreseen
Mitigation measures
Measures during the construction phase:
- reduction of accidental spills to avoid contamination of runoff water.
Measures during the operation phase:
-  reduction of spills during production, transport, loading and unloading to avoid
contamination of runoff water;
-  providing efficient waste water treatment;
-  quality control of industrial effluents;
-  issuing permits withi specified effluent standards for discharging into saline water;
-  monitoring quality of saline water receiving discharged waste water.
SECTION B: ENVIRONMENTAL MANA GEMENTPLAN, MITIGATION AND MONITORING PLAN      196



Regulatory control
Water quality standards for saline waters are available. Nevertheless, there is a whole range of
common (toxic) parameters, which are not covered by the present list of quality standards such
as mineral oil, heavy metals, monocyclic aromatic hydrocarbons and chlorinated hydrocarbons.
It is recommended to complete standards with some of these parameters.
General industrial effluent standards for discharging of effluents in saline waters are not available.
According to the "Environmental Discharge (Permitting) Regulations, 1999", processes with a
potential for pollution, have to be carried out in accordance with those regulations and are not
allowed without registering with and obtaining a discharge permit from the National Environmental
Agency. If a permit is issued, the maximum allowable quantity or concentration of discharge of
liquid waste will be specified as well as monitoring requirements and environmental management
measures to be undertaken.
Fish processing is considered as a process with a potential for water pollution. The following values
can be used as a guideline to set effluent standards for some parameters:
- BOD: 25 mg/l
- COD: 150 mg/l
- oil and grease : not visually detectable
With regard to electronics and light, TV and computer assembly, concentrations below can be used
as a guideline:
-pH: 6.5-9
- BOD: 25 mg/I
-COD: 120mg/l
- chlorinated hydrocarbons : not detectable
Waste water treatment
As mentioned before, a biological treatmnent plant will have to be installed if the quantity of
waste water discharged exceeds the amount of 30,000 m3 a year. The most appropriate system
for treatment of the expected waste water is an activated sludge system, consisting of an aeration
basin and a decantor with sludge recirculation. Incoming wastewater can be pretreated by means
of a bar screen, or rack, a gril remover, a fat trap or a primary settler to remove coarse solids
which decant easily.
Investment costs for a treatment plant with a capacity of 30,000 m3 a year, including:
- preliminary preparations and engineering studies;
- waste water treatment: pumps, screens, sand trap, activated sludge basin, decantor
- infractructure for services
SECTIONB: ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENTPLAN, MITIGATIONANDMONITORING PLAN          197



- sludge treatment infrastructure: digestor, thickener, gas holder, sludge dewatering
- piping, electricity
will be in the order of magnitude of 120,000 USD.
Operation and maintenance costs will be in the range of 17,000 to 25,000 USD a year.
Monitoring
monitoring of effluent:
- fish processing: - frequency: monthly
- paramneters : flow rate, temperature, pH, BOD, COD, nitrate,
nitrite, ammonia, phosphate
-  electronics/assembly:: - frequency monthly
- parameters : flow rate, temperature, pH, BOD, COD,
chlorinated hydrocarbons, heavy metals
monitorinn of saline mangrove water:
-  frequency: monthly
-  parameters: temperature, pH, dissolved oxygen, BOD, COD, nitrate, nitrite,
ammonia, phosphate, heavy metals
2.2    Estimation of the environmental impact reduction
Avoiding pollution of runoff water, waste water treatment before discharging and quality control
of discharging into surface water guarantees an acceptable surface water quality in future times.
2.3    Responsibilities and costs
Measures to be taken to reduce leaks, spills and losses during construction and operation should
be enforced to the companies carrying out the activities. Waste water treatment and monitoring
of the quality of liquid waste discharged into water, should be carried out by the involved
companies as well. If the total amount of waste water discharged exceeds 30,000 m3 a year,
installation of a biological treatment plant will be necessary. Provided waste water from
different plants undergoes appropriate pretreatment, one treatment plant would be sufficient for
the whole of the project.
SECTioNB: ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENTPLAN, MITIGATIONAND MONITORING PLAN         198



Present "Water Quality Standards for Saline Waters" have been established by the National
Environmental Agency. Setting standards for some additional parameters is a government
responsibility as well.
According to the "Environmrental Quality Standards Regulations, 1999", the National
Environmental Agency is responsible for implementation of the standards. The Department of
Water Resources is responsible for monitoring. The industrial exploiter has to pay for the controls.
With regard to monitoring of saline waters, samples will be taken at tourist beaches, offshore and at
fishing villages. Mangroves are not included. In case waste water is discharged into mangroves,
mangrove water quality will have to be monitored by the National Environmental Agency as well.
costs: monitoring effluent: fish processing: ca. 3,000 USD/year
electronics : ca. 3,000 USD/year
monitoring saline water: ca. 5,000 USD/year
effluent treatment : depending on the type and production capacity of the plants to be
operated
3.   GROUNDWATER AND SOIL, INCLUDING WASTE
There is no major impact on soil and groundwater to be expected. Nevertheless, the proposed
activities of The Trade Gateway Project may have a slight negative impact on soil and
groundwater quality if storage, production and waste disposal, are managed without appropriate
precautionary measures. Furthermore, accidental spills and losses, impossible to predict, can
also result in minor soil and groundwater contamination.
Therefore, a number of mitigation measures are provided, along with some management and
monitoring issues.
3.1    Description of measures to be foreseen
Mitigation measures
Measures during the construction phase:
- reduction of accidental spills through preventive measures, to be specified when
construction methods are known.
Measures during the operation phase:
SECTION B: ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT PLAN, MITIGA TION AND MONITORiNG PLAN    199



- reduction of leaks, spills and losses through preventive measures, to be specified
when operation and storage methods are known;
- siting of liquid product storage tanks on an impervious surface with a low wall to
collect possible spills;
- siting of process unit areas on impervious surfaces to control product spills or losses;
- siting of solid waste collection zones on impervious surfaces;
- avoiding placement of storage tanks, process units and solid waste collection zones in
the near surroundings of hand-dug wells;
- issuing permits with specified effluent standards for discharging to land;
- quality control of industrial effluents;
- introduction of quality standards for soil;
- monitoring groundwater quality.
Regulatory control
Water quality standards for groundwater are available. Nevertheless, there are some (toxic)
parameters, which are not covered by the present list of quality standards such as mineral oil,
monocyclic aromatic hydrocarbons, and volatile chlorinated hydrocarbons. It is recommended to
complete standards with these parameters.
Quality standards for soil are not available. Therefore, introduction of a set of standards is
recommended. The following parameters should be included: pH, conductivity, heavy metals,
niineral oil and polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons.
With regard to standards for soil and groundwater quality, distinction should be made between
background concentrations and cleanup criteria. Background concentrations can be considered as
tentative concentrations, while cleanup criteria are set to identify concentrations, which could be a
risk to the environment. If cleanup criteria are exceeded, action should be taken to remove pollution
or at least to minimise possible risks.
As for discharging into water, there are no general industrial effluent standards for discharging to
land. Processes with a potential for pollution have to obtain a permit issued by the National
Environmental Agency. Maximum allowable quantity or concentration of discharge of liquid waste
as well as monitoring requirements and environmental management measures to be undertaken are
specified in this permit. At this stage, monitoring guidelines are premature since the type of
industries is not known.
Monitoring
Monitoring of effluent quality was discussed in the management plan for surface water.
At present pH, conductivity, total hardness, free carbondioxide, Ca2+ , Mg2+ , K+, Na+ , Mn2+,
total Fe, NO3-, Cl-, F, S04 2-, C032- and HC03- is measured on groundwater extracted at water
supply boreholes. Concentrations of these parameters are an indication of general groundwater
quality. Pollution from industrial sources for example, can only be detected by analysis of more
SECTIONB: ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENTPLAN, MITIGATIONAND MONITORING PLAN             200



specific components related to the industrial activities. Therefore, a groundwater monitoring
network should be installed in the project area.
The observation wells are installed in the top of the phreatic aquifer. The number of observation
wells depends on the number of "potential point-sources" of groundwater pollution in and
around the project area. The following guidelines can be use to determine the number of
observation wells:
-  one observation well for each "potential point-source";
-  in case different "point-sources" are situated close to one another (in a radius of less
than 10 m), the number of wells can be reduced;
-  a number of wells, not related to point-sources, should be spread in the area on
locations with an indication of potential for groundwater pollution (1 observation well
for each ha);
-  some wells should be located at the upstream part of the site to monitor quality of the
incoming groundwater and some should be located at the downstream part.
Definition of potential point-sources should take into account factors such as preventive actions
taken around the source, age an size of the source.
Parameters to be analysed in groundwater samples can be divided into two groups:
- specific components related to point-sources (only to be measured in observation
wells close to the source);
- standard parameters (to be measured in all observation wells);
- pH, conductivity and temperature
*  heavy metals: Pb, Zn, Cd, Cu, Ni, As, Hg, Cr
*  mineral oil
- monocyclic aromatic hydrocarbons : benzene, toluene, ethylbenzene, xylene
* volatile chlorinated hydrocarbons;
Periodicity of groundwater sampling depends on the activity executed.  Fish processing
industries and electronics and assembly activities should be forced to carry out a groundwater
sampling campaign every 10 years. Companies storing and handling bulk quantities should
monitor groundwater quality every 5 years, activities with a minor pollution risk only when
ending. The first groundwater sampling campaign should be carried out before starting the
activities, in order to get an idea on initial groundwater quality.
3.2    Estimation of the environmental impact reduction
If construction and operation are conducted with appropriate preventive measures, risk of
significant soil pollution is very limited. In case leaks, accidental spills or losses will occur
anyway, possible soil or groundwater pollution will be determined by means of soil and
groundwater monitoring networks. If cleanup standards are exceeded, either soil and/or
SECTION B: ENVIRONMENTAL MANA GEMENT PLAN, MITIGA TION AND MONITORIBG PLAN    201



groundwater sanitation will be necessary or action will have to be taken to reduce risks. If the
proposed measures are applied, soil and groundwater will maintain an acceptable quality in times
to come.
3.3    Responsibilities and costs
As the project is only in a design stage, preventive measures to be taken in order to prevent soil
and groundwater pollution, such as impervious surfaces and walls and localisation of potential
pollution sources, will not entail excessive costs since they can be incorporated in building plans.
Preventive measures to reduce leaks, spills and losses during operation are mainly good practice
measures and will not lead to significant extra costs.
Costs of soil and groundwater monitoring is difficult to estimate as most cost-determining factors
are not known, such as:
- number of groundwater observation wells and soil sampling points;
- depth of groundwater wells and soil sampling;
- specific components to be analysed.
Cost of a standard parameter set will vary between 100 and 120 USD. Installation cost for standard
observation wells (depth 5 m below ground level) will be approximately 200 USD.
3.4    Waste Management
Seen the uncertainties concerning types and capacities of the proposed industries, it is impossible
to work out a detailed plan for management of solid waste generated at the project site.
Therefore, some general recommendations for action are given below:
-  Waste management involves prevention, treatment and disposal. Especially for
hazardous wastes, the objective of any waste management plan should be to minimise
as far as possible the quantity of wastes being generated and the amount requiring
final disposal. Prevention includes volume reduction at the source. The objective can
be further achieved by recycling and waste recovery;
- Apart from technical devices and regulations, solid waste management requires
responsible involvement of people to be successful. Therefore, a public awareness
programme is recommended;
- Collection of solid wastes should be assured during construction;
- For waste collection during the operational phase, a compaction-loading truck should
be available at the project site. Loading capacity is approximately 15 tons a truck.
SECTIONB: ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENTPLAN. MITIGATIONAND MONITORING PLAN       202



Considering the assumed maximal waste production of 3,000 tons a year, one
movement a day should be sufficient for waste collection.
Waste collection during construction and operation goes along with the respective activities and
should be implemented at the start of each phase. A public awareness programme may be
prepared in an early stage and requires sustained efforts and reviews of ideas for action.
For all sites, it is proposed to provide a number of waste collection zones on site from which
licensed disposal companies can collect and transport the waste off site. In case waste production
largely exceeds the assumed 3,000 tons a year, establishment of a dump site should be
considered.
If a dump site is planned, the following guidelines have to be considered.
First of all, a sealing layer should be provided at the bottom of the dump site. This layer consists
of an impermeable soil layer topped with an artificial layer. Sealing layers are characterised by:
- impermeable soil layer: at least 3 m in thickness and hydraulic conductivity
below 1.10-9 m/s;
- artificial layer: equivalent to an HDPE-foil of 2.5 mm in thickness.
Waste is dumped in layers with a maximal height of 2.5 metres. In order to prevent dust and odour
nuisance, a covering layer of at least 0.2 metres has to be provided on top of every layer of waste.
If dumping activities are ended, another sealing layer should be provided, build up of an
impeimeable soil layer covered by an artificial layer. Sealing layers are characterised by the
following properties:
- impermeable soil layer: at least 0,5 m in thickness and hydraulic conductivity
below 1.10-9 mis;
-  artificial layer: equivalent to an HDPE-foil of 2.5 mm in thickness.
On top of this sealing layer, a finishing layer is provided, consisting of a drainage layer of at least
0.3 metres thick and build up of materials such as and debris and sand. Finally, this finishing layer
is covered with a soil layer, suitable for plant growth.
Special attention should be taken to site selection. A wide range of physical and ecological
constraints has to be considered. Dump sites may have important effects on landscape and land
use and could cause groundwater pollution. It is recommended to use the EIA-procedure at this
aim.
SECTION B: ENVIRONMENTAL MANA GEMENTPLAN, MITIGATIONAND MONITORING PLAN        203



14-    VGETATION (FORESTS AND WILDLIFE)I
This mitigation plan focusses on the two priority sites for FEZ development, namely the Airport site
and Bund Road site.
The area across from the Bund Rd. is the eastern edge of the proposed Tanbi Nature Reserve that
extends west to Serrekunda and south as far as Mandinari Point. In the wake of the Ramsar
designation, the Department of Parks and Wildlife prepares a management plan for the reserve.
Concerning the planned FEZ at the airport, mitigation measures for the expected indirect effects
merit special attention.
Two IUCN-reports have been consulted as technical background for management principles in
tropical areas: MacKinnon et al.,1986 and Sayer et al., 1992. Concerning Tanbi Wetland , we
refer to the "recommendations for management" included in the Ramsar Wetland Study (DPWM
& The Ramsar Bureau, 1997).
4.1.   Description of the measures to be foreseen
Construction phase
(i)   Landscaping of the project sites
Although the height of the proposed industrial units and office buildings at the Bund Road site is
moderate (heighest level: 3 floors for the office buildings), the completed industrial estate will
certainly be prominent in the flat Bund Polder area. In order to soften this visual impact it is
suggested to foresee a buffer between the estate and the remaining polder.
It was is not included in the development plan for this site (EI, 2000 and Figure 3.5. in Section
A, Part II, chapter 3.). The following mitigation action could be part of the detailed engineering
at Bund road:
- buffer to be constructed along the southem edge of industrial the estate, from west to east;
- at least 15 m wide, with dense scrub vegetation;
- irregular shape - using local scrub species and low trees ( up to 10 m growing height).
At the other sites no planting will be required to create buffer zones. At the Airport site in
particular, sufficiently large strips of the existing woodland can easily be saved all around the
area that will be cleared for construction. Even if the remaining trees and scrub will not restore
the destroyed habitat, there is a good chance that woodland birds will continue to use this kind of
buffer zone around the FEZ as a stepping stone to other forest areas.
(ii)   Careful design of Bund road widening (ifrequired)
SEcTION B: ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENTPLAN, MITIGATION AND MONITORING PLAN       204



As pointed out in Section A, Part III, it is imperative to limit any possible widening of the road to
the Bund Polder side of the road.
This mitigation action should be imposed via the tenders for detailed engineering of the FEZ at
Bund Road.
Operational FEZ
(i)   Compensation for habitat loss
The Ramsar Convention on Wetlands (1971) imposes compensation measures whenever a parts of
the protected habitats in a designated Ramsar site are destroyed or reclaimed for development of
any kind. The Tanbi Wetland Reserve was proposed as a Ramsar site in 1997, following the
preparation of a Cabinet paper. Though no decision has been reached since, the proposal has been
be re-submitted to the National E,nvironment Management Committee (NEMC), which is headed
by the President.
Bund Polder is part of the proposal'5. Development of the Bund Road site thus requires clearance
of some 35 ha of the proposed Ramsar site. Assuming that the proposed perimeter of the Ramsar
site will be accepted soon by the National Environmental Council meeting, compensation for this
Bund Road site area would be necessary. From an evaluation of this issue in the wider context of
the Trade Gateway Project, it appears that compensation at Mandina Wetland would be a
valuable suggestion. Indeed, the mangrove forests south of Mandinari (extending between
Mandina Point and Mandina Ba - Tunjina) equally deserve protection and sustainable
management, in particular when the hinterland of the Airport FEZ would be developed [i.e.
intensification of agriculture and trade interface between Gambia River and airport at
Mandinari].
Following the discussion of the draft final text of this report, the Department of Parks and
Wildlife Management endorses this suggestion. Compensation at Mandarine Wetland also
fulfills one of the World Bank's operational policies regarding natural habitats (OP 4.04):
minimizing habitat loss and establishing and maintaining ecologically similar protected areas.
Note:
The fate of the total Bund Polder area is unclear at the moment. The natural habitat
within the polder is seriously degraded, so that returning it to a natural state would
require large amounts of money to clean up the area. Conservationists and city planners
disagree over what should be done with the area. It is reasonable to expect that Banjul
15 Denton Bridge site, as part of the coastal strip to the north of Banjul-Serrekunda highway, is equally situated
within the Ramsar site perimeter. However, this site is excluded from this mitigation plan because all EIA experts
agree that development of the coastal strip is inadmissible from an environmental point of view.
SECTION B: ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENTPLAN, MIT7GATIONAND MONITORINGPLAN            205



City will gradually expand into this area given the unmet demand for land in the city.
But on the other hand the polder is part of the proposed Tanbi Wetland Reserve.
Furthermore, the polder has a network of drains from the city discharging into the area.
An ungoing study project concerns this network of drains and the natuLre of the
interventions required for the Bund Polder area and flood control system16.
Although the Bund Polder does not appear to be a priority focus of conservation efforts,
the complexity of the situation permits no further considerations within the framework
of this EIA.
(ii) Protection of the edges of Tanbi Wetland Complex
The proposed reserve is under serious threat because of the expansion of rice cultivation all
along its western edge, from Serakunda to Mandinari, concurrent to deforestation for the
production offuelwood. There is a dramatic over-harvesting of mangrove oysters, particularly
by the squatters of the Bund Road polder. Nevertheless, the reserve's core area still consists of
relatively pristine mangrove that constitutes a major attraction for tourists.
Impacts from FEZ development on Tanbi are likely when the prediction of a population increase
in the project areas is accepted as justified. According to the socio-economic analysis made in
this EIA, increased human pressure on Abuko NR as well as on the Lamin and Mandinari sides
of Tanbi Wetland certainly have to be considered as indirect issues of project development at the
Airport FEZ.
The management plan prepared by the DPWM was not yet available during the EIA study period
(see § 6.2.2.3. and figure 6.2/2 in Section A, Part II). As a matter of fact it makes no sense to
develop new management actions as part of the present report. Therefore, we just want to highlight
some former recommendations (DPWM & The Ramsar Bureau, 1997) which are considered
particularly relevant in connexion with FEZ development, more specifically with the expected
increase of human pressure on the reserve:
- A clear land zoning should be an essential component of the management plan. This would take
into account the need to meet the demand of local communities in terms of natural resource
utilization together with the need to maintain the ecological integrity of Tanbi and its
surroundings .
- The concept of involving all the stakeholders, including tourism operators and the communities
living around the protected area, in the management of this wetland complex is critical. The
government is aware of this important issue (see NEA, 1997 - objectives for wildlife
management) . The Ramsar study suggests, for example, a detailed investigation into the
16 Rehabilitation of storm water drainage and flood control system for the city of Banjul. A poverty alleviation and
capacity building project (GOTG / World Bank/IDA)
SECTIONB: ENVIRONMENTALMA NAGEMENTPLAAN, MITIGATIONANDMONITORNG PLAN             206



potential for sustainable harvesting of the mangrove to partially meet the local demand for
fuelwood ("firewood").
- Another recommendation from the Ramsar study (DPWM & The Ramsar Bureau, 1997)
concerns a sensitization programme to demonstrate values and benefits of conservation efforts
at Tanbi. This awareness programme should involve all concerned institutions, local
conmmunities and developers.
(iii) Improvement of the effectiveness of the buffer zone around Abuko Nature Reserve
The Abuko buffer zone was gazetted in 1985, and demarkated in 1995 by cement pillars. This
zone should produce the desired result, that is, constitute a real protection of the sensitive core of
the reserve. The present situation shows frequent tresspassing and even permanent
encroachment. Increased human pressure linked to FEZ development at the Airport site could
seriously aggravate this tendency.
The solution would be to increase the protection given to the buffer zone, by fencing it out - a
sollution supported by the DPWM.
(iv) Preventive actions against encroachment on Salagi and Furuyar Forest Parks
The approach of community forest management, as developed in 1991 by the Forestry
Department and the Gambia-German Forestry Project (NEA, 1997), could prove useful when
development of the reservation sites at Sukuta andlor Kuloro should start. It is based on the
already mentioned concept of involving all the stakeholders in sustainable management of forest
resources.
4.2.   Regulatory actions
- Compensation measures for habitat loss to be communicated in The Gambia Gazette.
- Adaptation of the Land use Plan 2000 (made up in 1988, revised draft 1990) including land
zoning for the westem fringes of Tanbi Wetland (from Cape Point to Mandina).
An update is needed anyway in order to provide a legal basis for industrial development at all sites
except for the Airport, where a special zone is already reserved.
4.3.   Monitoring
In general , the monitoring needs of protected areas mentioned above are more are less fulfilled
by the DPWM, at least at Tanbi and Abuko. The Forestry Department is responsible for Forest
Parks such as Salagi and Furuyar. An evaluation of wildlife and forest resources monitoring
needs is outside the scope of the present study.
SECTION B: ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENTPLAN, MITIGATIONAND MONITORING PLAN       207



4.4.   Estimation of the environmental impact reduction
For some actions, it is clear that the impact reduction will be obvious and easy to assess:
- the proposed landscaping of the industrial sites will soften visual impacts as well as - albeit to
a lesser extent - habitat fragmentation;
- careful detailed engineering design of the future Bund road widening will exclude adverse
effects (of construction and use of the road) on the sensitive mangrove swamps of Tanbi;
- fences around the Abuko buffer zone.
Other actions require sustained efforts and a good repartition of responsibilities and tasks.
Especially at Abuko NR and at the western borders of Tanbi, law enforcement and regular
patrolling by DPWM rangers will be required to ensure the effectiveness of the proposed
management actions.
4.5.   Responsibilities and costs
The principal agencies involved with monitoring and management of forest and wildlife
resources are the National Environment Agency, the Department of Parks and Wildlife
Management and the Forestry Department.
The repartition of tasks is sufficiently well defined to assure a coordinated execution of the
various mitigation measures.
Seen the present status of (community) forest management as well as wetland management by
the Forestry Department and the DPWM respectively, no major institutional capacity problems
(cfr. World Bank's operational policies OP 4.04) exist. Indeed, international cooperation (among
others with the Ramsar Bureau) resulted in state-of-the art environmental planning and
management plans for forests and wetlands. Qualified experts work at the key departments but
most institutions definitely lack staff, basic equipment - and financial means in general -, to bring
their otherwise well prepared management plans into practice.
Cost estimation
- Compensation of habitat loss at Bund Rd. : administrative measure (no resettlement
compensation required);
-  Planting of buffer zone at Bund Rd. site (to be included in detailed project design): 1 - 2,000
USD;
-  Abuko - buffer zone fencing (approximately 7 km - height: 2 m): 15,000 USD;
-  Other actions: as part of broader management plans / no cost estimation provided.
