5.   COASTAL AND MARINE RESOURCES
The marine resources are still very rich. On basis of sparse data, there can be concluded that
seawater and sediments are unpolluted. Pelagic fish resources are very rich, demersal fish may
be heavily fished. There is a wide variety of marine species, such as sea turtles, manatees, marine
mammnals, etc. However, the status of these species is rather limited.
The impact of the FEZ development on the marine and coastal resources is very small. Only
indirect effects can occur to a mostly small extent. Some mitigation measures can be foreseen so
that the possible impacts remain small or become negligeable. First of all, present Integrated
Coastal Zone Management is described as the framework in which the mitigation measures can
be placed.
5.1.   Integrated Coastal Zone Management (ICZM) - the present situation
The Gambia is a party to the convention for co-operation in protection and development of the
marine and coastal environment of the West and Central African Region.
The Food and Agriculture Organisation (FAO) initiated the project 'Integrated Coastal Areas:
Training and Development of National Capabilities for Planning and Management of the West
and Central African Region" (WACAF/I 1) in co-operation with the Ocean and Coastal Areas
Programme (OCA/PAC) of UNEP, and through the Priority Actions Programme/Regional
Activity Center (PAP/RAC) of UNEP's Mediterranean Action Plan (MAP). A part of
WACAF/11 refereed to Gambia and within this scope, a Coastal Profile was developped and the
development of an Integrated Coastal Area Management (ICAM) Strategy is being prepared.
In August 1995, a technical working group on "Coastal and marine environment (C&ME)" was
established, chaired by the NEA. Following terms of reference apply to this working group:
to formulate, review and revise policies relating to all coastal, marine and fluvial activities;
to advise the NEA and the Government on matters arising on the sustainability, protection,
development and monitoring of the coastal, marine and fluvial environment
to form and guide the work of task forces on issues that may arise relating to coastal and
riverbank erosion, marine and fluvial environment, sand mining and oil spill contingency
plans
The current coastal erosion rate of 1-4 metres annually is a major environmental problem, which
has therefore required the creation of a multi-sectoral working group due to the complexity of the
problem. Due to the working group, the sand mining at Bijilo Beach was transferred to Kartong
in 1996. Up to day, the ICAM project mainly focussed on the Southern Coastal Region.
SECTIONB: ENVIRONMENTALMANAGEMENTPLAN, MITIGATIONANDMONITORINGPLAN         209



In The Gambia Environment Action Plan Phase II program proposal document (GEAP II,
February 2000), following actions are stated. "A Coastal Zone Management Team would be in
place to enhance the monitoring, co-ordination and effective management of the coastal areas.
The team would be backed by forceful legislation to enable it to address illegal activities such as
sand mining. The team would be supported by a monitoring body comprising NEA, Geology
Unit, the Police Intervention Unit and the Municipal Police."
Furtheron, GEAP II defines 2 specific programs for the coastal zone management.
Appropriate institutional framework established and provided with a strong legal basis for
the management and regulation of the coastal zone: this will essentially involve studies and
implementation of study recommendations for the development of the relevant legal and
institutional bodies for the sustainable management of the coastal zone, and;
enhance capacity of institutions to regulate the activities on the coastal zone: GEAP II
resources will be used to develop the capacity of the institution(s) developed to manage and
control the coastal zone. Specifically, this will entail the development of capacity in
enforcing the relevant codes as well as putting in place a sustainable monitoring and
management program within the coastal zone
With respect to coastal erosion, Haskoning is conducting a comprehensive study for the coastal
protection of The Gambia.
Conclusive, The Gambia is convinced of the need for planning and actions to be taken in the field of
ICZM. They are on the good way but are still lacking in personnel for a thorough monitoring of the
17
resources and inspection to prevent illegal activities
The management in the ICZM should address two focal points: erosion is the major (partly natural
and partly human induced) problem endangering many socio-economic activities in the coastal
zone. Secondly, the mangroves are the major coastal resource under threat by socio-economic
activities.
5.2.   Description of the measures to be foreseen
Given the small impacts of the FEZ development on the coastal zone, a restricted number of
mitigation measures is necessary to ensure the protection of the marine and coastal resources.
5.2.1. Dredging
1. location of dumping sites for dredged material should be chosen wisely, if dredging is
necessary f.i. for creating a channel at Mandinari or at the basic wharves upriver. Dumping
17 this is of course linked to the economic situation and will most probably remain a problem
SECTION B: ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEAfENTPLAN, MIrIGATIONAND MONITORING PL4N     210



should not take place at the inlets of bolongs as this may disturb the ecological function of
the mangrove areas
2. the location of the basic wharves upriver should be chosen wisely so as not to intersect or
encroach mangrove belts along the river - natural embayments which serve already as
fishing shelter or harbour serve normally perfect for this purpose
5.2.2. Monitoring
3. specific monitoring should be started in the Tanbi Wetland area when FEZ development
takes place at the Bund Rd. or Denton Bridge during the preparation stage, before the
commencement of the works and continued long after going into operation to look for
eventual impacts on the environment - monitoring should include surface water quality and
sediment measurements in the mangrove (see also Blasco et al., 1996).
4. Concerning sea water quality parameters, mineral oil and heavy metals should be
incorporated in the monitoring program. To reduce costs, this may occur with a smaller
frequency. The frequency and the number of stations should be increased if pollution is
detected.
5.2.3. Human resource development, education and sensibilisation
5. When looking at the inventory of the NGO's implementing environmental projects in The
Gambia, or the Environmental Management Technical Assistance Projects, many of these
projects are of an educational or capacity building type (National Environment Agency,
1997). However, none of them seem to address specifically the issues related to coastal
resources and fishermnan. Therefore, specific community based actions could be taken with
regard to the coastal resources protection - this is not a mitigation measure but a
complementary management tool for effective integrated coastal zone development
5.2.4.  Estimation of the environmental impact reduction
Mitigation measures may reduce significantly the indirect effects on the coastal zone due to
impact of supplementary dredging activities which should be needed to establish the interface at
Mandinari or the basic wharves upriver.
5.2.5.  Responsibilities and costs
The mitigation measures foreseen should be enforced or coordinated by the NEA. Mitigation
measures related to dredging are good practice measures which will not lead to extra costs. The
supplementary monitoring program will depend upon the frequency and number of stations that
will be analyzed. A good monitoring program should be able to be performed for a yearly cost of
20,000 USD..

EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
PARTI: DESCRIPTION OF THE TRADE GATEWAYPROJECT
1.   GENERAL DESCRIPTION OF THE PROJECT ELEMENTS
The Trade Gateway Project comprises the following four project elements which, according to
the TOR, had to be included in the environmental impact assessment study:
(i)       On-site infrastructure and operations of the Free Trade Zones;
(ii)      Off-site infrastructure;
(iii)     Upriver wharves;
(iv)      Port operations development.
As the detailed engineering design of the Free Zone development plans was not yet available, it was
not possible to describe all of these elements in sufficient detail .
The feasibility study prepared by IDI Consultants (February, 2000) provided - for 2 out of 5
possible FEZ sites - descriptions and site plans useful as reference for the project description.
The proposed basic infrastructure of the FTZ development zones comprises:
- roads
- buildings: factory cells and offices
- land available for companies that want to construct their own facilities.
As a matter of fact a number of basic utilities have to be present in the zones in order to serve the
FTZ operations:
- power
- telecommunications
- water supply
- sewerage and sewage treatmnent
- solid waste collection zones
The types of activities to be expected are:
* LIGHT ASSEMBLY
SECTION C - EXECUTIVE SUMMARY                                                   212



* ELECTRONICS
* TV/COMPUTER ASSEMBLY
* TRADING AND PACKAGING
* NEAR THE AIRPORT: EXPANDED AGRICULTURAL PRODUCE EXPORT
At a recent Stakeholders Seminar on the TGP (June 2000) IDI Consultants elaborated on the type
of recommended activities for the free zones. Much attention has been given to "niche
manufacturing" (AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTS, FOOD AND FISH PROCESSING, PACKAGING OF
PHARMACEUTICAL PRODUCTS,...) as well as to the services component of the FTZ.
Although these recommendations are based on the results of market surveys and though they
were the object of careful consideration by IDI and the TGP Task Force, no further details of the
expected activities are available so far. Therefore, the EIA preparers were expected to work with
several assumptions regarding the activities and their expected emissions, residues and nuisance
for then environment.
For the purpose of environmental assessment of upriver wharves the available notes and
reconmmendations by [DI did not provide specific information, nor did they indicate suitable
locations where wharves / freight jetties have to be constructed to enhance river transport.
Consequently, this project element was perforce considered in a very general way in this EL4-study,
e.g. by proposing the minimal requirements which should be observed when constructing wharves
at environmentally sensitive parts of the river.
In this context an additional project element ( added to the initial TOR after the TGP Stakeholders'
Seminar) has been studied: the concept of a trade interface site along the road at Mandinari,
reinforcing the link between the Gambia River and the proposed FEZ at the airport.

work on only 2 out of 5 initially nominated TGP sites.
Indeed, from the comparative analysis it was concluded that the two sites worthy of primary
consideration are the Bund Road and Airport sites. The remaining sites were considered to be
unsuitable in the short or medium term, due to environmental sensitivity, location, lack of
infrastructure or issues of resettlement, or a combination of these factors.
Although the TGP Task Force clearly selected both sites as the most favourable with regard to Free
Zone development in the short or medium term, the other 3 alternative sites still had to be
considered in the environmental assessment study. However, the degree of detail was different:
- The two priority sites have been studied in depth, on the basis of the available, rather detailed
development plans, design lay-out and project element descriptions;
- For the three other reservation sites (Denton Bridge, Kuloro and Sukuta) all environmental
items have been described albeit in a more general way. Consequently, the sampling and
analysis programme has been concentrated on the water and soil quality of the development
zones at the Airport and Bund Road sites only.
From the conclusions of the IDI-study it also appeared that free zone development should start at
the Airport site. Reclamation of Bund Road site would be a later phase, if the former proves
successful.

13.   DEVELOPMENT PLANS
Development plan for the AIRPORT SITE
- The proposed site is located within the "EPZ Zone" designated by the GCAA (The Gambian
Civil Aviation Authority) on its lands at Banjul International Airport.
- In the initial phase, a zone of approximately 8.8 hectares will be located on the existing
airport approach road and will be entered via a new roundabout on the approach road. The
zone is positioned so as to permit its future expansion to the west: the completed phase will
cover an area of approximately 162 hectares.
Development plan for the BUND RoAD SITE
- The proposed site of approximately 34 hectares is located at the northern end of the larger
area bounded by the Bund Road, in order to minimise disturbance to the more
environmentally sensitive southern part of the area.
- As with the airport zone, this site is planned on a regular grid of distributor, access and
service roads and rectangular sites, capable of accomodating a variety of building types,
from small standard advance factories and warehouses to large detached custom-designed
manufacturing plants and office buildings. A service zone for power plants, waste treatment
plants, gas and other utilities is located at the outer perimeter of the zone .
- It is important to note that even in its completed phase the Bund Road free zone will be
restricted to ± 34 hectares located along the Banjul - Serrekunda highway. The IDI-study
did not consider any further development in the environmentally most sensitive area of Bund
Polder, south of the FEZ limits.
No detailed design nor site maps were provided by the IDI-study, for the sites serving as
reservation zones in the current TGP-context.
As a consequence, the following assumptions were made by the Consultant for environmental
assessment of the TGP at those locations:
- for the Denton Bridge site the same site plan, area (34 hectares) and basic design of the
Bund Rd. site was taken as a basis for impact assessment;
- for the Kuloro and the Sukuta sites on the other hand , where the available space is more
important, the design of the development plan for the Airport (final phase) served as the
reference (160-200 hectares).
SECTION C: EXECUTIVE SUMMARY                                               215



PART:II DESCRIPTION OF THE BASELINE SITUATION
1.   GENERAL CHARACTERISTICS
The study area is situated in the valley of The Gambia River. It is essentially flat. Both Bund
Road and Denton Bridge sites are situated at or just above sea level. The three other sites
(Airport, Sukuta and Kuloro) are located in a flat and well-drained area, suitable for building.
2.   CLIMATE AND AIR QUALITY
The study area is characterized by a long dry season from October to early June and a short rainy
season from mid-June to early October. Temperature variations are rather modest. High
evaporation results in a water shortage during the dry season. Due to the poor development of
power generation, a lack of important industrial activities and the fairly low road transport
emissions of air pollutants are quite low. For most of the general air pollutants i.e. NO, and SO2
the pollution degree is low and a substantial carrying capacity still exists. The PM10
concentration is elevated in crowded areas with dense traffic, most probably also resulting from
natural dust formation. As far as air quality is concerned no major differences are expected to
exist between the different sites to be studied.
13.   WATER AN9D AQUATIC RESOURCESI
Ground water
In The Gambia, two aquifer systems can identified, respectively a shallow sand aquifer and a
deep sandstone aquifer. The shallow aquifer system occurs throughout the whole of the Kombo
district and is up to 80m thick. The system is divided into upper and lower aquifers, the former
being phreatic and the latter being semi-confined. Recharge of the shallow aquifer system has
decreased over the last 15 to 20 years, precluding its further development.
Ground water is exploited from the shallow sand aquifer by means of hand dry wells and
boreholes, the former being suitable for small villages and communities, the latter for large
municipal supplies. Water is mainly exploited by the National Water and Electricity Company
SECTION C: EXECUTivE SUMMARY                                                  216



(NAWEC); it is treated before use. As far as water provision at the different alternative sites is
concerned, the following observation can be made:
- Bund Road and Denton Bridge: water originates from the Salagi and Jambur well fields;
upon treatment, it is stored in large tanks in the Banjul area;
-  Airport: water supply is provided by the Salagi and Jambur well fields; treatment and storage
is available.
-  Sukuta: water can be tapped at the SS2 and SS3 boreholes from the Salagi well field.
-  Kuloro: actually, no water supply facilities do exist.
From the samples analysed (Airport and Sukuta), it appears that ground water quality is
generally good.
The Sukuta site, being situated in a well field is most vulnerable to ground water pollution.
Surface water
Apart from the River Gambia and its major side rivers, all other surface waters are short-lived
and flow only during the wet season. Only the coastal sites are situated nearby surface water of
permanent nature. The quality of the drainage water inflow into the Bund Road site mangrove is
rather poor with high levels of COD and ammonia. There will be no use of surface water in any
of the proposed sites.
Coastal dynamics and characteristics
The water quality in the coastal area has not been monitored in a systematic way. However, as
far as information is available, no signs of serious pollution have been found in the coastal
waters up till now. Sediments generally do not appear to be contaminated.
Tidel currents along the coast are generally weak but some littoral drift is being experienced
between Cape Point and Banjul. The major issue of concern, however is the coastal erosion
problem caused as a result of the natural vulnerability of the country to sea level rise and to
unplanned developments in the coastal area. The coastal erosion actually destroys tourist
facilities, cultural and historic sites and buildings. It is the most devastating environmental
hazard.
SECTION C: EXECUTIVE SUMMARY                                                     217



4.   SOIL AND SEDIMENTS
The soil at the coastal sites (Bund Road and Denton Bridge) is predominantly alluvium
consisting of sands, silts and clays to a considerable depth. The soil is strongly influenced by the
occurence of mangrove. Stability and carrying capacity are rather limited and settlement may
occur when important constructions are build. Some soil pollution already exists at the Bund
Road site.
The inland sites are characterized by soils of sandy texture which may include bands of coarser
material. Actually, soils are not polluted
5.     NOISE
Expected noise levels at the different sites have been estimated. It appears that non disturbed
noise levels are quite low, LA95 values not exceeding 40 dB(A). Traffic and in particular aircraft
operations result, however, in locally increased noise levels. At the airport site, an LAeq value of
55 dB(A) is reached. At all other sites, a noise level of approximately 45 dB(A) prevails.
6.  FAUNA AND FLORA
After a concise description of the protected areas outside of the TGP project sites, the habitats
and wildlife of the five nominated project sites were described in detail.
6.1.   Coastal sites
Both sites are situated within the perimeter of the proposed Ramsar site Tanbi Wettand
Complex.
Tanbi occupies an area of approximately 6,000 hectares, the majority of which is composed of
mangrove forest. The wetland complex is located on the southern shore of the River Gambia and
includes the area between the island of Banjul and Cape Point in the north and extends to Lamin
and Mandinari Point in the south.
The wetland complex is extremely important for wildlife, in particular for the avifauna.
Denton Bridge has a vital role in the ecology of the wetland complex because of its commnunication
function. Denton Bridge constitutes the entry point of fish and invertebrates that breed in the
SECTION C: EXECUTIVE SUMMARY                                                 218



mangrove but live adult lives in the sea. It is particularly important for migrant waterfowls and for
African waterbirds.
Bund Road site
Bund Polder (the area between Bund Road and Banjul city), shows a rather limited variety of
vegetation types. In fact, the natural habitat within this polder is seriously degraded and polluted; it
includes several garbage tips and some parts are used for agriculture.
At the Bund Road site proper the only remnants of natural vegetation consist of mangrove
fringes along a few channels, and dispersed patches of grassland and scrubland.
Denton Bridge
The coastal strip , north of the Banjul-Serrekunda highway, is a diverse mosaic of dune
vegetation, lagoons, spots of dune woodland and grassed woodland, salt and freshwater marshes,
mangrove forest.
On the drier parts of this sandy spit, a savanna woodland has established on the more elevated
grounds. These grounds represent the only places that can reasonably be taken into consideration
for development.
6.2.   Inland sites
Airport site
The land at the Airport site is basically flat, level and covered with a vegetation typical of a
secondary succession consisting mainly of shrubs, trees and grass. The woodland is attractive for a
good number of bird species.
Due to the absence of grazing, bush fires and the amount of regeneration on the site, there is great
potential for the woodland vegetation and ecology in general to be restored to a more primary
ecosystem.
Kuloro an Sukuta sites
Both site are located near protected Forest Parks. They are mainly designated to agricultural
production, both sites being farmed for several decades with short fallow periods.
The vegetation is best described as Open Tree-Shrub Savanna type. The ecology of the area
until probably the last 20 to 30 years was a Closed Woodland Forest of the Guinea-Savanna type.
SECTION C: EXECUTIVE SUMmMARY                                                  219



6.3.   Marine fauna and flora
The coastal zone of The Gambia is the scene of large areas of ecologically sensitive areas. These
include the area of mangrove swamps between Toll Point to Cape Creek as well as the Oyster
Creek and Stink Corner mangrove swamps, which form in large part the Tanbi wetland reserve,
which is proposed as a RAMSAR site. Further south, the Tanji Bird Reserve is a protected area.
Furthernore, Brufut wood, Solifor point, Tujering lagoons, the River Kakima delta en Kachuma
Forest, the Dau Dula to Kartong coastal forest and the Allaheim river mouth are the most
sensitive areas of the south coast of The Gambia.
Some specific features of the marine ecosystem in the study area are:
- mangroves in the Tanbi Wetland Reserve;
- seagrass fields in the shallow waters before Tanbi Wetland;
- some rare endangered species i.e. the manatee, the hump-backed dophin and the green
turtle.
The Gambian coastal zone is situated in one of the richest fishing zones of the world. There has
been a considerable increase of pelagic fish resources the last years, while demersal fish stocks
are enduring the highest pressure.
While industrial fishery operations by licenced fishing vessels is reduced, remains poaching a
widely distributed practice. The artisanal fisheries show a gradual upward trend of fish catches,
due to improved fish catching methods and new entrants in the sector.
Apart from fishery operations, oyster harvesting is a common practice inducing pressure on the
mangrove ecosystem.
7.   LAND USE AND TOURISM
The official planning tool for the GBA is the Greater Banjul Physical Development Plan and its
map component, the "Land Use Plan 2000" (revised draft 1990).
7.1.   Coastal sites
Both sites are part of the proposed Ramsar site "Tanbi Wetland Complex". The proximity to the
major urban centers of Banjul, Serekunda and Bakau results in considerable pressures on the area.
SECTION C: EXECUTIVE SUMMARY                                                   220



The area comprising the two proposed sites is subject to agricultural and horticultural activity on the
landward side. Along the Banjul-Serekunda highway industrial development is rather important.
Furthermore, it is reasonable to expect that Banjul City will gradually expand into the polder
between Bund Road and the city, given the unmet demand for land in Banjul City.
The central part of the project site at Bund Road is a rather open area with grassfield, scrub and
mangrove fringes. The eastern part , beyond the mangrove remnants and nearest to the city
limits, consists of open land (barren flat with standing water). Near Bund Rd. a NAWEC
transformation and distribution station forms the corner with the highway. An illegal waste tip
extends more than 200 m along Bund Rd. The FEZ site is only a small part of the Bund Polder
which is important as storm water drainage and flood control area for the capital.
According to the Land use Plan 2000, a rather narrow strip along the Banjul highway is zoned
for a combination of services, industrial development, hotels and institutions. It is hard to detect
a suitable site for FEZ development at Denton Bridge. Between a new ELF petrol station and
Denton Bridge, a dry, sandy strip could be taken into consideration but this area has strong
physical limitations for construction (34 hectares needed, only 5 hectares available).
All the nominated sites for FEZ development lie outside the Tourist Development Area (Land use
Plan 2000). On the other hand, Tanbi Wetland is famous with bird watchers and Denton Bridge
/Oyster Creek serves as a starting point for visiting the bolongs by boat, so that this location may
rightly be cited as a key site for eco-tourism infrastructure .
7.2.   Inland sites
The Airport site is a uniform piece of woodland, without any buildings. It is part of the Special
Area S.A. 2 (Yundum Airport Area) ) Land Use Plan 2000.
The two other sites (Sukuta, Kuloro) show a mix of open agricultural land, farms, wooded
savanna and open scrub savanna, and more or less developed settlements with houses and
gardens for horticulture. There are no existing tourist spots in these areas.
8.   ARCHEOLOGICAL, HISTORICAL AND CULTURAL VALUES
Except for a few scattered shell mounds nears the coastal sites, there are no sites of archaeological,
historical and cultural importance in the area of the project sites.
SECTION C. EXECUTIVE SUMMARY                                               22)



9.  HUMAN AND SOCIO-ECONOMIC ENVIRONMENT
9.1.   Coastal sites
The nature of the Bund Polder area, with mangroves swamps coupled up with the poor soil that
is usually inundated, has made human settlement impossible. Instead a handful of people from
Banjul have over the years cultivated the existing patches of dry land during the rainy season and
thus derive some economic gains from the harvests. They have been joined by a a few Jola
squatters who burn and sell oysters collected from the nearby mangroves. This activity is carried
out only during the dry season.
The nature of the coastal area at Denton Bridge has rendered it equally unsuitable for any form
of human settlement. Here also people do engage in farming, cultivating millet, groundnuts and
rice during the rainy season. . During the dry season, squatters bum and sell oysters collected
from the mangroves located on the site and the opposite side where the thick mangrove provide a
breading place for oysters. Firewood is also collected from the area.
Bund Polder and the Bund Road site are state land.
At Denton Bridge, the coastal stretch of approximately 60 hectares is owned by the state though
the area along the south-west of Oyster Creek is controlled by The Gambia Ports Authority who
claim that much occupancy by most of the people and businesses are illegal. However the areas
occupied by a fish processing plant and about 20 hectares of the area occupied by the former
Scan Gambia Company have been leased.
9.2.   Inland sites
Airport site
Although the site has savanna-like features ( flat land covered with grass, bushes, shrubs and tall
trees), there are no settlements. This is probably because the area is owned by GCAA.
However, people from nearby villages carry out cultivation of groundnuts and millet during the
rainy season. Women from nearby Yundum also collect firewood from the site and beyond.
The existing airport has provided employment for people from the satellite villages of Yundum,
Lamin, Mandinari and Makumbaya.
Presently the roads leading to the airport are in fairly good condition. The road between Banjul
and West Field Junction is a dual carriageway. It converges into a single lane after this junction.
Recently this road was resurfaced and patched. There are plans to replace this single carriageway
SECTiON C: EXECUTrVE SUMMARY                                                 222



with a dual one to the airport and beyond to Mandinaba. There is also the near completed Coastal
Highway from Kololi via Sukuta to Yundum where the airport is located.
On the whole many people welcome the idea of developing this part of the airport as a FEZ as
this would create employment not only for the people from the surrounding villages but the
country as a whole.
Kuloro
Kuloro site ( approximately 10 square kilometres ), is located in Kombo East, east of the town of
Brikama. The land is flat levelled and well drained, ilndeed a typical semi arid savanna type
feature suitable for farming and grazing.
There are many villages that would be affected by the Kuloro site. The most popular are,
Tubakuta and Mandinaba. Farming is the main occupation of the inhabitants of these villages
and is done mainly during the rainy season. Women also take active part in farming, producing
mainly rice and millet. Recently a handful of women have also engaged in growing sesame.
The majority of the people engage in horticulture and animal rearing claiming that the income
from groundnut cultivation is insufficient.
The land that was communal comprises of farmland and settlements, which were owned by the
original settlers, who have passed down their rights of ownership to their descendants.
The inhabitants of these villages would welcome development on the site. Indeed, they claim that
they could allocate or sell land which could be used to set up enterprises and industries that would
create employment opportunities and improve their lifestyle. However, some of the villagers
expressed concern that some developments might not be beneficial to the people in the location.
Sukuta
The land is flat and well drained, with shrub savanna in areas that are not cleared for farning or
horticultural gardens. The most successful of these farms is the 85 hectares Radville farrn that
employs about 500 people from the villages of Nema Kunku, Sukuta, Old Yundum, Serrekunda
and other nearby villages and towns.
The nearness of the Airport makes this site well located for a FEZ. Moreover the newly
constructed Coastal Road that traverses the site will help to link the hotels and residences from
places like Kololi, Kuto and Fajara. There have been many cases of compensation for farmland
taken to make the new road possible.
The first residents on the project site settled here only about twelve to fifteen years ago with
more recent owners coming in four to two years later. Many of the owners of these plots of land
are still resident in places like Banjul, Greater Banjul, Western Division and beyond. They often
SECTION C: EXECUTIVE SUMMARY                                               223



build small corrugated houses for their watchmen or caretakers. Today a few owners have
constructed bigger stone houses on their farms.
The site incorporates about 80%/, of the farmland and residential property of people from Sukuta,
Nema Kunku and Old Yundum. Indeed some of the property though bought from individuals had
been used as farmland for many generations. Thus the land on the project site belongs to various
individuals who acquired it by purchasing it from the owners or the alkalos.
While some people on the FE Z site and neighbouring villages welcome development as a
possible means of providing better job opportunities and improved life style, many fear that new
projects would deprive them of their farmland and property, cause pollution and overpopulate
the area with new migrants.
PARTIII: DESCRIPTION AND ASSESSMENT OF THE
EXPECTED IMPACTS
11.    CLIMATE AND AIR QUALITYl
As far as air pollution is concerned, it is expected that in the vicinity of the project air pollution
will slightly increase. Some dust may be emitted during construction while operation will lead to
an input of in particular SO2, NOx, TSP and CO. The effect will, however, be low and can be
mitigated to a large extent. When comparing the location alternatives proposed, there does not
appear to be a significant difference with regard to air pollution impact, background levels being
equally low and meteorological conditions being similar.
2.  WATER AND AQUATIC RESOURCES
During the construction phase, it is not expected that impacts of significance will occur with
respect to ground water or surface water.
Potential lowering of the ground water table will be temporary and the radius of influence will be
rather limited in all sites. Because of the local character of potential ground water pollution
during construction and the absence of hand-dry wells in the immediate surroundings of any of
the sites, there is no risk of contamination of ground water supply.
During operation existing water supply can be used for all sites, exception made for the Kuloro
site, where a stand-alone borehole should be foreseen. Provided preventive measures are taken,
the risk for ground water pollution will be limited. However, proper treatment of waste water
SECTION C: EXECUTIVE SUMMARY                                                224



will have to be put in operation when waste water discharges become significant. Infiltration of
waste water and resulting ground water pollution may be considered to be more significant at the
Sukuta site in comparison to the other site, since this site is situated in a well field.
Surface water pollution during operation can only occur at the coastal sites of Bund Road and
Denton Bridge. Even when treated waste water effluents will affect significantly the water
quality of the receiving waters of the mangrove ecosystem. At Denton Bridge, further spreading
and pollution of sea water is likely to occur, however, dilution will limit significance of this
effect.
3.   SOIL RESOURCES AND WASTE
The assessment made for ground water also applies to soil. Minor effects are expected during
operation. Attention should be paid, however, to the stability issue at the coastal sites.
Since all sites are rather flat, no significant erosion risks are expected to occur.
In particular during operation, a considerable amount of waste may be generated. It will have to
be stored and collected in a proper way. When a dumping site is constructed, precautionary
measures should be taken to prevent effects on soil and ground water.
When properly managed, waste should not be a major sources of concern in any of the sites.
4.     NOISE
During construction some intermittent noise output may be expected, without creating a burden
of significance. During operation noise will be produced from industrial activities and from
increased traffic. Based upon our calculations it is not expected that noise contribution from
industrial activities will be significant beyond 200 m from the FTZ border. Traffic will increase
noise levels with 0.5 up to 3 dB(A). Although the expected noise output during the phase of
operation is limited, due to the actual elevated levels at the airport the guideline value of 55
dB(A) may be surpassed. This will not be the case at the other sites.
SECTIONC: EXECUTIVE SUMMARY                                                   225



15X   FAUNA EN FLORA 
Industrial development of the coastal area near Denton Bridge would result in inadmissible, very
important negative effects on the coastal ecosystem.
The impact will be less important at Bund Road, where an already degraded part of Bund Polder,
adjacent to the highway, will be cleared for site preparation. In comparison with the three purely
terrestrial sites, the possible impact of accidental pollution on the vegetation is more important at
this site.
At Yundum airport an island of regenerating woodland will be destroyed for FEZ development.
Indirect effects on Abuko Nature Reserve could arise when the industrial site would attract
settlers in the Lamin area.
At Kuloro and Sukuta the actual ecological value of the shrub savanna is lower. Anyhow,
clearance of this vegetation would reinforce habitat fragmentation. Moreover, both sites still
have a good potential for reforestation.
Most effects on (avi)fauna are linked to the construction phase of the project. The most negative
impact would probably be observed at Denton Bridge, fewer and less negative impacts are
predicted at the other sites.
For the Bund Road site, the Airport area, the Kuloro and Sukuta sites, the birds (and a few of the
characteristics mammals) associated to this habitat will probably locate habitat in the
surroundings that meet their ecological requirements. For the airport area, the location will
probably be more difficult through the absence of the same type of habitat in the close
surroundings.
Denton Bridge on the fringe of the proposed Ramsar site Tanbi Wetland Complex has a vital role
in the ecology of the wetlands because of its communication function. Denton Bridge / Oyster
Creek constitutes the entry point of fish and invertebrates that breed in the mangrove but live
adult lives in the sea. Furthermore, this coastal strip is particularly important for migrant
waterfowls and for African waterbirds. The destruction of this important coastal habitat would
probably be difficult to compensate by another one on The Gambia territory. Consequently, the
selection of this site would better be avoided .
6.  LAND USE AND TOURISM
Both Bund Road and Denton Bridge sites are unsuitable for human settlement Development would
not require any compensation of illegal settlement or present activities. At Kuloro and Sukata on the
SECTIONC: EXECUTIVESUMMARY                                                    226



other hand, the loss of farmlancl and residences would involve huge financial compensation of the
residents. Finally, no settlements exist at the airport site which is owned by the Gambia Civil
Aviation Authority.
Industrial development at Denton Bridge could create adverse effects on a growing eco-tourism
industry as this site is the main gate and point of departure of tourist excursions into the Tanbi
reserve.
7.  MARINE AND COASTAL RESOURCES
Generally, there is no significant impact to be expected on the seawater quality or the marine
resources. There is a moderate negative effect on the marine resources at the Denton Bridge site.
Mangrove and wetland will have another landuse by which this area loses its ecological function
as spawning and feeding area. The effect of this on the global Tanbi Wetland ecosystem is only
relative in the sense that a percentage surface is lost for these functions. Other effects are
expected to have no significance or relevance or only a slight negative one. The seawater quality
will not be changed due to the FEZ development, with the exception of the Denton Bridge site,
where some emissions from f.i. fish processing factories may end up through tidal action in the
coastal zone, the Oyster River and its tributaries. Other effects are similar for each site, as they
are produced indirectly through the development of the interface at Mandinari or the basic
wharves upriver. These structures may create erosion and sedimentation effects in a local range
around the developments.
The impacts of coastal erosion on the FEZ development are expected to be more serious when
FEZ location would be at Denton Bridge or Bund Road then adversely the impacts of the FEZ
development on the coastal resources. However, some impacts may occur which are mainly
related to the loss of spawning and feeding area, emission of fish processing facilities, the
construction of an interface at Mandinari and the construction of basic wharves upriver. These
impacts are considered (with the exception for Denton Bridge) to be small or negligeable when
the necessary precautionary measures are taken. Conclusive, Denton Bridge is not advised from
the viewpoint of marine and coastal resources. Other sites will create only an indirect slight
negative effect.
8.   MAN AND LIVING ENVIRONMENT
The project most probably would create various jobs and improve economic conditions for a lot
of people. The beneficial impacts seem most obvious at the airport site: the socio-economic
study revealed a variety of expected improvements with regard to employment, housing,
agricultural activities,... Furthermore, there are good reasons to accept that FEZ development
SECTION C: EXECUTIVE SUMMARY                                                227



would further attract people to the surrounding villages, especially to Lamin, Banjulding and
Yundum. This in turn could create an increasing pressure on Abuko Nature Reserve .
As far as human health is concerned, both some rather positive and slightly negative effects may
be expected. These will be approximately the same for all sites. The increasing economic activity
may be accompagnied by an increase in income as such indirectly leading to better health
conditions. On the other hand, the project will lead to some air emissions and an increase in
noise levels nearby the sites. Although these impacts are expected to be low, some burden is not
to be excluded totally.
Impact assessment table
Upon identification of the potential impacts, an assessment has been carried out in order to
determine the magnitude, extent and significance of the impacts, taking into account special
sensitivities of the regions concemed. It should be recognized that the present impact study is
general in nature and that a number of gaps in knowledge preclude a detailed analysis of a
number of impacts or lead to uncertainties in assessment. The major drawback is the lack of a
sufficient degree of detail on the project description.
Within this view and for reasons of clearness of the comparisons the impacts have been
classified according to the following schedule:
o    :       no effect of significance or no relevance
+    :       slight positive effect: this is an improvement of the existing situation for a specific
impact with limited magnitude, extent and significance
++   :       moderate positive effect: this is a significant improvement of the existing situation
for a specific impact leading to surpassing the criteria used and characterised by a
clear magnitute or extent
slight negative effect: this is a deterioration of the situation for a specific impact
without surpassing the criteria set; the impact can generally be rnitigated and is
reversible or limited in extend and magnitude
moderate negative effect: this is a deterioration of the situation for a specific
impact giving raise to surpassing the criteria used: it is characterised by a clear
magnitude or extent; however mitigation may lower the effect
high significant negative effect: this is a significant deterioration of the situation
for a specific impact characterised by a large magnitude and extent
SECTiON C: ExECUTIVE SUMMARY                                                      228



very important negative effect: this is a significant deterioration of the situation
for a specific impact characterized by a large magnitude and extent and
irreversible in nature without mitigation possibilities.
The assessment has been carried out in a quantitative way as far as possible and the assessment
criteria include Gambian standards, international guidelines, environmental quality objectives,
loss of surface, modifications etc. The criteria used have been outlined in each chapter of the
environmental impact study.

9.  CONCLUSION
Prior to this EIA, the Trade Gateway Project Task Force clearly selected two sites as the most
favourable with regard to Free Zone development in the short or medium term.
Indeed, from an initial comparative analysis made as part of a feasibility study it was concluded
that the two sites worthy of primary consideration are the Bund Road and Airport sites. The
remaining sites were considered to be unsuitable in the short or medium term, due to
SEcyoN C: ExEcuTivE SUMMARY                                                           229



environmental sensitivity, location, lack of infrastructure or issues of resettlement, or a
combination of these factors.
Nonetheless, these reservation sites (Denton Bridge, Kuloro and Sukuta) have been considered
in the environmental assessment of the TGP. All environmental items have been described albeit
in a more general way.
When the comparison of all alternatives is made again after a full environmental impact assessment
of the proposed project elements, the following conclusions can be drawn:
(1) Apart from moderate effects on habitat and wildlife, solid waste production and noise, the
Airport site appears to be a very suitable place for FEZ development. The assessment
shows minor effects in the other environmental compartments, while a significant
improvement of the social and economic conditions of local communities may be expected.
The fenced land is owned by the Gambia Civil Aviation Authority, so that no compensations
of private landowners is required prior to project development.
Mitigation measures concerning solid waste handling and noise have to be considered
carefully. The loss of a rather undisturbed woodland habitat is irreversible. However,
sufficiently large strips of the existing woodland can easily be saved all around the
construction area. It may be expected that woodland birds will use this buffer zone as
stepping stone to other forest areas.
(2) The Bund Road site lies within the boundaries of a proposed RAMSAR site but its current
environmental quality is clearly degraded. Clearance of the remaining vegetation would not
have a major influence on the ecological value of Tanbi Wetland, though the opportunity to
use this strip of land as a natural buffer zone between the Banjul-Serrekunda highway and
the core of the future reserve is lost when development will take place (= moderately
negative effect of changed land use). This effect can be mitigated by foreseeing a scrub
buffer zone between the industrial estate and the rest of the polder. A slightly negative effect
on eco-tourism will probably be limited to the construction phase. Furthermore, waste water
effluents of an operational FEZ could affect the water quality of the Bund Polder channels.
Even if the present water quality is bad (mainly through discharges from Banjul city), the
possible effects of surface water pollution deserves special attention.
In conclusion, this alternative is suitable for FEZ development at short distance from the city
and port of Banjul. There will be no need for compensation or resettlement of private
owners and the project would not involve the conversion of a critical natural habitat.
(3) With regard to industrial development at Denton Bridge, the conclusion of the
environmental assessment is the most negative of all. Inadmissible effects on the coastal
ecosystem are predicted . Moreover, an industrial estate nearby the main starting point for
tourist excursions into Tanbi Wetland could have a permanent negative effect on eco-
tourism.
SECTION C: EXECUTIVE SUMMARY                                                  230



Sumnmarizing, it may be stated that the expected conversion and degradation of this northern
part of Tanbi Wetland Complex excludes this alternative as a possible FEZ-site if
environmental considerations are taken into account..
(4) Both Kuloro and Sukuta sites are flat and well drained areas. The available surface is
largely sufficient for development and - apart from groundwater pollution risk in the Sukuta
well field area - only slightly negative environmental effects are expected. Consequently,
these sites may rightly be considered as suitable reservation sites for future development. On
the other hand, the potential for reforestation is equally high. A clear disadvantage of the
establishment of a FEZ area would be the dispossession of farmland and huge financial
compensation of many private owners of residences, especially at Sukuta. The low
feasibility of project development in these two areas is thus primarily based on socio-
economic considerations, including the absence of basic infrastructure (e.g. water supply) at
Kuloro).
SECTION C: EXECUTIVE SUMMARY                                                   231



ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT. MITIGATIONAND MONITORING
PLAN
For the following environmental sections the proposed mitigation measures have been worked
out in a concrete mitigation plan:
1. AIR QUALITY AND NOISE
2. WATER RESOURCES
3. SOIL AND WASTE
4. VEGETATION (FORESTS) AND WILDLIFE
5. COASTAL AND MARINE RESOURCES
For the socio-economic component of this study no particular mitigation plan is needed because
the outcome of the project would be predominantly positive. Given the fact that financial
compensation of resettlement at the reservation sites Sukuta and Kuloro excludes immediate
development in those areas, this issue has not been discussed in the present report. At the 2 most
suitable sites - Airport and Bund Road - no compensation of illegal squatters would be required.
In the 5 mitigation plans the same content has been considered:
-  a brief summary of the major environmental effects
- description of the mitigation measures to be foreseen
- regulatory control
- monitoring
- an estimation of the environmental impact reduction
-  responsibilities and estimated costs.
In the coastal and marine resources mitigation plan particular attention has been paid to
"Integrated coastal zone management - ICZM" and human resource development.

The following abbreviations have been used as an indication of status and degree of abundance:
Status
RB       Resident breeder
MB       Migrant breeder
FB       Former breeder
PV       Palaearctic passage visitor
WV       Palaearctic winter visitor
DSV       Dry season visitor (intra-African migrant)
WSV       Wet season visitor (intra-African migrant)
AfM       Intra-African migrant (season unspecified)
AV       Accidental or casual visitor of up to 10 records
(  )  Indicate that the status is variable or irregular
?       The status is uncertain or unknown but does not question occurrence, e.g.
(PV) = irregular passage visitor
? RB = status uncertain, possibly resident breeder
RB ? = probably resident but breeding uncertain
*       Breeding is almost certain but has never been proved
Degree of abundance
Six termns have been used to try to give a general indication of the degree of abundance of each species,
defined as follows:
Abundant            - invariably encountered, when present in their normal habitat, in
large numbers as residents or visitors
Common              - invariably encountered singly (e.g. birds of prey) or in quite
good numbers in their normal habitat
Not uncommon        - usually, but not invariably, encountered in their nornal habitat
Uncommon            - fairly frequently, but not regularly, encountered in their norrnal
habitat
Scarce              - only irregularly and infrequently encountered in their normal
habitat
Rare                - rarely encountered



The distribution in The Gambia has been divided into 4 sections, which have no administrative or
geographical basis:
- The Coast:   covers offshore waters, the mouth of the River Gambia (including the
Gambia) and the beaches,
- Lower River: covers from Banjul inland to Tendaba,
- Middle River: from Tendaba to Georgetown
- Upper River: from Georgetown to the eastern border of the country.



APPENDIX 4.4: Socio-economic field notes concerning the Sukuta site [Part II, chapter
10.2.5.1
rough sketch of the Proposed Project Area:
r ----------------------------------------------------------------------_________________________
SUKUTA
to Kololi
Madina Sey Kunda
.-''  Kombo Coastal Road
Jarbang            ~~Old Yundum
Jarbangz
to Airmort
This appendix is supplemental to the main text of section 10.2.5. (Section A, Part II - description
of the human and socio-economic environment in the area of Sukuta site), and it is based on
interviews with the Alkalos (i.e. village heads). It is still imprecise, as they themselves do not
know the number of compounds/plots in their respective villages - land is allocated by both the
alkalo or the kabilo (household) head, although the alkalo's consent must be obtained for any
transaction.
Madina Sey Kunda
Madina Sey Kunda is a relatively new village, and its land resources are limited. Demand for
land has grown sharply in recent years. The rapid population growth of Serrekunda has reached
its outer limits due to the extensive areas of surrounding swamps. High rents have resulted in
many people coming from Bakau, Serrekunda and Banjul to Madina Sey Kunda seeking land
upon which to live.



The land allocation system here is 60 m x 50 m and all of the land within the Proposed Project
Area has reportedly been allocated to individuals. The total number of compounds in the Area is
estimated at 450, with an unknown number of leaseholds.
The inhabitants of Madina Sey Kunda are mainly petty traders , civil servants, and marabouts.
There are no full-time farmers, although some women engage in horticulture. Women are
engaged in petty trading. Incomes are low, and they vary between D200/month - D300/month.
Nearby Radville Farm (located in the centre of the Proposed Project Area) provides source of
employment, but wages are disappointingly small. Tilling one's own land provides superior
income, particularly for vegetables. Giving land to commercial operations would nonetheless be
preferred if they were viable and provided employment for the community.
Sukuta
The village has the largest land reserves in the Greater Banjul Area. With immigration, there has
been an increased demand for land. Up to the 1980s, the allocation was by kola-nuts. After the
1980s, the drought years the Alkalo started selling land ranging from D50 - D500 for a piece of
compound. However, this amount has changed over time to between D10,000 and D150,000.
The attractive prices motivated many people to sell most of their agricultural land. Nowadays,
land is very scarce in the area. Three-quarters of the Proposed Project Area has been demarcated
and sold for residential purposes. Most of them remain empty plots, however, and only a small
number are fenced. A small number have been leased. There are disputes, even among family
members, over land sales. The total number of plots in the Proposed Project Area is not known
(the total number of compounds in the entire Sukuta Village is estimated at over 3,000).
Jarbang
The number of compounds in the Proposed Project Area is estimated at 75. They have all been
demarcated as plots of compounds up to the boundary of Sukuta and Old Yundum. Plot owners
do farm on the undeveloped plots. The plots are not leased.
Old Yundum
Total number of compounds in the Project Area is estimated at 425, with only three leaseholds,
including the Thomas Farm (situated at the other side of Kombo Coastal Road, opposite Radville
Farm), the National Agricultural Research Institute swamp fields. 60% of the houses are of mud
walls, and 40% cement blocks. This area used to be farrnland, but now it is designated for
residential purposes or used for horticulture. The inhabitants are mainly businessmen, followed
by some farmners and then by a small number of civil servants. Farming is still carried out in the
undeveloped compounds.
